BEFORE THE MIRROR
By “JEANNETTE.”
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and
all of us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying- influence, is casting its shadow
over the lives of many of us women and while perhaps a few—the more
stoical among us—can avoid worrying over past and prospective events, We
may all, with a little care and forethought, succeed in preventing the results
of worry from being noticeable in our appearance. To these fortunate
few this little chat will, however, be as interesting and instructive as to
those who stand in real need of help in toilet matters, and may be the
means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown method of retain-
ing or regaining that beauty of'face and figure which is the birthright of
every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present, not
generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Some Mints about tbe Hair.—An Excellent
Shampoo. —Few women realise how all-impor-
tant a really good shampoo is if the hair is
to be kept in perfect condition. To shampoo
the hair properly, sl.Ake it down, comb it out,
then dip the fingers into a little pure- olive or
almond oil, and thoroughly massage the
scalp. Then mix a teaspoonful of stallax
grannies in a cup of warm water. Fill a basin
with warm water, into 'which the ends of the
hair can fall, and shampoo the stallax mix-
ture into the roots in the usual way. Rinse,
and dry by fanning, or in the open air, if the
weather permits. Should the hair be excep-
tionally greasy, the- oil massage of the scalp
should Ise omitted. Stallax can be bought at.
any chemist’s in quarter-pound sealed pack-
ages. It keeps indefinitely, and one package
is sufficient for 25 or 30 shampoos,
A Tonic for Thin or Falling Hair.— When the
hair falls unduly, or has become thin and im-
poverished, a tonic should be applied every
night for six or eight weeks. To make a
simple and inexpensive but most efficacious
lotion., at home, take a quarter of a. pint, of
bay rum and add to it one ounce of boranium.
Shake thoroughly, and leave for 30 minutes;
then strain, and add sufficient water to make
half a pint. Every night massage the scalp
for 10 minutes, going over the entire head,
then dal) the mixture into the roots with a
soft- sponge. Regular brushing for a few
minutes every morning will also help to im-
prove the condition of the hair.
A Greasy Skin and Blackheads. —A greasy,
oily skin, with blackheads and enlarged
pores, is a sore trial, and many a girl goes
through life with her appearance entirely
spoiled with these unsightly blemishes, which
maj now be so quickly and effectively removed
with but little trouble. To remove blackheads
ami the dull, dirty look of the skin which
accompanies them, dissolve one st.vmol tablet
in a glass of hot water. Allow the efferves-
cence to subside, then dip a small soft sponge
into the liquid and bathe the face. Leave on
the skin for a few minutes, then dry with a
towel, and the blackheads will come right off.
For a greasy skin, bathe the face three times
a week with stymol and spray with cold water
every morning, using a vulcanite throat spray.
The Secret of a flood Complexion.—The secret
of a good complexion is so simple that, were
it only more widely known, there would be
no such thing as a rough, blotchy skin.
Nature has decreed that the outer cuticle,
When it shrivels and dies, Shall be removed;
but so many women fail to realise this.' To
remove, by absorption, the dead outer skin,
and preserve the complexion in all its pris-
tine beauty, mercolized wax is recommended,
ordinary creams and lotions being quite in-
effectual for this purpose. Smear the wax
over the face and neck, rub it gently into the
skin, and leave for the night. In the morning
wash it off. using a good soap, such as
•Pilenta, arid all the dead skin will be removed
with the wax. Then apply a lotion to re-
move any trace of greasiness and brace up
the skin, making it firm and white. The lotion
can lie made at home quite easily, by mixing
one ounce of cleminite in four tablespoonfuls
of hot water. Bottle, and. when cool, apply
to the face with a soft sponge, or the. tips of
the fingers, allowing it to dry. on the skin.
ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
C.B. —Clynol berries will reduce your weight.
They are harmless, and no alteration need be
made in your diet. They have also one great
advantage over other flesh-reducers: they re-
duce so gradually that there is no fear of
loose, flabby skin and wrinkles.
ZTJLA. —No, I am afraid I cannot tell you how
to prevent yourself from biting your nails.
You must exercise your will power.
CONSTANCE.— : To remove the yellowness
from your skin, get a tin of mercolized wax
and apply it every night, rubbing it well into
the skin. Leave it on till morning; then wash
it off with warm water and a good soap. (2)
To give a color to the cheeks, get a little col-
liandum and rub some on a leather. Apply, this
to the cheeks. It gives a perfectly natural -
looking flush to the .face, and is quite unde-
tectable. (3) To remove the blackheads, take
a stymol tablet and dissolve it in warm water.
When the effervescence has subsided mop the
lotion over the face, allow to remain on for
a few minutes, then wipe off, and the black-
heads will disappear.
GKOKGIE. —I am not surprised to hear that
•the electrolysis treatments were not success-
fill. They seldom are. Get an ounce of phe
minol and a tube of tekko paste. Use them in
conjunction, and you will soon be rid of the
troublesome superfluous hair.
SHEILA. —To restore your hair to its original
color, get two ounces of tammalite and mix it
with three ounces of bay rum. Apply to the
hair every night or morning. In a couple of
weeks there will be no sign of the greyness.
Tammalite is not a dye, but a natural re-
storer.
ELSIE.—To remove the roughness from your
skin, use a lotion made by mixing cleminite-
with warm water. Allow the lotion to cool,
then bottle, and use after washing the face
every morning. . [I

The Red Page
Lines
While the prose writer constructs sen-
tences and paragraphs, the poet builds lines
of communication —hard lines, like Tenny-
son’s “The shrill-edged shriek of a mother
divides the shuddering night” ; clothes-lines,
such as those on which Herrick was wont to
hang his Julia’s wearing apparel, and on
which Swift immodestly exhibited the soiled
linen of a lady friend; narrow-gauge lines,
which bump and jolt horribly ; fishing-lines,
like Wordsworth's: “Sisters and brothers,
little maid, how many may you be?”; or
lines of least resistance, in which the poet
tells “Pierrette” that he “is striving to for-
get,” or the “birds that pipe at morn pro-
claim the coming dawn,” or “the sea with
voice sonorous sings a grand eternal chorus,”
or words to that effect.
Generally speaking, great poems can be
identified by reason of the ease with which
single lines taken from them can produce
pleasure, though detached from their con-
text. All the same, many first-class poets
have been guilty of lines which workmanlike
minor bards would blush to father. Tenny-
son, who could produce wonderful lines like 1
“The long wash of Australasian seas,” could
also face the world with: “Out she went
while the wind like a broken worlding
wailed.” Our own Arthur Adams, who gave
us “The mighty calm of immortality”—
second only to Bedford’s “The.arctic pomp of
God” —also perpetrated : “And gaze and gaze
blankly at a blank face.” John Masefield,
like his great Australian rival, “Bellerive,”
favors telegraph lines, as* though every word
cost a penny :
His father clubbed
The girl on the head.
Young Will lipped
And shot him dead.
They hanged Will,
As Will said:
With one thrill
They choked him dead.
The foregoing, please note, is a quotation not
from “Bellerive,” but from Masefield.
Thomas Hardy, in his old age, has taken to
erecting clothes-lines
Oil, my pretty pink frock,
I shan’t be able to wear it!
Why is he dying just now?
I. can hardly bear it!
Oh, my pretty pink frock ;
Puff-sleeved" and accordeon-pleated !
He might have passed in July,
And nte uncheated !
Some poets deal in lines like shopkeepers,
who announce that they have new “lines” of
boot-brushes or sardines. This is the way
that Emerson (not Australian “Milky
White,” but the much-boomed Ralph Waldo)
brings some new lines under the notice of his
customers:—•
Hay,
corn,
roots,
flax,
apples
&
wood.
It looks like a storekeeper’s catalogue, but
Emerson puts it forward as a complete line
of poetry.
The most enduring lines —those which will
last as long as the world has' a sense of
humor—have been built by minor poets.
FTinstance, those in which Alfred Austin
mentioned that the British sceptre would
“never fade,” that the death of Queen "Vic-
toria left “a widowed world,” and that “A
mournful crowd round royal shroud is all
now left of him but yesterday a king”—
which was Alfred’s way of intimating that
all that remained in death of the person of
Edward VII. was a mournful crowd, which
yesterday had been a part of the king. Yet
Alfred, who is remembered chiefly for his
foolishness, could produce good Byronic
lines such as :
And all confess that none behave their best
Till the half-drunk lean over the half-dressed
which happen in “The Season”—a satire
upon London sassiety.
Among lines specially deserving of immor-
tality are Douglas Sladen’s “Her baby was
better and more cheerful. And condensed
milk appeared to agree with it” (which he
has attempted to excuse with the plea that he
was making a literal quotation from the
diary of his heroine) ; Ella Wheeler Wil-
cox’s “Be not afraid —the alftnony will be
promptly paid”; Arthur Bateman’s “Select
vour dresses and your underwear, and I
will send them on by carrier” ; and Jawbone
Nields “Eftsoons a nocent waterspout.”
HARRISON OWEN.
The Inky Way

THE POET’S RAW MATERIAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bai cl Algernon’s a fickle wretch;
No fealty can bind him;
With no sweet girl for any stretch
Of time you’ll ever find him.
He drifts about from flower to flower,
From cot to cot, and bower to bower.
A maiden sweet
Engenders heat
In him more fierce than tropic suns,
First melts his heart, and then it runs.
You see him flit from May to June,
From Marguerite to Daisy.
’Tis Pearl at morn, and Ruby’s noon;
For each he’s seeming crazy.
With Olive and with Lettice dearly
He loves; but not with Prudence, clearly.
Not creamy blonde
Nor brunette fond
Has held the wicked vagrant true
For longer than a song or two.
Yet golden Flo was sweet and gay,
Dark Kate was tall and solemn.
Of Flo he made a merry lay,
From Kate he got a column.
In lyrics or a sonnet sequence
He rhymed a few, but none with frequence.
“What luck that sad
Old Dante had.
The modern Beatrice, all me!
Is so soon written out,” sighed he.

Four Grain Silos, 120 feet high, 25 feet diameter, at Wagga, for the Murrumbidgee Co-operative Milling Co. Ltd.
The walls of these 4 great Silos are only 7 inches thick.
Built by Country Concrete Constructions Ltd., 47 King Street, Sydney,
General Scheme and Machinery Equipment by Thos. Robinson &. Sons, Rochdale, England; and Sydney .
Australia’s problem to-day is the preservation of wheat until ship-
ping becomes available.
Reinforced Concrete provides the cheapest, strongest, most durable
and fireproof structure that can be built for this purpose.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Midnight, Hobson’s Bay.
Greatness and littleness that speak together;
The mobile sea’s embraces, and the sky;
The dull old life slips oft' its cankering
tether;
I feel it is not possible to die.
The star-shine and the mystery of the sea
Make a dim twilight in the memory.
The winking city lamps, and Williamstown;
The soft insistence of the water breaking;
Profluent moon-rays in the deep sea drown —
Stirring the memory from the past to
waking—•
The sea embraces me —the sea’s caresses —
Around my body curl the long sea cresses.
The shore is hushed of all its noise and
laughter;
Ocean and sky are full blood-kin to me ;
Serene as they, for all that may come after,
Am I; serene of all that fate bears
stealthily
On the full tide, the starshine in it pearled.
All sorrow floats unto another world.
The midnight moon is wan to its downset-
ting;
The full, deep, silent tide heaves like a
bosom;
The crinkled water catches in its netting,
Star-flowers of sky, and every ocean
blossom;
The night sighs like a woman for her lover,
And for all shame and grief the sea is cover.
f /
Inter-related is all sense and sound,
Brother am I of sky, and earth, and sea;
Brother unto the ant upon the ground,
The eagle and the dove are friends to me.
The system’s music takes the song and
psalm—
Soul of the very weed; soul of the palm.
Soul of the man, and of the thistledown,
Greatest and least are equal in the sun ;
And the good thrush, singing in dress of
brown,
Knows not his message till the song’s
begun;
Nor knows that tree, air, sunlight, rose and
clover,
Are in his song until the song is over.
For who shall write the symphony of life
Shall use the sounds of life in all its
motions—
Lyric of lover, cry of the bedded wife,
Profluent light and fluxes of the oceans;
As from the sky above my face, while I lie
dreaming,
Music falls over from the moonlight stream-
ing.
QUEENSLAND.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
The Closed House.
Yon is a dwelling
Where one and his wife
Through the years eke out
What they misname life,
With never a joy or a joyous wind
To brush the cobwebs from ceiling or mind.
Closed are the windows
And drawn are the blinds,
Though the world without,
With its frank, fresh winds.
And song and color and scent, is rife,
And shouts aloud of the joy of life!
Rose of the morning
And gold of the day,
With the tides at work.
And the winds at play,
They know not, and seek not, as ever they
hide
From the big, brave fact of the world outside.
Little of beauty.
And little of love,
In the gloom have birth,
Though the shadows move ;
And ill is woven, and webs are spun,
By spiders shunning the light of the sun.
Hearken, O People !
The hour is at hand
When a voice shall ring
Through the waiting land :
And soaring, shouting, that voice shall say :
“Make broad the path for the Light-of-Day !
“Up with the windows,
Open the doors.
Let the sunlight in
On the walls and floors;
Too much of shadow and too much gloom
Make foul the corners of nation and room !”
N.S.W.
NOMEN.
Sky.
The Summer sky is just a bowl
Of old blue cbinaware;
The purple hills around the rim
Serrate a pattern rare,
And traceries of leaf and limb
All help to make it fair.
Those great airplanes some day will break
A gaping hole right through.
And showers of glittering shards will fall.
Then Philistine and Jew
Will sell the tourists souvenirs
Of bright enamel blue.
If one should climb up then and look
Out through the jagged hole,
What awful gulfs of void abyss
W r ould yawn beyond the bowl!
Such sights I do not think would be
Good for a human soul.
TASMANIA.
H. W. STEWART.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £1 10s. per annum, you can have The Bulletin
sent regularly to you every week for a year, to any
address in Australasia, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Politiial, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length, or, say,
3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned or preserved unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
ivlrich has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 31, 1918.
SCRAPS OF PAPER.
Moke than 25 years ago The Bulletin
explained in many articles the advantages of
a Government note issue as a means of mak-
jing great wealth out of nothing. It advo-
cated State notes .before there was a Com-
monwealth and Federal notes when the Com-
monwealth hove in view. The theory was
that the joint stock banks should be re-
quired, as in Canada, to hold at least 40
per cent, of their cash reserves in Govern-
ment notes, and should pay out in that cur-
rency, unless gold was specially requested.
That would mean, of course, that they would
hold probably 50 per cent, of their cash in
Government currency to prevent payments
reducing the stock below the legal margin.
Government banks, if any, would hold the
same proportion —probably a larger one. As
the figures then stood this arrangement was
reckoned to offer the Government the use of
about £10,000,000 without interest —not the
temporary use, but the command of it per-
manently—and the amount would increase
with the increase of population. (As events
have proved, the figure was ridiculously
under-estimated, but this paper was careful
to keep clear of wild-cat finance.) The
money, it was proposed, should be used in
the purchase of State securities in the open
market —the cheapest ones available. It was
not to be used in subscriptions to new loans,
which would have merely been making the
existing loan jag a little easier. When secu-
rities fell due the Public Notes Fund was to
renew its share of the loan on no better or
w'orse terms than private holders. The fund
created out of nothing was to accumulate
at compound interest till it overtook the
aggregate debts and extinguished them—
whenever that might be. At the rate of
interest then prevailing the whole debt of
that time might have been extinguished in
60 years or a little more, all out of nothing.
And more than one-third of 60 years has
alreadv elapsed.
* * * *
The proposition aroused various classes of
opposition. The banking community was
against any interference with its note busi-
ness, not having sufficient foresight to realise
that interference was coming and that if it
didn’t support this scheme it might get some-
thing worse. Fossils talked vaguely about
the dangers of unredeemable paper currency.
The proposal was for a currency redeemable
on demand, and one which should have at
the beginning £25 of gold and £75 of old
well-tried securities behind every £lOO of
paper, with the prospect of having in 50
years £25 in gold and £450 of old well-tried
securities behind each £lOO of paper, but the
Fossils didn’t notice the point. A lot of
people didn’t really know there was any debt,
or any debt worth mentioning, to pay off.
One N. S. Wales Labor member called on
the editor of The Bulletin to state that
the public debt was necessary as a means of
investment for widows, being apparently
under the belief that there was so little debt
and so many widows, or such intensely
absorbent widows, that the supply of public
embarrassments was really unequal to the
demand. Possibly, however, he was a fraud
rather than a fool. The big trouble, of
course, was that a note issue, the profits of
which were to be rigidly set aside for the
extinction of old debts instead of being
available for new razzles, was not a thing to
be advocated or advertised or even mentioned.
The only party which wanted Government
paper wanted it on very different lines.
* * * *
At the end of 1910, when a good many
years’ interest had been lost, the business
was commenced in a half-hearted way. The
simple, well-tried device of requiring banks
to bold at least 40 per cent, of their cash
reserves in paper was omitted, so the paper
had no assured circulation. Because of that
it was only considered safe to reckon on a pre-
carious issue of £7,000,000, with a 25 per cent,
gold reserve against it. Against every £1
note issued above £7,000,000 a sovereign was
to be held, so the largest amount the Com-
monwealth could have to invest was
£4,500,000. whereas it should have been at
least £10,000,000. No 10s. notes were printed
for another three years, so a valuable and
vital feature of the business was neglected.
And there was no cast-iron rule about the
investment of the money at compound inter-
est for the ultimate extinction of the exist-
ing public debt. Possibly the weak Fisher-
Hughes Ministry didn’t care. Probably it
had orders from the caucus that the profits
must not be tied up so that they couldn’t
be seized by hungry Treasurers for new
works. It was all shuffle, half-heartedness,
side-stepping and apology.
* * * #
The Australian Notes Fund up to Septem-
ber 16 had made a profit of £4,271,656 —all
money made out of nothing. It might show
a profit of £8,000,000 or more if the idea had
been adopted in the first year of Federation,
instead of the tenth, and if it hadn’t been
a feeble botch in addition. But the earnings
are no provision against old debts falling due
in disastrous times. They are lent to wild-
cat Treasurers so that they may pursue the
wild cat more strenuously, now that the
animal is shy. also greasy about the tail.
Often the Federal Treasurer borrows from
the Federal fund for his own public works,
and puts the new Federal p.n. into it as
security. In the early days, when George
Turner was the most frequent Treasurer the
Commonwealth paid for its works out of
revenue. Now, when Webster makes
“poetry” for the Ministry, and Hughes
lives in England at the public expense, and
High Commissioner Fisher dwells in a
£1,000,000 palace in London, things are dif-
ferent. Even lighthouses are being charged
to loans, though a lighthouse isn’t a wildly
reproductive institution, and the obliging
Notes Fund finds the price of the sea-illu-
mination and takes a lien on the thing as
security. The great, idea has become a de-
moralising agency and an aid to extrava-
gance, like everything else that Australia’s
rulers lay their hands on. Even religion has
been pawned in Tasmania for £lOO,OO0 —
which seems a wonderful thing.
A few weeks ago it was announced that
the Westralian Government proposed apply-
ing to Britain for a suspension of its sink-
ing fund. The Gold State stands on a
different footing from its neighbors. By a
special and rigid agreement all its loans
carry a sinking fund, while in the case of
other States only a few of the older ones are
thus encumbered. Partly through the confi-
dence thus inspired Westralia contrived, until
1913, to owe money at a lower rate of in-
terest than any of its neighbors. But though
it had a bigger sinking fund than all its
neighbors put together it borrowed so hard
on the strength of the aforesaid confidence
that its debt, over and above the sinking
fund, is the biggest per head in the Common-
wealth. As already mentioned, it proposed
to keep the debt and abolish part of the
security, and the other States, bar Victoria,
decided to give it moral support by asking
permission from J. Bull to repudiate then-
obligations also. The amounts involved are
miserable enough. The States which are
rushing in to back Westralia added' less than
£240,000, or a quarter the price of Australia
House, towards their old debts reserve dur-
ing the year to June, 1917. equal to rather
less than :Jd. in the £. After falling back on
the Federal Notes Fund until it can hardly
be fallen back upon any longer, and borrow-
ing from the hard-pressed parent in England
for such purely peace purposes as school-
buildings and river improvements until
the hard-pressed parent in England
told the States to stop crowding, it
seems rather poor business to ask him
for permission to swindle their creditors
in a petty way by depriving them of a frac-
tion of their security. It is understood that
if Petty Repudiation Bills are passed they
will not be vetoed. All the same, the credi-
tor will probably add a bit per cent, to the
interest on next renewal owing to a new feeling
of insecurity, and in that way some more
of the glamor will be rubbed off the Note
Reserve and the wonderful Australia House.
Canada, the other big white dominion, has
lent .T. Bull a trifle of £120.000,000 or
£130,000,000 to help on the war, and the old
man. busy as he is, may have time to notice
the difference.
POLITICAL MACHINES AND
MACHINE POLITICS.
Everybody is weary at times of machine
politics, save those who live or at
least profit by machine politics. The
system is neither useful nor. beautiful, nor
has it, like many: plain females, the saving
grace of being honest. Its purpose, of course,
is not to get the majority party into office,
but to get “our” party into office, and if “our”
party with a minority of actual supporters
can secure a majority of seats there is all the
more glory and satisfaction. The system
starts with the creation of a bureaucracy of
party managers and organisers. These indi-
viduals don’t represent a productive class in
any sense of the term, and they use up a
great deal of salary and stationery. Their
duty is, in the first place, to deprive the
people by arts and devices of the right to
choose their own law-makers save in the
sense that they are allowed to decide between
two candidates for each seat —“our” nominee
who is necessary, and the other machine’s
nominee, who is an unavoidable evil and a
nuisance. Party organisers and managers
are usually men of an inferior type, with
cunning in place of wisdom. Their business,
as a rule, leads to nothing except in the end
a nomination as the machine candidate and
a billet as a machine hack in Parliament,
neither of which prizes appeals to a person of
high ideals with a desire to be keeper of his
own conscience. They exist, as already men-
tioned, to get into power the men whom the
people don’t really want, for in a community
which had no party machines the men whom
the people want would get in automatically,
and no back-door or back-yard agency would
collect salary or blackmail or “chout” (a
good Indian word that means a lot) for
getting them in. The party managers and
organisers arrange for the supply of candi-
dates, and also for the supply of two sets of
political principles, or at least propositions.
These being to a great extent furnished by
unproductive men of moderate intelligence
who like that kind of work, they naturally
represent that type of intelligence. They are
such as the managers, judging other p.eople
by themselves, think most likely to put men
like themselves in power. Nobody, or hardly
anybody, tells unnleasant truths to the people
about the people, however necessary it may
be to arouse the public to the fact that it is
on a downward road ; but unpleasant truths
about the rival machine are abundant —also
unpleasant lies. Thus, as every party for
the last 50 or 60 years has been a lazy,
easy-going, corrupt, borrowing party, neither
crowd of machine politicians will declare
that borrowing is an opium-eater’s disease
which should be sworn off. That would be
an act of repentance, and the machine never
repents. But each one declares that the other
one can’t be trusted with the loan money,
and that pure politics depend on Nielsen
instead of Beeby having the cheap trip to
America—or else Beeby having it instead of
Nielsen.
* •* # *
When matters have been so fixed that it is
hardly worth while for any candidate save
the machine article to run, and it is
hardly possible to get a chance to vote
for any principles save machine prin-
ciples, a certain degree of order or political
paralysis has been arrived at. At the last
N. S. Wales State elections four contests
were won by men who didn’t wear a machine
label except inside their clothes, and three of
them scraped in on a second ballot. This is
a device whereby, when there are three or
more candidates (generally two machine
nominees and a dark horse) for one seat, and
ho one secures aii absolute majority, the first
two run again while the third looks on from
the grandstand. Contrary to expectations,
the result at the N. S. Wales election of 1917
was that more voters turned up at the second
ballot than at the first. One theory was that
careful record was made of the absentees at
the first poll, and they were represented,
either personally or by unauthorised deputy,
at the second. Another unexpected result
was that out of eight second ballots six
shifted the second man into first place. This
may have been because the second man’s
crowd felt more desperate apd reckless. But
the result was much the same. The new
Assembly consisted of one machine party
which couldn’t denounce the outrageous
Norton-Gbiffiths contract because it had
been i n the deal, and another which was in
the same situation. Norton-Griffiths
being a huge borrowing proposition, both
parties had been in it, so the pot and the
kettle smothered their feelings.
The preferential voting idea, which is
now coming, into fashion, holds out the
blessed possibility that it may upset all
previous calculations and introduce an ele-
ment of the unexpected. That is, provided
it is done on whole-hog principles. There
may be devices by which the old party mana-
gers can work it to their own advantage in
the old way, but these ways haven’t become
apparent yet. The aim of preferential
voting (on whole-hog principles, as already
mentioned) is to encourage an abundance of
candidates and to give the dark horse a
show. If there are 10 applicants for one
seat the elector must vote for them all.
numbering them one to ten in the order of
his preference. That is going the entire
animal; half the animal is purposeless. If
he is a machine-made elector he will give
his first and best vote to his own party
hack, and his tenth and worst one to that
loathsome contemporary, the rival party
hack. Then there are left him eight votes
which he must allot to prevent his ballot
being null and. void, and the ordinary indi-
vidual will give them according to the
merits of the candidates as he understands
them. He may, of course, be so besotted
with rage at the innovation that he will give
his second suffrage to a person whom he
suspects of insanity, and the third to the
most immoral man on the list, but that is
unlikely. When the first count is indecisive
there ensues a counting of second and third
and other votes, with the result that a
man with only his merits or supposed merits
to recommend him may come out on top,
largely. through the secondary votes which
were given him to keep the loathsome con-
temporary out. What might happen if each
machine party put up 10 candidates, in
order to rope in the secondary votes, is
not clear. But at aN. S. Wales State
election the two old parties would have then
to find 1800 candidates between them, which
would be thrilling but improbable.' The
theory, on which real preferential voting is
based is that a number of men with no party
organisation, but with an idea To Dut for-
ward or a protest to antpr. might
together and put up 90 candidates on an
abolition-of-borrowing or extirpation-of-
Party-Government issue, and achieve sur-
prising results. They would have to get a
hearing. Many a hack voter, after giving
his first vote, as a matter of routine, to a
hard borrower who wore his party label,
might give his second vote to the non-bor-
rower, because he thought the idea worth
trying, or even out of pure astonishment. And
the evening of that eventful day might see
the two party machines in the debris heap
and an Assembly returned on an issue re-
garding which neither set of old managers
would have given the community a chance
to speak. There might be no such sensa-
tional results as these, but at least there
would be an opportunity to take a vote on
many matters which are now smothered. It
says a great deal for the preferential-voting
idea, whether it is whole- or half-hog, that
the Labor men are its strenuous opponents.
Labor, with the trades unions behind it, has
by far the more efficient, though not neces-
sarily the more dishonest, election apparatus,
and if there is a chance of apparatus being
ruined it has more to lose than its opponents.
A CITY WITHOUT LAND SALES.
On a recent day, which looked just like any
other day, a deputation, which looked just
like any other deputation, called on a very
ordinary-looking N. S. Wales Minister with
a grievance that is so old and frequent that
rabbits are rare historical landmarks by
comparison. What the deputation desired
to draw attention to is the habit of running
almost every kind of public enterprise in the
special interests of the importing and land-
owning and land-jobbing crowds of Sydney.
Also, of course, in the interests of the papers
which live on these folk and profit by them.
Likewise, of course, in the interests of the
Sydney Million Club, a purely city concern
which, when unpleasant comments were
made on it, hastily camouflaged itself as the
N. S. Wales Millions Club without changing
its character. The instance which the depu-
tation alleged was certain railway wheat
rates. If a wheat-grower at Tamworth
wanted to send a ton of wheat to a flourmill
at Glen Innes (141 miles from Tamworth
and 141 miles further away from Sydney)
the charge was 18s. 4d., or rather over l|d.
per mile. If wheat was sent from Glen
Innes to Sydney (423 miles) the charge was
12s. Bd., or less than -Jd. per mile. Flou
milling is a difficult industry to drag to the
city because Sydney doesn’t lie in a wheat-
growing district, and so is further from the
source of supply than the usual country mill.
Moreover, as a mere matter of weight and
bulk, it is more simple to grind wheat where
it grows and move the flour by rail than to
move the whole raw* article. Still, nearly
one-fourth of the milling business has been
persuaded to the One Big City. Seeing that
the One Big City has grabbed nearly three-
fourths of the State’s manufacturing indus-
tries in general it hasn’t done so well as
usual in this instance, yet it has done well
enough. In 1896 Sydney and suburbs stood
for 58 per cent, of the State’s manufactures,
judging by the number of workers employed,
while in 1917 the city grab had gone up to
72 per cent. So the movement cityward goes
steadily along.
* * * *
In a recent issue of The Bulletin
appeared a list of the perishing towns and
counties of N. S. Wales. There were half a
hundred towns on the list, not reckoning coal-
mining centres. Naturally the country towns
have to perish. If they weren’t discouraged
by careful differentiation between “up” and
“down” railway charges and such-like de-
vices, those which don't naturally fall within
the orbit of Sydney might demand that new
outlets. should have a chance to grow at more
convenient ports. It is explained, of course,
that Port Stephens is wanted, or may be
wanted, for Federal naval purposes, which
is a rather thin device. The Commonwealth
doesn’t show any sign of wanting the place
so badly as not to be open to argument,
especially seeing it has dozens of equally
suitable places to choose from. Also, it is
explained that Jervis Bay is required by the
same Commonwealth cormorant as an outlet
for the scarcely-born and already-dying capi-
tal. Also, that Coff’s Harbor and Grafton,
which were places of great promise till the
North Coast railway threatened to begin to
make them accessible, are really shallow
spots of no account. Also that Twofold Bay
is for some unexplained reason a place that
mustn’t be mentioned, possibly because it is
infested by devils. All these objections might
be over-ridden if a northern or southern
groun of country towns—those outside the
natural Sydney hinterland —became suffici-
ently strong to speak with effect. And if
new cities like Sydney were to arise, the
Labor party, and a good many non-Labor-
ites, couldn’t for very shame demand that
the ground on which they stand should be
privately owned, and that the unearned in-
crement should be a private grab and
private gamble and private boom and
private steal and private swindle. The
non-borrowing Labor principle has been
thrown overboard. Even the abolition
of State Upper Houses is rather dead.
But to directly oppose the principle
of public land-ownership would b 6 too much.
Public ownership of city lands, where the
unearned increment is, counts for far more
than public ownership of dusty backblocks
areas which run one ox to the square mile.
A sleeping dog of size is a dreadful animal to
wake.
So the Labor party, like the non-Labor
parties, is quite speechless about any rever-
sal of the One Big City policy. None of the
parties can be made aware of its existence.
For the idea opens up the horrible prospects
of the city without land sales. There would
hardly be a single land-agent in the place,
though there would probably be house-agents;
but then house-agency is a dreary thing.
There would be hardly a conveyancer or a
land company, for what dabbling there was
to do would be in country lands and would
gravitate to the country towns. There would
be 30 or 40 columns less of advertising in
the Saturday edition of the very big daily,
for the land-auction advt. would be missing.
The land auctioneer would be as extinct as
dodo. That cheery Saturday-afternoon


festivity, when hundreds of people are
beguiled by golden weather and free lunch to
places beyond the bounds of habitation,
where they buy bits of cow-paddock at more
per foot than the cow and the paddock were
worth a year or two before, and come home
feeling rich because they have 25J years in
which to pay the instalments —it would all
be as dead as the debris of Caesar's feast.
No spider would become suddenly affluent by
selling land-titles when the community was
in the midst of a paper-money boom, and no
fly would think he was suddenly affluent
through buying them. There would always
be some dealing in Government leaseholds,
but compared with the eternal gamble in free-
holds it would be like thin flat water com-
pared with over-proof rum. The dealing
might even be done by a Government office,
which would be the last straw.
* * *
Sydney has been for the past year or two
the centre of a boom. Very few people recog-
nise that a boom is a boom until it has
passed and become a burst, so as usual the
phenomenon has attracted little notice. It
has been assumed to be merely a develop-
ment of the great natural resources of the
country. With the population of the State
actually diminished by the departure of men
for the war and by other temporary causes,
and with building reduced some 50 per cent,
through high-priced labor and high-priced
imported materials, the rush for suburban
allotments is chiefly a poker game, but it is a
very pleasant game while it lasts. If drink,
opium and horse-racing were all abolished at
once it would hardly be a greater change
than if this great city industry of swapping
fictitious values for a little cash and a stack
of p.n.’s was extinguished. Labor members,
in proportion to their numbers, are
just as much addicted to this dis-
sipation as other members. The fas-
cination lies in the fact that, so long as a
city keeps increasing in population, there
must, on the whole, be more winners at the
game than losers. The new population
creates the stakes — i.e., the unearned incre-
ment —by its mere existence, and that part of
the population which runs the game or is
paid for advertising the game divides them.
And it is the biggest game going where, as
is the case with Victoria or S.A. or N.S.W.,
there is only one really big faro table in the
whole State. The creation of new seaport
cities on the private land ownership system
would mean the establishment of rival faro
tables with rival sets of players, which would
be annoying to the olcl crowd at the old
table. The competition might even make
the old table run at a loss for a while; in
other words, if the Big City had to live on
that part of the State which is geographi-
cally attached to it instead of on the whole
State there might be an actual decrease in
values, though there would be a bigger ex-
pansion elsewhere. As for the establishment
of rival tables with the stakes divided equally
among the people at large, there might as
well be no faro game at all. It was all very
good to “save face” by making the Federal
capital public property, for everybody knew
Canberra to he a hopeless place that wouldn’t
compete seriously against anything ; but with
a real new city the case would be different.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Habit of Prying into Things.
Last week the Australian newspapers re-
ported this
A. B. Piddington, Chief Inter-State Commissioner,
began an investigation into house rents yesterday.
He explained that his colleagues on the Commission
were engaged in a similar investigation in Melbourne.
It came as a revelation that there was still
to be found in the Commonwealth an open-
ing for Red Tape to make more investiga-
tions. The flight of N. S. Wales Minister
for Labor Beeby towards U.S. in order to
make investigations about something or other
seemed to confirm the apprehension that the
opportunities on the premises had been
worked to death. Anyway, inquiries appeared
to have been made by somebody or other
during the last four or five years into every
conceivable subject, point, commodity, matter,
rumor, allegation, complaint, supposition,
illegality and proceeding. From leaking
cashboxes to the price of meat, from the
habits of the weevil to the cost of Can-
berra, from investigations into the hygienic
conditions of soldiers’ camps to prolonged
study of booze, from Maryborough to Perth
Royal Commissions, committees and boards
have raged like a plague. Some of them have
been worth the expenditure. Most of them
have been just plain experiments in the art
of side-stepping. As the Inter-State Com-
mission has now got to the stage of doubling-
back on its tracks, so to speak, and starting
investigations into matters that have been
investigated on about 175 occasions, the time
appears to have arrived for appointing a
Board, or something, to examine a.nd report
upon (11 the causes of the investigating
mania, (2) the cost thereof, (3) the results
achieved by investigation up to date, (4) the
matters, if any. open to further investiga-
tion. (51 the possibility of preventing blither-
ing idiocy masquerading as a manifestation
of the spirit of inquiry. Unless something
of this kind is done it looks as if the whole
population will have to knock off work for a
couple of years and proceed to give evidence,
and burrow for several more years through
vast accumulations of report and documen-
tary exhibit. This “investigation into house
rents,’’ for instance, seems about the limit of
futility. Commissions beyond number have
at various times wrestled with the question,
and all the major and minor questions in-
cidental thereto. There is an easily avail-
able mountain of literature on the subject
available to everybody, and anyone who
wants more can find tons of it in the caverns
beneath the nation’s numerous Houses of
Parliament. The ink is scarcely dry on the
report of a Victorian Housing Commission.
The industrious Knibbs is busy every day
accumulating more statistics about rent.
Labor Department officials toil from morn to
night keeping pace with records relating to
the subject. The facts relating to the
influence of congestion on rents, to the preva-
lence of the slum, to the prices of land and
building material, to the existence of trans-
port facilities, and to Jerry Builder. Esq.,
have all been dragged out on multitudinous
occasions. What has not been dragged out
of his hiding-place is the politician capable
of making up his mind about what he wants
to do and how he proposes to do it. His
place is taken by the individual with a pas-
sion for getting a report, dropping the docu-
ment into the morgue and later on deciding
to get another report. Meanwhile the kind
of evidence that is secured at these inquiries
was cheerfully emphasised by the testimony
of a witness who appeared before Pidding-
ton in Sydney last week. He announced
that he had given the housing and rent pro-
teins close study, and that in order to secure
the steady construction of new houses it: was
desirable to —
1. Authorise the State Savings Bank to advance
more than two-thirds of the capital value on sound
house-building propositions.
2. Permit or encourage the payment of war bonds
and stock for the construction of houses.
3. Abolish the restriction of rent returns on pri-
vate house investments.
4. Fix the price of locally-produced building
material and furniture.
5. Restrain the excessive sales of suburban allot-
ments, so that more money may be diverted into
building on lots already purchased.
The boiled-down meaning of this is recom-
mendation of a public policy based on (.1)
no restriction upon the rent collected for the
slum or any place; (2) restriction upon the
wages of furniture-makers and other work-
ers; (3) an intensification of land monopoly.
It may be worth while paying Piddington
and other people to go round in search of
this sort of stuff. But The Bulletin would
not like to have the job of proving that
Piddington would be giving the nation less
valuable service if he did some more useful
work.
The Art of Using the Kaiser as a Stall.
Asked the other day in the House of Reps,
by H. Gregory, from W.A.. to give Parlia-
ment a chance of seeing a balance-sheet of the
Small Arms Factory, Partly Prime Minister
Watt repeated a much-worn gag. This was
to the effect that the information asked for
could not be supplied “because it would
enable other countries to know what we are
doing.” This answer has been so often given
to cloak muddle and waste in the past that
The Bulletin is disposed to strongly sus-
pect it of being used for similar purposes
again. In any case the allegation is dis-
honest. No reasonably sane citizen of the
Commonwealth can be expected to believe
that it matters 2d. whether the Turk or
the German are made familiar with the
details of the operations at Litligow. Nor
is it within the region of probability that
if the particulars were forwarded per aero-
plane to Berlin the German would waste
five minutes over them. He has important
matters to attend to. Wherefore Watt’s
talk about “the harm that might -be done”
is useless. No man knows better than
himself that the question is not whether
other countries might get to know of
what is being done hereabouts (mostly
because nothing is being done that would
interest them), but whether the people 0 f
Australia are entitled to know the facts
relating to their own business. There is a
huge annual expenditure on the Small Arms
Factory, and in view of that circumstance
alone it is a gross breach of duty for the
Government to persist in secrecy. How
many rifles have been produced at the fac-
tory? How many have been condemned?
How many have been actually issued for ser-
vice? How many retained by the men they
were handed to? How long has it taken the
factory to produce a rifle of service pattern?
Has the factory produced a single machine-
gun? If not —why? What has been the
cost of the output? Until Watt can get
out of the habit of publicly shivering at the
idea of the Kaiser finding out something
fresh about Pearce and can brace himself
up to recognise the difference between win-
ning wars and making foolish excuses for
untidiness, this paper is going _ to wait for
answers to the foregoing questions without
enthusiasm. Moreover, taking the Defence
expenditure all round, there is great reason
for the unhappiness that comes from dread-
ful forebodings. The recent report of the
Naval Commission concerning the outflow
of cash on various works is a dismal docu-
ment. Horrible waste was kept secret from
the Germans (and Australians) until the
other day, and there is mighty little guaran-
tee that more of it will not be perpetrated
to-morrow. The estimates for the year also
provide for enormous expenditure on machin-
ery and plant for the arsenal, a concern
which is apparently going after all to be
added to the collection of humpies at Can-
berra. The current votes under this head
amount to £172,820, and as somewhere over
a couple of millions will be needed to provide
housing for the arsenal employees (unless they
are supplied with houseboats on the ornamental
lakes) there ought to be quite a lot of money
available directly to fling after the millions
flung thereabouts before. As the Govern-
ment also announces that there must be a
lot of secrecy about the arsenal as well as
about the Small Arms Factory, The Bul-
letin suggests that the day fixed for taking
the lid off should at least be made a public
holiday. Spending money in the open has
usually been a momentous business in Aus-
tralia. The results of spending it down a
shaft ought to be worth hearing about.
The Caucus of the Anti-Caucus Party.
It seems no more than a day or two since
the Australian atmosphere was shattered in
frequent places with great regularity by the
yells of political sprouters about the horrors
ot the party Caucus. The tyranny of the
Caucus was a subject of violent denuncia-
tion. Freedom-loving citizens were ex-
horted to arise and banish the Caucus-ridden
push from the premises, to restore to Par-
liament its independence from the wire-pull,
to give juntas the boot, and thus slay
a brutal and irresponsible tyranny.
The evils of the machine-made process were
wept over daily by the S. M. Herald ; the
beauties of a Legislature in which every
man would stand four-square by lus prin-
ciples and be answerable to his constituents
only were held up to public admira-
tion by the moving prose of the Telegraph.
At the Federal and N. 8. Wales State
elections the electors gave the anti-
Caucus party what was reported to be
“an emphatic victory.” It is quite likely
that a lot of simple souls thought so. any-
way. Since then they have been able to
notice that the Caucus system, instead of
being buried, has flourished like prickly-
pear; that the party which was dead against
the Caucus before the polls has repented of
its oratory and become even more addicted
to the machine-in-the-cellar than the other
crowd. Not only is the anti-Caueus crowd
“taking control of the Legislature,” but the.y
are getting busy in municipal politics if
Richard Teece. late serang of the A.M.P.,
is to be believed. Teece had latterly de-
veloped an ambition to be an alderman of
Sydney, but because he objected to being con-
trolled by the Nationalist machine he dis-
covered that the machine had decided
to try to get someone else elected.
Also, during last week there were
cheerful revelations of the Caucus hav-
ing prevented certain proposals, formulated
by the Ministry, to control the Sydney meat-
market pirates being submitted to Parlia-
ment, and about a wildly-comic struggle in
the Caucus concerning preferential voting
and proportional representation. The case
of Teece is just about as bad as anything
the Nationalists could produce from the
records of the Labor Caucus. In the other
two instances both happenings turn a
thousand Nationalist speeches and resolu-
tions and pamphlets into ridicule. In both
the object of the Caucus has been to dragoon
individual members of the party and _ to
throttle Parliament. This paper has nothing
to say in defence of the Caucus system. It
is an evil thing, generated from evil methods
of government. The sooner the public
purges the political life of the Common-
monwealth of the causes which keep the
Caucus alive, the better for the people. But
the first move in that direction is not com-
menced by flinging out one set of Caucus-
mongers and telling another set to take
charge of the vacancy—especially when it
happens that the first set are the least dis-
reputable. They at any rate did not lie.
The Nationalists lied hard, and the. best
they can say about their Caucus perjuries
is that they are not ashamed of them.
The Voluntary Ballot.
“Syd Ney” : Over on the other side of the
world Billjim is doing his job, and not say-
ing much. But he sometimes wonders what
is going to happen when the constantly-
diminishing trickle of reinforcements dries
up altogether and leaves him stranded.
His favorite nightmare is a vision
of all the surviving Billjims being banded
together into a Labor Battalion, filling in
shell-holes and making roads behind the line.
At our end we try every dodge except the
right one to persuade the elusive slacker into
a fashionable suit. Making passionate
appeals to a crowd of old men, women, and
returned soldiers in Martin-place is one of
the least effective. The voluntary enlistment
ballot is well enough as far as it goes, though
i'c doesn’t go very far. But Minister for
Recruiting Orchard seems to be going the
right way to check its already dubious chance
of success. His pathetic appeal in the daily
papers to rejected men to rush in a body to
lend their support to the. movement savors
of a desperate desire to fill out the ranks of
the balloteers at whatever cost. He said :
“The physical standard is in no wise re-
duced ; rather it is more severe than hither-
to.” Obviously he holds out no hope to rejects
of getting into the army under the voluntary-
ballot scheme. What, then, i§ the object in
appealing so earnestly, to them to submit
their names? It is a waste of time on their
part and on his. Orchard said that the
scheme *‘is a nation-wide endeavor to bring
home to the eligible man the need of his
services and at the same time appeal to his
sporting instincts.” If the elusive eligible
had any sporting instincts he wouldn’t be
standing on the kerb cheering his cobbers
away. The voluntary enlistment ballot would
be an excellent thing if only it were not vol-
untary.
♦
No Bear Has Honor in His Own
Gum-tree.
This letter reaches The Bulletin from
New York: —
The Bulletin’s cartoons representing \V. M.
Hughes as indulging in a career of riotous living
and lavish festivities are as misleading as the German
communiques. I sat beside him at a dinner given
him—gratis—in New York, and I take my oath
that he did not eat anything but two spoonsful of
soup (thin) and four slices of pineapple, or drink
anything but ice-water. I never met a man who
would talk longer for fewer viotuals. If you think
Mr. Hughes has been over-eating just weigh him
when he gets back. As for his sumptuous suite, let
me say that he received the journalists —who, as you
know, are the most important people in any com-
munity—in a 10 x 12 bedroom, he sitting on the
bed, and his guests, such as could not get one of the
three chairs, also sat on the bed or on the radiator
or leaned against the wall. We used to think our
President democratic and unpretentious, but that
was before we knew the Australian and Maorilarid
Premiers. Also, while I have got your ear —if I have
—don’t you think you are over-doing the prophet-
without-honor-in-his-own-country business? We found
Mr. Hughes’s ideas about the partition of the Pacific
and preferential Protection mighty interesting. AVe
have, in the past, learned lots from Australia in
the way of labor legislation, land reform and other
matters of internal organisation. Possibly we may
also become your more or less docile disciples in
foreign policy.
To which The Bulletin offers a few words
in unobtrusive reply :
It is alleged, till the remark becomes boresome,
that a prophet isn’t honored in his own country.
Neither is a bogus nobleman honored in his own vil-
lage. When a romantic fraud sets up as the Earl of
Manhattan or the Count de Vanderbilt, he doesn’t
begin his adventurous career amid his childhood’s
surroundings, where everybody knows that he is plain
Smith, gifted with a mass of raven hair, a Grecian
nose, two attractive legs, a pleasant way with the
girls, and an air of mystery taken out of cheap
novels. The famous Ticiiborne claimant was a pass-
able baronet of high degree at Wagga (N.S.AV.).
AVhen he went to England to collect the estates he
didn’t pass at Wapping, where he immediately met
the fat girl he had mashed in his youth. Very
often, however, his own country is the only one in
which a prophet is honored —especially if he is a 3ft.
prophet in a 2ft. community. There he becomes
mayor and passes as a considerable rooster. The
time when he discovers that he is only an egg comes
later. That is when he goes out to a bigger place
to offer his services as Chancellor. However the
illustration has nothing to do with the Hughes
case. The Bulletin never accused or suspected
Hughes of being a heavy feeder. It always viewed
him as the skeleton at the feast, a skeleton being a
thing that doesn’t eat because it has no place to put
the food. Australia’s Prime Minister has some place
to put his food, but not much of a place. His indi-
gestion is proverbial. Even those who live with
Hughes, and know him—know his irritability, his
pomp, his limelight, his special police, his important
entrances and exits and all the rest —never accused
him of being a bad stage-manager. His appearance
in New York as the simple village maiden or as
Diogenes in his tub was the right thing to strike
the press. Apparently it did strike the press, and
the press was the right thing to strike. It was a
pocket-edition of the Abraham Lincoln style. The
objection to Hughes in Australia is his failure to
Do Things. If he had merely to talk in Australia
and had to do things in England the estimates of hid
value might be reversed. Hughes in England, with
a constituency to hold —perhaps a difficult constitu-
ency, with a dozen local interests worrying him, with
a department to administer and with 95 per cent,
of his oratory cut off, would be just our old Hughes
and no other. And seeing that there are in England
more than 30 full-blown Ministers to divide the
talking, and the whole lot don’t habitually talk
much more than Hughes does on his own, fully 95
per cent, of his oratory would be cut off. Then he
would be the same hot, testy and hesitating poli-
tician, balanced on one leg on the fiscal question and
a dozen other questions, mixed in his finance, igno-
rant of the value of money, filled with a boundless
faith in loan expenditure, hardly able to tell the
difference between loans and revenue, and impatiently
referring almost every difficult matter to a hew
“bureau” of expensive and badly-chosen men, with a
general instruction to look into the universe immedi-
ately and present a report—but a splendid party
engineer all the time.
The Prime Minister appealed to England as a
dauntless orator who could afford to be dauntless
because be had a whole Treasury behind him to
pay the libel damages and costs. He hasn’t had-
time to grow very stale. And he is a speaker of a
pattern that is unusual in England, so he has the
charm of novelty. In Australia there are many of
his type, though not many of so pronounced a type,
and that makes a difference. One reason why even
the chief bear often lacks honor in his own gum-
tree is that gum-trees with bears in them are numer-
ous. The same bear going abroad as a solitary or
rare specimen, with his secretary carrying his gum-
tree, has chances of his own while he is new.

“Passenger”: Talking about the N. S.
Wales Railway Department—the marvellous
department that demands from the ordinary
citizen an electric torch wherewith to dis-
cover (1) its scheme of making its lines pay,
and (2) the lighting of its trains after sun-
down. If this isn’t the dead and ultimate
limit I would admire to be contradicted.
Some days ago I lost a first-class quarterly
ticket (paid for in advance, plus ss. deposit,
the metal ticket being worth, perhaps, decimal
0), which was the sign manual to checkers
that the department had contracted to carry
me to and from a Sydney suburb. After wait-
ing to see if the lost disc had been returned
to the department and placed in the lost-dog
office (naturally that is where the depart-
ment Avould put it) I applied for another
ticket; Having made a statutory declara-
tion before a ,T.P. that I had lost
my medal I was given a slip of
paper which informed guards, etc., that I
was a sound customer and had paid up. That
Convoy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Fifteen ships from Halifax, we stride across
the seas,
And every mile’s a little mile beneath our
heavy tread;
For we have fought with distance and have
brought her to her knees,
And the bully armed merchantman is boom-
ing on ahead.
Fifteen ships from Halifax and little do we
reck
Of the wave’s thrust or the wind’s words that
echo on our steel,
For the singing of the Englishmen is ringing
from the deck,
And the naked strength of England’s arm is
living in the keel.
Fifteen ships from Halifax; we’ve dropped
the lights of town,
For the deep zones and the sky’s arch, and
the level plains of blue.
Our walk’s the walk of drunken men, we’re
painted like a clown ;
But the silver threads of Destiny are in the
winding screw,
And the crank’s leap and the bow’s lift and
the drift of funnel smoke.
Oh, fifteen ships from Halifax are driving
down the way,
With brown hands at the throttle and the
guiding tiller-spoke,
For the sun-white cliffs of England and the
glint of channel spray.
Amid the brooding midnight ’neath the mar-
vel of the skies,
When the drifting, shifting fog-hank puts its
fingers o’er the sight,
Our merry bells are pealing out like little
mellow cries;
And you’ll hear us calling, calling through
the shadows of the night.
The dark waves turn, the pale fires burn
along the gliding strake,
The knuckled fingers tighten on the tautened
whisfle-cord;
For ’tis tenderly and daintily the passage
we must make —-
The fifteen ships from Halifax with British
hearts aboard.
Fifteen ships from Halifax, we quest the
outer seas:
Oh, the grounders in the tide-rip that are
racking into foam !
But the yellow lights of London town are
flaring by the quays.
And the yellow lights of London town shall
guide the English home.
Fifteen ships from Halifax, we play our
honest part,
And brave upon the ocean roads our banners
are unfurled.
To the pulse-beat of our engines, like a beat-
ing British heart.
Me bear the English homeward from the
corners of the world.

Where the Enemy Stands.
The point of interest is swiftly moving from
the trench to the place where people are
talking. Germany is done. There can
be no doubt about that, for even though her
armies be still in being, so to speak, they
are incapable of defending her from the
forces they failed to defeat. Austria is
apparently dropping out of the fray, and the
empire of the llapsburgs looks to be broken
for ever. This collapse of his neighbor
leaves the Kaiser dejectedly contemplating a
heap of ashes and multitudinous cemeteries.
Talk about the power of the German to
resist invasion and the terrific price the
Allies would have to pay for breaking the
power of the German militarists is obvious
nonsense. The German can resist invasion
only by making it unnecessary. The mili-
tarism of Prussia will be broken by the
Germans themselves. They are smashing
it by degrees already. After getting a
little more encouragement to keep busy there
is nothing more certain than that they will
finish the job, and quickly, too. The Ger-
man is in all things a realist. He knows
what war is—or at any rate what war can
be. He knows what peace is—what Im-
perialists and Jingoes and Kaisers are —and
knows that fighting a losing battle for
Kultur and the House of llohenzollern
and all that these stand for can be taken off
the programme with great advantage to
himself and other people. And because he
knows this he can be depended on to make the
best of a bad job with just as great expedi-
tion as he would have forced the rest of
the world to take the worst of a job of
another sort.

Why Hock Up Scientific Discoveries?
Are there any possible combinations of cir-
cumstances which could justify the State
withholding from the world a scientific fact
relating to preventive medicine? If there
are, quite a lot of people would like to know
what the combinations happen to be. Also,
they would like to know whether the Federal
Institute of Scientific Research proposes to
keep secret any or all of its discoveries or to
go in for compounding patent or unpatented
medicines and blight specifics and vermin
eradicators. Curiosity on these subjects has
been provoked by the extraordinary position
the N. S. Wales Government has been getting
in connection with the McGarvie Smith
vaccine for the prevention of anthrax in
sheep. Under the will of McG. Smith,
lately deceased, the State was made a sort of
partner with the Smith Vaccine Institute.
It appeared as if Smith had made a present
of his vaccine formula to the State. But it
transpired afterwards that there was a string
to the bequest or a fly in the ointment, or
something. Anyway, other people repre-
sented by the Board of Management of
the Institute (i.e., the vaccine factory) raised
objections to the State getting a sight of the
vaccine formula, and rushed the document
into a bank for safe keeping. All this appa-
rently was a proper proceeding, viewed in the
terms of Smith’s bequest. The result of it
was that the State had a share in a docu-
ment concealed in a bank safe, but couldn’t say
what was in the document—and the Smith
Institute had another share in the docu-
•ment, and knew all about the contents. The
suggestion then seemed to be that the Insti-
tute _ should go on preparing and selling
vaccine under “Government auspices.” After
prolonged and mysterious haggling this was
modified to the extent that the Institute direc-
torate, noticing that the Government was
being handed another process of making-
vaccine, offered to confide the Smith
formula to a Government bacteriologist on
condition of getting sole rights of producing
the germ annihilator under the aforesaid
Government auspices. It was represented
that there would be something “most injudi-
cious” in having two dopes on the market, as
this would "seriously affect the institute’s
affairs.” This would 'make it look as if the
McG. Smith bequest, for which he received
much posthumous applause, was a mere
device to get the State in as a sleep-
ing partner to sustain a monopoly.
Either the State did or did not re-
ceive from Smith, deceased, as a testa-
mentary bequest the anthrax vaccine for-
mula or a share therein. If the State did,
then its clear business is to treat that
bequest in the only way by which science is
distinguished from huckstering. If it did not,
or if the bequest involved a half-and-half sort
of deal, then the State’s clear duty is to
dissociate itself from an entanglement which
can bring it neither credit nor desirable pro-
tit. Should it be necessary in this case to
buy anyone out, or otherwise reconcile the
spirit of Mammon with that of Pasteur,
the thing should be examined on its merits.
The present position is preposterous, even
offensive. If McG. Smith had discovered
a vaccine for the prevention of Spanish ’flu’,
or tubercular disease of the lungs, or pene-
tration of the tissue by cancer, or leprosy, or
even for excessive agitation or the jaw, and'
if Smith had bequeathed the mystery of pre-
paration on whole or in part to the State,
projects to keep Science in the dark and run
the commodity after the manner of a proprie-
tary pill would not provoke much applause.
A Sarah Gamp in Armor.
Germany has now set up a Responsible Gov-
ernment after the British and Australian
fashion, and everything in the garden is
democratic and lovely. The Imperial Reich-
stag (House of Representatives), which runs
the show on paper just like our Reps., has
always been a manhood-suffrage body with
the secret ballot and other fixings; in fact,
the Hun had manhood suffrage before Aus-
tralia had it. Certainly Ministers were ap-
pointed by the ruler, as in the United States;
but this is regarded in the U.S. as rather
democratic than otherwise, for though Con-
gress has full power of legislation and con-
trols Ministers, they haven’t to waste their
time in the House answering fool questions,
and they don't make corrupt bargains with
middle parties in order to buy or keep then-
portfolios. However, the Hun reports that
he has adopted the British-French-Italian
custom of making Ministers sit in Parlia-
ment, and he assumes that this is a gigantic
reform or concession, and that there is now
a new, humane, reliable and indubitable Ger-
many, with which the Allies will naturally
treat on easy terms. In his own bland
fashion he proposed an armistice, or spell
of rest and deep thought, which wasn’t
necessarily to include an abandonment of the
foreign territory which the Hun occupies,
though if he abandoned part of it volun-
tarily and thereby got some of his armies out
of a tight, place, that would redound all the
more to his credit and show his reasonable-
ness. The spell of meditation wasn’t even
to include of necessity a complete abandon-
ment of submarine butchery, for it was care-
fully explained that some of the submarine
commanders might by accident not receive
news of the arrangement and might still
butcher. But it was suggested that the
truce should be based on the existing strength
of armies-—in other words, that no addition
should be made to the forces on either side.
The United States was to cease sending
250,000 or 350,000 able-bodied men to the
Front every month (a matter which could
easily be verified), and the Hun wasn’t to
put in training the new year’s crop of school-
boys (a matter which couldn’t be very well
verified). As a lavish return for these
arrangements the German people were in
future to speak through a one-man-one-vote
Parliament (as they have always done) and
a Responsible Ministry (which the Kaiser
can dismiss at any moment, just as George
Y. can—the difference being that the Germans
are accustomed to that sort of thing and the
British are not). It is a curious appeal to
the folk who pin their faith to written guar-
antees and scraps of paper. There is some-
thing grotesque in the old Hun gang, with its
uniform and its spiked helmet, assuring the
world that there has been a silent but com-
plete revolution over-night, and a new nation
with new ideals was born in the small hours
of morning with an armed Sarah Gamp as
midwife, and that they—the aristocrats with
uniforms and spiked belltoppers—are in per-
manent eclipse. It is almost as striking as
an Australian sinking fund, or as an Auditor-
General’s report regarded as a check on the
finances. Plain people in plain times have
sent the heads of the old Government to the
enemy in bags to prove that its eclipse was
really permanent and that the new Govern-
ment . was a genuine affair with power to
negotiate. Something of the sort is required
in the present instance.

“Ted C.” calls for cheers or something:—
The Military mind does move in a weird and won-
drous way. 0082 Pte. W. Blank, a young pommey
in the A.1.F., was invalided to England from France
18 months ago. In January, 1918, he was classified
C 3, which means unfit for all military duty and
due for discharge. Pending return to Australia he
was given a mild job at A.I.F. Headquarters, London.
Now, Blank’s father was a well-to-do landholder,
and, besides 0082, had two sons in the Tommy Army
and one in the Maoriland Forces. Owing to lack of
assistance he was much hampered in the management
of his business, and when news of son No. 0082’s
medical classification reached him he urged Blank
to seize his discharge with both hands and to get
released in England, as others had done. But when
the young man asked for his paper he came the pro-
verbial stummicker, and was told he’d have to get
it in Australia. He urged that he had no wives,
families or other ties in Australia, nor was any
job being held open for him there. He also pleaded
that his people lived in England and that they had
offered him profitable employment at work indirectly
of national importance. No use. Then dad
came to London, and stayed five days awaiting mili-
tary verdicts on. various points raised by himself. He
urged that his son was no longer of use to the
military, had no work to go to in Australia, so what
was wrong with releasing him in England, and
saving the expense of transporting him across the
world? Both dad and son, growing desperate, offered
that the latter would surrender all his deferred pay
for three and a half years’ service, and even relin-
-quish his right to the incapacitation pension awarded
him for the rest of his life! No use again; 0082
Blank would have to go to Australia before he could
be discharged. That’s as far as the story has gone
at time of writing, except that 0082 has since been
re-classified Cl, which permits him to be nominally
fit enough to remain in England on a Headquarters
job. But 0082 merely wonders what the use of
that is. And dad is dazedly trying to gauge what
sort of an institution it is that refuses a common-
sense bargain. Comparing this action with
the Government’s openhandedness in the matter of
despatching to Australia wives and fiancees, alleged
and otherwise; also, the casual way in which hun-
dreds of men have been enlisted, clothed, fed, paid
and transported from Australia to this country at a
great expense, only to be adjudged totally unfit for
soldiering when they arrive here, and then have to be
shipped back again, it makes me mournfully suspect
that military mental-balance is. not all it’s cracked
up to be by those who are handicapped with it.
“Pungawerewere” : One of the most bril-
liant surgeons in Maoriland, Dr. Cahill, of
Wellington, died a few days ago, and by his
death the poor of that city have lost a real
friend. “Tommy” Cahill, as he was affec-
tionately called, would give you the hat off
his head. His generosity, in the course of
a busy career as a medical man, was a
standing lesson to all men. Born and edu-
cated in Ireland, where he took his M.I).
degree, he came to Australia in 1844 when
a young man of 25, but went across from
Melbourne to Wellington, where he settled.
He had all his countrymen’s love of a horse,
and it was an unknown race-meeting around
Wellington at which he was not present.
Cahill was recovering after an operation on
the throat when his heart gave out.
“Figurehead” : Unless the ancient mariner
glued his telescope on to the buxom lass with
her left hand pointing the way from the bow-
sprit, and still as good as new, he would hardly
recognise in the Portuguese barquentine-
rigged Ferreira of to-day, the famous clipper
Cutty Sark, designed and fitted by Captain
John Moodie (who still thrives at Auchter-
muchty in his 93rd year) In 1869. In May,
1916, she was towed into Capetown Harbor
after being dismasted off Cape Agulhas on a
voyage from Delagoa Bay to Mossamedes
with black diamonds. No sticks could be got
for her till this year. During the long lay-up
old shellbacks boarded and wrangled over the
hoary-headed question as to whether the
Cutty Sark or the Thermopylae had been
the speediest. On one occasion an excited
inatelot threatened to finish the wordy war-
fare with a stiletto. He wound up the assem-
bly by hissing: “Whatta for a Thermopylae
nowa coala hulka if shea betta thana
Cutta Sark—eh ?” The Sark in 1875-76 estab-
lished a record run of 55 days between
London and Melbourne, and was actually
within 50 miles of Melbourne on the 50th
day out. The record has not been lowered
since. Others that go near it are a number
of Thermopylae flights, the voyage of the
Decapolis from London to Launceston in 56
days in 1869, and that of the Commonwealth
in 57 days in ’76. In the Sark’s record trip
the Line was crossed in 15 days, and 2163
miles of easting down was trampled in six
days, with one day’s run of 363 knots, and
the tally of the other five not far in arrears
of it. _ The Yankee hookers Flying Cloud,
Sovereign of the Seas, Lightning, and the
Liverpool-owned Marco Polo have each
recorded single day runs of over 400 knots.
The Sovereign of the Seas had a quota for
a day of 419 miles. That was on a voyage
from Sandwich Islands to New York, when
she covered a distance of 17.597 miles in
1896 consecutive hours —or just under 10
knots right through—precisely the same speed
as Billy Hughes’s first wooden packet for
Australia, the Cethana, accomplished on her
madden voyage, with the aid of the latest oil-
engines and the fruits of 60 years’ engineer-
ing and shipbuilding progress to work on.
There is a lot to be said for the clippers even
yet.

The Melba-Verbrugghen concert in the
Town Hall (Sydney) was a great achievement,
and the huge audience got an artistic treat
it will long remember. Melba’s voice was
fresh, pure and limpid in its silvery clarity,
and showed no trace of the passing years in
its girlish sweetness. There may be a trifle
less volume in the lower register—or it
seemed so from a seat that overhung the
full blast of musical instruments —when the
orchestra accompanied the great soprano in
the early part of the programme. If this
were so, it was not apparent later, when
Melba sang, with the piano only, a group
of chansons. These, exquisitely portrayed in
every light and shade of which the vocal
chords are capable, roused the audience to
frantic applause. In the beautiful “Chan-
son Indoue” the great singer gave of her
best (as she always does), but added an
elusive charm to the words difficult to de-
scribe. Even the well-worn Tosti’s “Good-
bye,” in response to request, took on a new
life when she breathed upon its dry bones.
Verbrugghen and his orchestra were strong
in a magnificent programme. John Lem-
mone was recalled for a brilliant perform-
ance, in which his flute caught the fairy
delicacy of a “Melody” by Gluck. A new
Belgian pianist, Francis de Bourguignon.
immediately won public favor. The Lord
Mayor’s Fund for soldiers’ dependants ben-
efited by about £l5OO.

“The Great Divide,” at the Criterion
(Sydney) since Saturday, is new to Aus-
tralia as a “live” drama, though the writer
has a vague recollection of having noticed
the play per film. The piece was a great suc-
cess in America, and it is not difficult to see
why this should have been the case. It is
very American in setting, in sentiment and
in characterisation. And because of this, and
perhaps because of its artificiality, it is easy
to imagine “The Great Divide” making a
strong appeal to people who are able to appre-
ciate the local color. But the very reasons
which made a success in YVilsonland are
just those which may be found to limit its
staying power in Australia. Judging by the
shoes worn by the ladies and the cut of the
heroine’s skirt and blouse it appears that the
proceedings may have taken place last week.

Now, if Arizona is
still in the habit of
capturing a wife
by force in the
middle of the night
from a lonely home-
stead, it would
seem as if there is
still a lot for
Uncle Sam to do
before Arizona is
safe for democracy
and experiments in
land settlement. It
is just there that
the Australian
audience finds its
sense of probabil-
