possibility, in vio-
lent collision with
the preliminary
episode. Ruth Jor-
dan, living with her
brother on a re-
mote selection, is
left on the premises
alone one night.
Three picturesque
males burst into
the humpy and pro-
pose a game of Yan-
kee grab with Ruth
as the prize. The terrified girl appeals to the
least tough-looking of the trio to save her
and expresses her readiness to pay him his
price. He buys off one of his mates, shoots
the other, takes possession of Ruth and de-
parts with her towards the distant peaks of
the Cordilleras. It transpires later that on
the way thither they were married. But six
months thence, though her husband has dis-
covered a rich deposit of gold, Ruth is living
in misery in a shack among the mountains,
with a great loathing for her proprietor and
much anxiety to see him cleansed of his
wickedness. Quite by accident her brother

The dialogue incidental to the three acts of
“The Great Divide” is wholly commonplace.
In their most eloquent moments the inhabi-
tants of Arizona and the Cordilleras and Mil-
ford Corners (Mass.) talk the most amazing
fustian without the slightest provocation.
* * * *
“The Great Divide” is almost entirely for
Muriel Starr and Frank Harvey—the one
as Ruth Jordan and the other her over-
powering husband, Stephen Ghent. They
both act well, though the opportunities for
Stephen are much more numerous than for
Ruth. The actress seemed to be rather be-
wildered when The Bulletin attended the
show —to be not quite sure of what it was
all about. Harvey, as the strong-willed, hard-
living Westerner, cuts a fine figure, and
though the performance is entirely a theatri-
cal sequence of posing and rant, he manages it
with a fine semblance of human dignity. The
rest of the cast merely happen to come into
the story. As Polly Jordan, the wife of
Ruth’s brother, Miss Norma Mitchell is one
of the few bright spots. Polly’s femininity
is of that self-conscious kind which makes
the whole world sin. The sententious old
party who sits smoking outside Ruth’s shack
on the roof of the world is the other bright
spot.
Melodious “Katinka,” w’ith its everlasting
“Rackety Coo,” keeps tired people from
thinking too much and lazy people from
thinking at all at Her Majesty’s (Sydney).
It’s still Emelie Polini and “The Eyes of
Youth” at the Palace -( Sydney). The noble
Indian yogi who supplies the visions per-
spires beneath his stage tan these close even-
ings ; but the smart scene-shifting staff who
actually work the changes sweat unseen.
“Pierrot Pie No. 7” will be opened by
Sydney James at the Playhouse (Sydney)
on Friday. It is understood to be a strongly
patriotic dish.

“Honi Soit,” with Barry Lupino flickering-
in and out, is still keeping penury from the
door of the Tivoli (Sydney).
At the Crystal Palace (Sydney) “The
Better ’Ole,” Bruce Bairnsfather’s illustra-
tions of the doings of Old Bill, Alf and Bert,
is holding audiences this week with its
simple humor and pathos. The story cen-
tres round Old Bill’s discovery of an enemy
plan. Single-handed he frustrates that little
bit of Hun devilry, and returns to Blighty
with a decoration and a salutation from the
French Commander. At the finish all his
sorrows are forgotten when his missus pre-
sents him with a snug little pub, where he
can find refreshments after the heat and
thirst of active service. The film corpora-
tions have evidently bought up some hotel
property, for they have presented Mae
Marsh also with a grog-shop. Mae
is working in a doll factory when she hears
the glad tidings, but her ardor is damped to
find her bit of real estate is not in Respect-
able-street. However, she has a big heart,_ so
she sets to work to clean up the reputation
of the pub and the locality, and when she
marries the village lawyer you know that
she’s fairly on the job. The name of the
story is “AH Woman,” and its address is the
Strand. At Hoyt’s and the Ilaymarket,
“Let’s Get a Divorce” gives Billie Burke
great opportunities in the part of a whimsi-
cal, romantic young wife. She decides that
there aren’t enough thrills in matrimony, so
she sets off for Reno, where you can get a
divorce with a pound of tea. However, on
the way she thinks better of it, and returns
to her husband, who provides her with board
and lodging and a free pardon. Mary Bick-
ford is still a feature at the Lyceum, and
each visit to “Stella Maris” deepens the im-
pression made by her fine acting. “Ace
High” is the supporting yarn at the same
address. It shows Tom Mix in outdoor
sports amid wild and untameable scenery.
This (Thursday) afternoon, .T. and N.
Tait ring up the curtain on their first Reper-
tory production at the Palace (Sydney).
Producer Gregan McMahon is starting off
with Shaw’s “The Doctor’s Dilemma.”
“Pershing’s Crusaders,” given its first
Australian run at West’s (Adelaide), is the
new U.S.A. official war-film. It might have
been called “America Prepared,” for, after
a symbolical picture or two linking up the
present anti-Hun expedition with the old
anti-infidel crusades, it gets down to a repre-
sentation of what U.S. is doing to make
things easier for the Allies and more un-
pleasant for the enemy. It should make
Australia blush to see how ship-build-
ing is tackled. Everything is on the big
scale, even to the electric knife which cuts
about 60 thicknesses of khaki uniform much
as one cuts butter. Later pictures show the
U.S. boys fraternising with the populace of
Franee; but, like most other war-pictures,
these stop just where one wants them to be-
gin. The nearest they get to the actual
fight is a row of sulky prisoners—the first
that Uncle Sam bagged. The main value of
the film is the moral to be learned from the
sight of a whole nation in dead earnest about
the war. The Royal (which is just getting
back to musical comedy with “Oh, Boy”)
has had IJ. B. Irving giving effectively his
double part in “The Lyons Mail” with larger
effects (outdoor) than he was able to get on
the stage. At Wondergraph, Enid Bennett
shows what a very mild sample of naughti-
ness is enough to shock an American back-
block village, and Hayakawa puts up (in
a strong story) a fairly effective bluff at
being an African native. Leila Doubleday
is at the Town Hall, the Anderson co. at the
Tivoli is showing how Girls are brought to
Ruin (with a special request that every
mother and father shall come to learn), and
at the Majestic comedian Paul Stanhope
continues to lead a bright show, in which
the singing of the Grafters is conspicuous.
* :!:
Deprived of its glitter and girl, “Hello,
Everybody,” at Her Majesty’s (Melb.),
seems the hollo west apology of an entertain-
ment. There are altogether 17 scenes, and
the bulk of them are either scraps of rough-
and-tumble humor or are expensively em-
broidered backgrounds for unimpressive
songs. The music throughout is tinkly,
trite and tiresome. One or two of the items
are positively stupid, and emphasis is given
to their quality by the inability of singers
like Minnie Love and Nellie Payne to
warble them with any degree of comfort.
On the male side the vocal performance is
better, Cannot, at any rate, improving his
numbers by his impromptu “business” and
infectious bonhomie. Whether as the bogus
King of Neutralia, the dishonored husband
of the Stone Age, or any other of his half-
dozen characters, he often changes a
threatening yawn to a laugh by his mental
alertness and irresistible geniality. Clyde
Cook and George Welch are minor humor-
ists with greater physical resources, but of
more negligible personality. The value of
their effort is comparatively small. Minnie
Love is dressed in the wildest fancies of a
futurist clothes-artist. But that is Minnie’s
sole distinction. Pearl Ladd and Villiers
Arnold are more competent singers with
fewer opportunities, and Sydney Yates is a
useful auxiliary as a comedian and dancer.
He is the festive partner of Maggie Dickinson
in an Oriental feature, “Sesame,” wherein
Maud Arnery introduces a slim and shapely
leg. Maggie appears earlier as the presu-
mable spirit of agriculture in an ingeniously
arranged “wheat scene.” Also, she is the
personified vision of a Red Injun romancer
in the imaginative first act finale, sumptu-
ously illustrated by the Falls of Niagara
and a gaudy Red Skin chorus. All the poetry
contributed to the handsome picture comes
from her poised and perfect toes. None of
the foregoing, however, may deceive them-
selves that they are the revelation in this
, “revelation in revues.” It is the sustained
cleverness of the chorus and ballet, dancing
and moving with unanimous decision in
scenes like the. “Oriental Nights” and the
charming “Gathering of the Clans,” that is
the lovely surprise the entertainment affords.
The cumulative effect of the brilliancy and
beauty of these girls is a double-distilled
joy in comparison with the weakness revealed
in some of the leads.
Potash and Perl-mutter are still success-
fully dodging insolvency in “Business Before
Pleasure” at the Royal (Melb.). J. C. Wil-
liamson Ltd. declare that the partners pro-
duce GO laughs a minute, but as some of
their jokes often require five minutes for
solution, the management’s statement can be
accepted with caution. One night last
week, at any rate, the claim seemed absurdly
hyperbolical. Abe and Mawruss were not
averaging more than five laughs to the hour,
and they probably felt, like the writer, that
the theatre had been engaged by the Cale-
donian Society.
“Mum Dawson, Boss” having failed to
touch the popular fancy, Bert Bailey has
revived “Gran’dad Rudd” for the last week
of the season at the King’s (Melb.). Gran’-
dad is the despot of “On Our Selection,”
with 20 years of accumulated bitterness to
work off and a more rudimentary view of
domestic justice and good feeling. In the
present spasm of his history his factious ill-
nature produces heartless reprisals, and he
is the irreverent butt of a family that never
was strong in filial devotion. Also his age
has affected his judgment and, to gratify his
overweening autocracy, he is now ready to
desert the cause of unhappy virtue and
marry his granddaughter to the first fluent
miscreant w T ho “chews his ear.” Moreover,
his blessed descendants are willing to support
the outrage and dance at the wedding, which
shows a weakness of moral stamina not sus-
pected of the Rudds of 20 years before.
There are changes, mostly errors, in the cast,
but the aggregate shortage of talent is not
conspicuous enough to invite a shower of
bricks. To-morrow the Rudd family re-
treats from the King’s, and on Saturday
Allen Doone will tread the ould boards in
“Lucky O’Shea.”

Mary Pickford is the week’s sunbeam on
the De Luxe and Lyceum screens (Melb.).
She is the joyous “Amarilly of Clothesline
Alley,” and she has the lead throughout five
acts of the cleanest and cutest comedy
drama. .Jewel Carmen follows close on the
track of Mary as the fascinating little fraud
in “The Bride of Fear.” Hoyt’s also have
“The Country Boy” to warn good young men
that woman and wine are worse together than
singly taken neat. “The House of Glass,’’staged
at the Royal (Melb.) a while ago, is now a
film feature, with Clara Kimball Young m
the principal part. The picture was un-
rolled at the Majestic (Melb.) on Saturday,
and the silent agony of Clara in her endeavor
to keep her past from her railroad president
lifted her another mile in the opinion ot
movie connoisseurs. Zoe Rae, a clever
juvenile, supports Clara in a five-act comedy
drama, “The Magic Eye.” Also views of the
hills around Warburton, Healesville and
Marysville (Vic.) add some- welcome Aus-
tralian scenery to the largely foreign pro-
gramme.
Two side-shows :
Rumors of an approaching dress shortage are
confirmed by the Norman Lindsay exhibition at the
Fine Art Society, Alfred Place, Melbourne. The
various damsels scattered about can hardly muster
one garment between them. They are the latest
Lindsay wenches modelled to a gracious slenderness
of flank, a fine bust, and a delicious piquancy in
facial design. For the most part they are ignorant
of corsets and the Nonconformist conscience, and care
only to idle in their natural state in an Eden of
purple evenings and golden afternoons. Yellow and
blue and crimson are most frequently combined to
produce the necessary environment of rich and pagan
ease. One must frankly admire these nudes for their
irresponsibility and their halt-evasive charm, they
arrest attention in a number of gorgeous color-
decorations. They challenge the eye and abide in the
memory when robbed of their alluring pinkness by
Indian-ink. Also, they acquire a strange fascination
in the etchings like “Femininity” and “Ballet
Entrance,” in which Lindsay has found a new ecstasy
of expression.
Some 70 pictures by a new artist, Marion Jones, at
the Athenaeum, Melbourne, show the effect of a
National Gallery training. The large subject, “For-
tunes,” allegedly painted for the Travelling Scholar-
ship, reveals the sound drawing and unimaginative
vision that are pre-requisites for the capture of three
years abroad. Miss Jones has thoroughly absorbed
the artistic principles that often remain rigid and
unaffected through a lifetime of stolid effort. Her
regiment of portraits in oils shows the same staid and
conventional painting of unimportant people that in
England leads to extinction in the Royal Academy.
Miss Jones does better in pastels. Some of her
studies in this medium are handled with liveliness
and freedom.
Adelaide has discovered a remarkable child.
He managed to get enrolled before he was
15 years old, and saw two years’ active ser-
vice in Egypt and France before being dis-
charged as too young to enlist. Last week
he was charged in an Adelaide police-court
with assaulting the military police, but his
lawyer claimed that the veteran warrior was
of too tender years to be tried anywhere but
in the Children’s Court! So the scene was
changed, and the child was fined £2 for
“language” and violent behavior.
From the desert:—
Since the French occupation in 1830 East and
West have been fighting in Algiers. I was told (by
a Frenchman) that the West had indisputably won ;
I have been told that of many places, and I have
never believed it. The French Algiers is still the
Arab El Djezair. In the Rue de Rovigo and
on the quay side one is in a rather dan-
gerous and raffish quarter of some squalid
African Montemarte at midnight. Very high
lights; frocks in shops (the last gasp of chic);
trams, motors; people in cabs—all very gay; heaps
of distraction of all kinds, legal and illegal.
Out of earshot of this gaiety little rocky moun-
tainous streets and stone stairways creep precipi-
tously up to the Casbah, the great fortress on the
hill, through the Arab quarter. Grass and moss grow
between the pavement-stones and on the stairways,
and lichen dots the Casbah walls. The stars and a
few swinging oil-lamps light the cloistered fountained
courtyards, the flowers and the dark palms. The
tiled or marble patio the Spaniard inherited from
his Arab father is a great possession—he has a
garden of silence, broken only by the whispering
water-jet, always in his house. In the Arab city of Eli
Djezair the West watches the cool oasis, the imme-
morial peace and dark quiet—from outside the gate.
Diagonal cedar-wood poles support the overhang-
ing upper storeys of the houses, like multitudes of
gallows’ brackets. The stucco of the silent walls
has the same luminous surface as that of the Alham-
bra ; only an Arab plasterer can make his walls
shine in the dark. A few Arabs make a lisping,
slippered way through the alley; they stop and stare
at me (the conquering West) and go their way in
silent contempt. The tiny mosque they pass below
the walls is always full of women praying for babies
—Eastern babies. Outside, the Sahara heaves and
burns; past Karouan the Sacred City, and Sidi
Okbar’s onyx and porphyry, out to Morocco.
El Biar, the Rose Bay of Algiers, is Western. On
the shore of Mustapha SupSricur, it looks across the
lake, from its gardens of olives and almonds and
weeping pepper-trees and sycamores, to the
snow mountains of Kabylia, over the factories
and smoke and dirt which pay its Monte Carlo rents.
The suburb is dainty and French. Kept like an
English park or a boudoir. Tobacco fields in the
interior and roulette tables (and so on) in Algiers
help to keep it. And the Rue de Rovigo screams
raucously that all the world is Western. But the
mighty Casbah on the hill and the Arab town is
silent —silent.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Queensland State Government Insur-
ance Office. —Tlie managements of most
insurance cos. throughout the Common-
wealth are watching the growth of tins
young Sosli tiger with some concern as to
what is going to happen later on. The cos.
operating in the Northern State received
their lirst bad knock when the Labor Gov-
ernment gave the State office, a monopoly ot
all the workmen’s compensation business. of
the State. Certainly under the Act which
made workmen’s compensation insurance
compulsory it was provided that any co.
which was “approved” by the Governor-in-
Council could do business, but none of the
private cos. was approved, and the Privy
Council when appealed to upheld the Gov-
ernment’s action. The State office is still
in its infancy, but even the happenings of
the past two years threaten to affect the
future of insurance cos. not only in Banana-
land but throughout the Commonwealth. For
it is certain that if this Slate enterprise is a
success—that is, if it can considerably
cheapen the cost of insurance—the other
States are certain sooner or later to estab-
lish insurance departments. The Queens-
land office is not confined to workmen’s com-
pensation insurance, and is now doing fire,
life, annuity and miscellaneous accident busi-
ness. Insurance Commissioner John Good-
wyn has now presented his second annual
report, and it is as voluminous and informa-
tive as its predecessor.
Workers’ Compensation Department.
Owing to the fact that insurance is com-
pulsory and that the State office has a com-
plete monopoly of the business, this branch
is a very big affair. The results for the year
work out this way :
Premiums .. .. .. ..£242,633
Interest, etc. , . . .. .. 5,766
£248.399
Claims £107,078
Provision for unadjusted claims.. 44,637
Provision for claims not notified
at June. 30 .. .. .. 10,000
Expenses .. .. .. .. 27,789
189,504
Surplus .. .. £58,895
The year’s claims are given as £132,277, but
these figures include £33,403 of 191.7 claims
adjusted after the accounts had. been made
up. For outstanding and unnotified claims
£25,199 had been set aside. Thus the net
claims (including what is reckoned a liberal
allowance for outstanding claims) charged
against the 1918 revenue amount to £161,715.
Working on these figures gives a claim
ratio of 66.65 p.c. of the premiums received.
It was an unfavorable year; cyclones and
floods alone being responsible for 13 fatal
accidents. Though the department has not
quite got into its stride yet, . because
it does not have to pay commission or
taxation .expenses absorbed only 11.45 p.c.
of the premiums, but the Commissioner hopes
to further reduce this proportion by getting
power to charge dilatory employers with the
cost of pursuing them for returns and pre-
miums. After making allowances for bad
debts, etc., and including the previous year’s
carry forward there was £63,389 to be appro-
priated in accordance with the Act. The
premiums charged for ordinary risks “were
in all cases the published net rates of the
Accident Underwriters’ Association of
Queensland under the Act of 1905.” Policy-
holders received a 10 p.c. bonus absorbing
£22,000, and the household workers’ compen-
sation policy-holders got a 50 p.c. bonus
(£1030). General reserves are strengthened
by £30,000.
As to the results this department has so far
achieved, allow the Commissioner to put it
his own way :
By the granting of a bonus of 10 p.c. the rates
for insurance under the new Act have been substan-
tially reduced below the rates charged by the cos. for
the insurance of the much lower benefits of the pre-
vious Act.
During the year statistics have been compiled
which will enable me very shortly to make recom-
mendations for the revision of existing rates in
accordance with our actual claim experience. While
the bonus system was the only safe method in the
early stages. . . .unless accompanied by frequent rate
revision it is manifestly unfair to policy-holders, who
receive the same rate of bonus in all classes quite
irrespective of the adequacy or otherwise of the
rates paid....l propose to recommend the adoption
of a system of net rating.
One of the notable features of the Queens-
land Act is the provision for compensation
for disablement arising from miners’ phthisis
and certain industrial diseases. This busi-
ness is still in the experimental stage, and
though the present premiums (a maximum of
40s. p.c. for underground miners) and the
Government subsidy have left a small margin
after meeting claims and paying exes, a
revision of the rates may be necessary later
on. Net premiums last year were £20,375,
and claims £17,697 and exes. £2919. The
business done in this section is, as the Com-
missioner puts it, “entirely of a co-operative
nature, the Office merely acting as an inter-
mediary, paying compensation on the one
hand and assessing employers’ premiums and
Government contributions on the other.”
Fike Department.
At June 30 this department had been
established 17 months. Gross premiums for
the past 12 months amounted to £48,908, and
net premiums (i.e., after deducting reinsur-
ances and return premiums) £30,004. Net
claims amounted to £3353 —11.17 p.c. of the
net premiums. This low loss rate is due
partly to the fact that a very big proportion
of the policies cover dwelling risks, and also
to the fact that a lot of the business was
acquired late in the financial year. Ex-
penses, including commissions, taxation and
fire-brigade charges, together with adminis-
tration charges, absorbed 46.78 p.c. of the net
premiums. “This rate.” says the Commis-
sioner, “should decrease as the proportion of
renewal premiums to the whole increases”
—21,128 new policies were issued last year.
Of the year’s surplus £12,000 has been trans-
ferred to an unearned premium reserve.
There have been two reductions of rates since
this department started, the total reductions
on dwellings being 33$ p.c. and on business
risks 20 p.c. It is estimated that these reduc-
tions mean a saving of at least £140,000 to
the public.
Life and ivlxscellaneous Accident
Departments.
The Life branch had been operating only
six months when the accounts were made up.
This Life business will be one of the Office’s
most interesting activities, but so far it has
not been advanced to a stage on which any
comment is of much value. “The policy con-
ditions and rates,” savs the Commissioner,
“especially for non-profit insurance, compare
favorably with those of other offices.” The
first valuation is to be made as at December
31. 1920, and thereafter an annual valuation
will decide the division of sui’plus pi’ofits, all
of which “will belong absolutely to policy-
holders.” The Miscellaneous Accident branch,
covering personal accident and disease insur-
ance, guarantee, plate-glass risks, etc., is so
far only a small affair. Net premiums last
year amounted to £4112, claims £324, and ex-
penses £1485. Of the surplus an amount
representing 40 p.c. of the net premiums has
been reserved. The premiums, by the way,
have been reduced 20 p.c. on old rates, re-
sulting in an estimated saving of £20,000 to
the public.
Under the Act £lOO,OOO was appropriated
to establish the State Insurance Office. Rut
all the Commissioner required was a tem-
porary loan of £3571, which was repaid
almost immediately. The balance-sheet shows
general reserves of £58,020. Total assets
now stand at £200,126, £101,278 in public
securities, £65,331 in cash, £26,415 due by
sundry creditors, £6964 in motor-cars, furni-
ture, etc.

Internal reserves disclosed, £90,000 . . 630,699
Reserves capitalised, £120,000 .. 510,699
This co. has had three gorgeous years. Pro-
fits are shown after amounts have been
written off securities, premises, etc., and last
year £14,520 was written off premises alone,
compared with £9761 for 1917 and £8604 in
1916, while, no doubt, there have been pro-
visions for contingencies which do not show
up in the balance-sheet. During the past
two years £200,000 of uncalled capital has
been got in, and the growth of income, less
provision for depreciation of securities and
premises, etc., has been as follows: 1916,
£231,059; 1917, £259,579; and 1918,
£318,179. Some of our products have been
disposed of at hitherto undreamt-of prices,
and the higher prices go the more Elder,
Smith’s get in commissions. But apart from
the handsome profits shown recently, this
year's accounts provide further reason for
shareholders to applaud. This is the dis-
closing of £90,000 of internal reserves. “Some
accounts that had been regarded as rather
unsatisfactory,” explained the chairman,
“had now been made good or been realised
.... so £90,000 had been introduced into the
P. and L. account from provisions that ex-
isted prior to June 30, 1914.” This amount,
together with £30,000 of undivided profit, is
being transferred to capital, which will then
stand at £720,000.
Since the war started the co. has been
piling up liquid assets. This shows how the
balance-sheet figures have moved during re-

Liabilities have increased by £432,773 since
1914, and £200,000 of new capital has been
got in; total, £632,773. In the same time
no less than £735,160 has been added to War
Loan and other Government securities,
deposits and cash. There are now liquid
assets amounting to £1,417,050 and liabilities
of £2,093,342. To meet the £676,286 of
liabilities not represented by liquid assets
there are the advances of £1.738,905 and
property, etc., £210,080. Shareholders have
done very well; since 1905 they have received
a 10 p.c. dividend each year, and on every
occasion there has been a substantial addi-
tion to visible reserves. After paying divi-
dends totalling £190,751 for the four war
years, no less than £287,503 has been added
to reserves, including the £90,000 recovered.
To what extent the management has been
providing for contingencies lately nobody but
those inside know, but in view of the abnor-
mal conditions the management admits that
it has been proceeding with extreme caution.
The fact that at this stage it can bring
£90,000 into the accounts indicates at least
that the management is thoroughly satisfied
with the position. A glance at the 1901
balance-sheet figures given above indicates the
co.’s rapid growth. It is still growing, and
lately the Elder, Shenton business in AVest-
ralia, in which the co. held a controlling in-
terest, was absorbed. The co. is now estab-
lished in every mainland State.

The “profits on property sales” column of
that table is an echo of the big smash. In
1900 the co. held a post-mortem on the pro-
perties which had been thrown on its hands,
and when it revalued its assets a considerable
proportion of the capital was found to repre-
sent nothing more than the hot-air of the
boom days. So assets were written down
and the capital was reduced to correspond.
Later on, however, as some of these proper-
ties were unloaded, they returned more than
their book values, and these amounts were
added to shareholders’ funds. This compari-
son shows how the business has been built
up since just after the clean-up:—

A very profitable half-year; and all the profit
is not disclosed, for the figures are shown
“after writing off all bad debts, the usual
depreciation, making liberal provision for
doubtful debts and placing ‘a bit up. the
sleeve,’ ” as the directors put it. The results
are very fine in view of the war difficulties
which, particularly in its carrying depart-
ment, the co. has to overcome. Inter-State
shipping is more scarce than ever, and a co.
like this, which takes delivery of goods for
clients and then has to chase round for cargo
space, has a strenuous time nowadays. But
besides being carriers and forwarding agents
tbe co. does a big produce and station-supply
business. In Victoria it is represented by
the McCulloch Carrying Co., Wright, Heaton
and Co. holding all tbe shares. There was
a time when this old Vic. concern was in a
bad way—. c lost half of its capital after a
big drought Nowadays, however, it is earn-
ing good profits for Wright, Heaton’s. Also,
tbe co. is interested in the Murray River
Saw Mills Co., which is also adding its bit of
profit.

The co. is in a very strong position, and
though there has been a big increase in the
price of commodities, the management has
been able to finance the business without
going outside for financial assistance. The
first table shows the substantial amounts left
in the business after paying the dividend.
Capital 10 years ago was £70,000. The only
new money got in since has been £14,000 six
years ago, and £4OOO of that represented pre-
miums on shares. Thus of the present capi-
tal of £125,000, £45,000 represents capital-
ised profit. Liabilities are £4127 less than
they were 10 years ago; meantime fixed
assets and the interest in the McCulloch co.
has been increased by £25,362, stock by
£27,978, book debts by £12,050, and cash and
public securities by £31,568. The co.’s posi-
tion has thus been strengthened by over
£87,000 out of profits, and besides there is
the “bit up the sleeve” which the directors
admit holding to meet contingencies. Alto-
gether a very fine record.

The latest copper-smelting plant to be erected in
Australia is that of the Mount Cuthbert Company
in North Queensland. It consists of buildings, power-
plant with boilers, generating sets and blowers,
blast furnace, copper converters and converter re-
fining plant, blister gathering and cleaning shed,
also steel bins for ore and coke. Wherever possible
the plant is electrically driven. The whole of the
plant was supplied by the Austral Qtis Engineering
Co. of Melbourne, who have made numerous smelting
plants working in Australia at present.— Advt. [2

For the half-year to June 30 Kampong
Ivamunting (F.M.S.) produced only 3234
tens tin oxide, compared with 482 tons for
the previous term. No. 1 dredge was in diffi-
cult ground, but No. 2 was ploughing through
a very lean patch—shallow and irregular
ground with a hard bottom. The results are
shown in this comparison:—
No. 1 Dredge.

With tin at anything like old prices a lot
of the ground worked by the No. 2 dredge
would have been passed by as unpayable. As
it is, this poor stuff has produced good pro-
fits. Despite the big drop in output,
owing to the record prices ruling the net
profit for the half-year amounted to £32,34G
compared with £33,929 for the previous term.
Two divs. absorbing £28,000 were paid.
With both dredges in good ground again—
and “it should be some years before another
lean patch will be dredged”—the outlook is
very bright. So bright that in addition to hand-
ing out the usual 2s. each quarter the direc-
tors propose to add a bonus of Is. so long as
profits permit. Including the September div.
and bonus, shareholders have received 19s.
Gd. on each of the 140,000 £1 shares. Six
months ago the" life of the show was esti-
mated at 11 years at least; since then a fur-
ther 40 acres of low-grade land have been
acquired to the south, adding another year
to the life of the mine, and also another 34
acres of 21b. ground 28ft. deep contiguous
to the main holding, which will help the co.
in cleaning up that particular corner. At
June 30 the co. owed sundry creditors £1522 ;
and it had £37,333 in War Loan, £4049 in
cash, £18.164 in stores, etc., sundry debtors
owed £llB5 and £7955 had been advanced
to the Asam Kumbang co.
* * * •
Kampong Kamunting’s latest report dis-
closes the arrangements that have been made
to finance the Asam Kumbang (F.M.S.),
which is also in the Freeman stable. At
Dec. last the Kumbang’s paid-up capital was
£74,820, of which £45,693 had been spent on
mine account, £16,554 on dredges, while
working account had absorbed £3BOO, and
£143 was in office furniture. That left £S63O at
the bank after meeting outstanding accounts.
Recently the big dredge being built by
Ruwolt’s was completed and shipped. To
help equip the show the Kampong Kamunt-
ing a while ago arranged to advance £35,000
at 7 p.c. p.a. on the security of the property,
at the same time securing the right during
the currency of the loan to take up 10,000
of the unissued shares at 255. per share.
Chiefly owing to the cost of transporting the
dredge to the Asam property it has been
found that a further £15,000 is required, so
the Kampong Kamunting has obliged on the
same interest terms. At June 30 £7955 had
been advanced, and the remainder is being
handed over as required. It is reckoned that
the Asam Kumbang will be in a position to
commence repayment about' the middle of
next year. There are 700 acres in the pro-
perty ; 234 acres of which were acquired
for £1493 last year. About 200 acres of
this ground averaged .71b. per cub. yard. Of
the original 490 acres, 442 acres were esti-
mated to contain 15,850,000 cub. yards of an
average, value of .91b. tin oxide, and 48 acres
containing 2,000,000 cub. yards, averaging
■sBlb. Reckoning tin at £l7O and working
costs at 4d. per yard, these 490 acres were
estimated to give an aggregate profit of
£295,000, giving a life of years with
one dredge.
* * * *
The new silver-lead show that caused
something of a stir in the Molong district
(N.S.W.) has been looked at by the Mines
Department. The occurrence consists of
irregular bundles of ore, confined to a single
formation, along the bedding planes of joints
of a limestone rock which have become chan-
nels for percolating solutions. The ore so
far revealed consists of galena and carbonate
of lead. The dolomite bed carrying it is from
20ft. to 35ft. wide, and was first thought by
the prospectors to be all lode matter; but
from present indications the minerals
appear to be developed in only the western
portion of the bed close to its contact with
the clay-shales. The ore bunches range from
Gin. to 2ft. 6in. wide. The patchy nature of the
ore is disclosed by cuts, which at the sur-
face showed little bunches that pinched out
within a few feet. All of which leads to
the conclusion that there is not sufficient jus-
tification for the highly-optimistic expecta-
tions of the discoverers. Still surveyor Jones
reckons that the prospects merit further in-
vestigation. Meanwhile the Prospecting Vote
is being tapped to subsidise limited sinking.
Samples since assayed show good copper con-
tents. with decent gold and lead, but with
low silver values.
Crushing has started at Mt. Dudley, near
Trunkey (N.S.W.), where the machinery
brought from the old Mt. David show has
been put into position. The co. had merely to
enlarge the hauling shaft, which goes down
along a perfect footwall. The mine had been
opened up by the previous owner, A. B.
Kirkman, to the 145 ft. level, where No. 2
drive is located. The top drive is at 80ft.
The co. has increased its lease area, and now
holds 45 acres. When full the dam at the
foot of the hill, from which the water-supply
is pumped, will hold a million gallons. Blocks
north and south of the co.’s area have been
taken up.
* * -X- *
A Sydney party with £3OOO to spend is
having a look at a bismuth show at Duck-
maloi, near Oberon (N.S.W.), held by Alex-
ander Riddel and Son, who have been ex-
tracting good values from the big ore body
since the beginning of the year. The field is
entirely new in that particular locality,
though earlier adventurers had a go at the
stone about the same region in the days
when profitable treatment methods had yet to
be discovered. The possibilities of the place
are said to have been put beyond doubt, so a
rush for areas has set in.
* * * *
White Crystal, Ardlethan (N.S.W.), has
been on the call list, and reports concerning
the various open cuts and shafts have been
too vague to enliven the market. A plain
statement of the quantity of ore raised and
its approximate value would do something
to inspire confidence. At Stackpool’s lease
a face of ore 12ft. wide is being worked
carrying “fair tin values.” Driving south
from the main open cut a face of kaolin ore
seems likely to provide something for the mill.
At Murphy’s shaft “good tin” values are
reported from time to time over a width
of 3ft., but. the quantity hauled towards the
next crushing isn’t mentioned.
* * * *
Carpathia tin (N.S.W.) recovered favor
after the slump on the strength of a winze
development from No. 2 level. Values im-
proved from 6 p.c. to 31 p.c. tin in the sink,
which is now 43ft. below the level. With tin
at £334, ore carrying 31 p.c. metallic tin
is worth big money. Still, the quantity of
this high-grade stuff raised week by week
is very small. Last half-year the Carpathia
treated 151 tons of special sulphides at the
Barmedman mill for a return of 46 tons of
tin oxide. Recently the management has
been sending only selected and special mill-
ing ore for treatment, and some lower-grade
stuff has been accumulating at the mine.
* » • »
For a whole year Langi Logan South,
Ararat (Vic.), has been either battling before
the Arbitration Court or bending the knee to
the A.W.U. in the vain hope of getting wash
stoped on contract. Justice Power was
agreeable, but allotted a minimum of 11s. a
day wages. To see how that would pan
out a party of panellers was given a job, and
tenders were asked for the extraction of
wash. The wage and work done is summed
up by George Bryant as “ridiculous,” while
the tenders sent in were over the fence in
price. That the lead isn’t barren is proved
by 262 fathoms giving 3680 z. gold. That
average with men on the job would mean
divs. The A.W.U. isn’t bothering about
efficiency, as it builds on pulling off a daily-
wage stunt for the field, although Alec
Peacock’s Board is fighting against anything
of the kind. All that can be done at the mine
under these conditions is to shove ahead with
the south-east drive down the gutter, so as
to open up the lead there and take the water
out of the gutter to the north. This bit of
luxury, with the added cost of pumping, now
eats up a monthly call of 3d. on 60,000 shares,
against a possible profit earning if miners
could be got to cut out the go-slow game. No
wonder Alec Peacock’s directors have got
snake-headed, for they have seen £54,350 of
calls, £11,666 of original capital and £lB,OOO
of gold tossed into the mine without the sign
of a div. So they are asking shareholders to
declare whether work shall be stopped. Share-
holders seem to be about equally divided on
the subject.
* * * *
N. S. Wales Chief-inspector of Mines
Jaquet has issued. his report on earth-
tremors at Kalgoorlie and, incidentally, the
Great Boulder collapse. He finds that the
earthquake shock, which resulted in a big dis-
placement of ground in the Boulder and the
death of a miner, was caused by the collapse
of a granite pillar surrounded by a large area
of worked-out ground, and that the pillar
did not give way on account of earth-tremors,
but that earth-tremors were caused by the
collapse of the pillar. In the western group
of mines, of which the Great Boulder is one,
Jaquet says that the walls of the lode worked
are exerting great side pressure below the
3200 ft. or 1400 ft. levels, and that there is
a slow general movement in a southerly
direction. He considers that in Hamilton’s
workings at the north end of Boulder ground,
and just outside the northern boundary of
the Ivanhoe shrinkage, stopes below 1350 ft.
should not be allowed while adjoining rill
and flat back stopes are being worked in the
Ivanhoe and Boulder, and that stopes worked
below 050 ft. should be filled. On the whole,
he finds that good and sufficient means of
supporting the workings are being employed
on the field, and that working conditions
there are not more dangerous, or even as
dangerous, as those on some other fields. The
presence of fire-damp, he says, is so small
that it is scarcely worth mentioning.
X- * * *
From Westonia: Central is faced with
heavy expenditure on development, including
main-shaft sinking, and future profit likely
to be derived from its economical manage-
ment will have to be expended on work
under the present bottom level. Holmes’
reef will be struck in the east drive 321 ft.
level next month, and this is almost certain
to be about as valuable to Central as it was
to Consolidated, that is, next to nothing.
It is unlikely that Deeps will turn out any-
thing more in the future than in the past.
Apart from its gold output, its production
to date has been as follows : One herd of well-
groomed and well-satisfied bulls, about the
same number of well-skinned bears, high
assays unlimited, many tons of optimism,
and unrealised prospects galore. In short.
Deeps seems capable of producing anything
but anticipated dividends.
* * * *
Stuckey, at the Edna Deeps, intends to
put out a £2500 yield this month ; but Novem-
ber is reckoned to see a recovery to £4OOO.
The reason given for bringing yields down is
that a slab of 700 tons of stone left in No. 3
level has to be broken out to switch work on
to the right track. But why is it that this
stone has been left to hold up results? Should
it not have been shifted when the run of the
mine output was being arranged? Actual
and estimated yields lately have been :

Edna May Battler (W.A.) claims to have
secured an. option on something promising
in the neighborhood of Southern Cross.
‘Careful sampling” of ore in the shaft and
an adjacent surface outcrop is stated to have
given assays ranging from 20s. to 84s. per
ton, and there is a lot of talk about “pros-
pects.” But is this property not identical
with the one that the Associated Northern
abandoned and others failed to make a living
out of? s

Owing to the long arm of Italy having
reached out for all its able-bodied men, Leonora
Blocks (W.A.) has to go short-handed. Mana-
ger Jas. Gale testifies to being satisfied with
the developments spread over the past six
months. His joy doesn’t extend to the pallid
“l* of holders who still see that debts of
£3500 have to be met, and that the claim is
admittedly as much in need of opening up as
ever. This state of affairs can’t be altered
till another lift of 100 ft. is sunk. If that be
done, there isn’t anything in Cale’s screed on
the mine to prove that it is a certainty that
pay-dirt will be got.

Manager Ryan, of the Pioneer (Tas.)
reckons that this sluicing proposition, which
has been handing out dividends at the rate of
os. od. a year, has a life of from four to
five years, but the payable period necessarily
depends on the tin market. Practically ail
the profits are distributed as they are earned
so the Pioneer has no reserve fund worth
mentioning, though the stock of unsold tin-
oxido on June 30 was valued at £9OOO. Share-
hoiders are candidly told to regard a portion
of the divs. as a return of capital.
* * * *
C.”: Many Peaks (Q.) has still about 50
men employed, and if the roasting process it
has in hand proves a success, the co. will
continue operations. During the September
quarter 491 tons were sent to the roasting
plant,_ and about 15,000 tons are dumped
awaiting treatment. The crudes contain
about 40 p.c. sulphur, which might, of course
be converted into sulphuric acid, for it is
from pyrites rich in sulphur that most of the
world s sulphuric acid is made. The fertiliser
trade is a large consumer of acid, but there
is an objection against pyrites containing
arsenic. Sulphuric acid and sulphur are too
valuable just now to waste.

From London:—
Winston Churchill, like our only William Morris
Hughes, seems unable to exist out of the limelight,
and, during recent months, has done his best—with
qualified success—to rise above the Dardanelles report.
Churchill resembles Hughes in possessing vivid powers
of oratory, but excels in having a great gift of apt
and scorching repartee. His command of words
failed him, however, on at least two occasions during
the stormy Home Rule debates in the Commons,
both defeats coming from
his political opponent
and personal friend, F.
E. Smith (“Galloper”).
On the first of the two
occasions Churchill,
doubtless intending to
explain that he “lived in
hope,” etc., had only got
out “I live.” Smith im-
mediately snapped out
“Yes, but wc fail to see
the necessity.” Churchill
recovered when F.E. took
him out to dinner. The
second time was when
Winston used the words
“I conceive,” and, losing
the thread of his argu-
ment, twice more re-
peated the two words,
when Smith up-ended
himself and interjected,
“Mr. Speaker, the right
honorable gentleman has
conceived three times
and brought forth
nothing.”
COMMANDANT
D’ANDRE, A.D.C.
to General Pau, of
the French Mission.
Lieut.-Col. Albert Holdsworth, just back
from France with the D.S.O. and a hand-
some Army Service Corps record, was one of
the few war-experienced Australians who
helped to push things ahead in Victoria
during the 1914 preparations for active ser-
vice. He put in a lot of monotonous work
in Egypt before the heads gave him a chance
to mix it with the enemy; but he was able
by-and-bye to take a part in the doings at
Tripoli, where he earned his D.S.O. Later
he went to France. Holdsworth’s previous
war-work was in 'the Boer diversion, for
which he collected the Queen’s medal with
six clasps..
The S. Aus. pioneers are rapidly thin-
ning out. Died at Torrensville a few 7 days
ago, John Ritchie, born in Scotland, 1833.
He had sailed around the world three times
before he was 20, and at that age he was
qualified to navigate a ship. In ’55 he landed at
S. Aus., where he ivas first employed on a
sailing vessel under his brother James, who
was captain. After a trip to Maoriland
John vrnnt to Goolwa, and for over 10 years
w 7 as skipper on a Murray River boat. He
then tried farming in the North, and was
also actively engaged at Methodist preach-
ing. Captain Barber, who stood by the
graveside of Ritchie, is another old Murray
navigator. He was born on the same day
and in the same village as Ritchie, and the
tw 7 o had kept in touch right through their
lives. They married sisters.

Lieut.-Colonel R. A. Waddy, recently dead
in Sydney, obtained his rank in the old
Volunteer Force, of which he was an early
member. His father came to Sydney as an
ensign in the 50th Regiment in Governor
Bourke’s time. On a second visit of the
50th, many years after, the ex-subaltern was
Lieutenant-Colonel, and before his death he
became Major-General Richard Waddy,
K.C.B. Lt.-Col. Waddy was engaged all his
life in banking, the Commercial Banking Go-
of Sydney being the institution in which he
served. His last stand was as manager at
Morpeth.
“Simon’s” yarn :—•
John (Pasha) Barry, just dead at 86, after years
of coach-driving and managing for Cobb and Co. in
Victoria and N. S. Wales, settled down in Mudgee in
the Imperial Hotel. He was the despair of every
candidate for public office —Parliamentary and munfi
cipal. The vigor of his advocacy on the one hand
scorched everything in opposition, while on the other
hand his industry and invective was just awful.
His favorite way was to take his stand in front of
a candidate he didn't like and spear into him every
second or two with the interjection “If elected.”
Once a blue-ribbon coaehbuilder, who had in some
manner raised the wrath of John, became a starter
in a local council election. John went stark mad
and followed him everywhere, Sunday-school in-
cluded, for about 40 days, murmuring something
about white-washed suplecurs. The Pasha’s greatest
win was with Billy Wall for the Mudgee seat. The
Protectionists were hard-pressed for a candidate.
The man they had picked on had disappointed
them 10 minutes before closing of nominations, and
it seemed as if they were not to be represented in
the contest. Suddenly a cloud of dust was seen com-
ing over Mudgee bridge, out of which emerged John
Barry, and beside him Billy Wall fresh from the
handles of a plough. Barry had raced 30 miles to
get Wall that morning and landed his man on the
stroke.
Tom Carrington, who passed out at a
ripe age in Melbourne the other day, was
Australia’s first cartoonist of consequence.
As an admirer of John Tenniel he
made his old Melb. Punch pictures resemble
as closely as possible those of the London
artist, even the signature, “T.C.,” being
almost a facsimile of Tenniel’s “C.T.” But
Carrington on his own pictorial
ability, as was made manifest immediately
the zinc-process block was introduced. Car-
rington's drawings for the process prove that
he owed half his effect to the wood-engraver,
llis real talent was literary, and “T.C.”
might have written a book of the Melbourne
world of letters in his early Punch days
that would have been valuable and interest-
ing. Nobody knew journalistic Melbourne
in the Marcus Clarke-Lindsay Gordon period
as Carrington knew it; nobody could tell so
many good stories of the writers, artists and
actors of that truly Bohemian period, and
it is a tremendous pity that Carrington
did not write of those men and those times,
instead of wasting his ink on theatrical and
art criticism, for which he had no notable
capacity.
Just a year ago Raymond Evatt, the
well-known North Sydney Rugby Union
footballer and swimmer, was killed at Pass-
chendale. after three years of active service
with the A.I.F. Now his young brother,
Gunner Frank Evatt, of the same swimming
club, and a medical student at the Univer-
sity, has died of wounds after being two
years away. Both these lads enlisted on
their 18th. birthday. Raymond was a born
soldier. His brother was the student, and was
responsible for much able criticism of music
and drama and literature. Both were sons
of Mrs. Evatt, of Kirribilli.
“F.H.”: Writing of the late James Bal-
four (B. 17/10/T8), “Vic.” says that in
1852 Balfour came to Melbourne as a clerk
under engagement to Ilenty and Co., Henty
being a son of the founder of Portland. This
is not quite correct. The actual founder of
Portland was Edward Henty, though his
father, Thomas Henty, supplied the cash,
stock and plant necessary for the enterprise.
Edward had no children. Thomas Henty
settled in Tasmania (he died there), and
visited Portland only in a casual way once
or twice. Mrs. Balfour, who still lives, was
a daughter of James, one of Thomas’s seven
sons. All the young Plentys but two,'by the
way, came to Portland, soon or late. Of the
two who stayed in Tasmania, Charles pre-
sently became the State’s Colonial Secretary,
in the days before the Speck had a Parlia-
ment of its own ; and William, of whom a
tintype still hangs in the unpretentious
Parliamentary picture-gallery at Hobart, had
a seat in the Legislature when responsible
Government came into being later.
The influenza raging in Africa just now
carried off a good Australian a week ago—
Lieut. Stanley Williams (24), an Anzac, who
fought in Gallipoli, France and East Africa.
After the Evacuation he got a commission
in the Royal Fusiliers. He was wounded at
Delville Wood, and for a spell was an in-
structor in England. Later he got after the
Hun again in German East Africa. After
that he transferred to the Ist Nigerian regi-
ment, _ was stationed inland at Zaria, in
Nigeria, and there the epidemic caught him.
He was the only son of A. B. Williams, of
the New York Life Insurance Co., and an
old Sydney Grammar boy.
Being the varied record of William Groves,
proven by the Base Records Office to have
been the first Victorian to enlist in the
A.1.F.: Enlisted 12/8/’l4; camped in the
open at Broadmeadows till the tents and
other equipment arrived; had five months’
fighting on Gallipoli; returned to Egypt with
dysentery ; had an operation for internal in-
juries; held down a storeman’s job at Zie-
toun; presently passed for further active
service and saw same in the desert; acquired
a septic foot and went to England, where
he had three operations; recovered enough
health to take on a busy job of painting land-
scape targets for airmen, and was presently
made staff-sergeant at a hospital. Grove's
is. expected to return shortly to Australia
with a contingent of original Anzacs.

Died at Dubbo (N.S.W.) last week, only
three months short of the century, Mrs. R.
J. Cross, as far as is known the oldest
native-born Australian. She came to light
ar Liverpool in 1819. Her father, William
Skene, was manager for J. .J. Moore at
Liverpool, and when he married, Governor
Macquarie granted him land on which Mrs.
Cross was born. She lived there up to the
time of her marriage to Ralph Jackson Cross,
in 1837.
Usually a solemn person who comported
himself in the Victorian Legislative Assem-
bly as if he were in the kirk, Robert George
McCutcheon, who handed in his checks a few
days ago, permitted himself to make a frivol-
ous and apt interjection in September, 1914.
Prendergast, a fellow printer, who at this
period was taking a spell from the job of
leading the Opposition, was contending with
characteristic vehemence that not enough
water had fallen to make it worth while to
have storage reservoirs for it, when McCut-
cheon chipped in with the remark: “What
you mean to say is that the rain has not
been worth a dam.” McCutcheon narrowly
missed inclusion as a salaried Minister in
the second Watt Cabinet (1913). He
accepted an honorary post w T hen Peacock
shed some of his colleagues in 1915, and
linked up with McLeod, McKenzie and Rob-
inson. Laborites respected McCutcheon be-
cause he was a decent employer, and his
criticism of their methods was not resented
as bitterly as that which came from some
other pillars of the Employers’ Federation.
Though 77, he was active to the last in
running a big printery in Bank-place, off
Collins-street, of which he was head. He
made way for Wynne at the last general
election.
After reading, will you please send this paper to
the Red Cross Book, Magazine and Newspaper Depot,
5 Wynyard Street, Sydney, or place it in one of the
receptacles provided for the purpose. All copies thus
received are divided equally between the Red Cross
Society and the War Chest Fund, and are posted
weekly to our soldiers abroad.
TAKE IT EASY
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
N.S.W.
He dwells in Take-it-easy,
A pleasant place, ’tis true,
Loved well and sought by many,
But tenanted by few.
Full-length in Take-it-easy
He stretches through the days,
Around him silken breezes —
And this, at times, he says:
“Though others scratch and scramble,
I let the whole world be;
The tree grows, the wind blows,
The tide ebbs, the tide flows,
The hour comes, the hour goes—
And that’s enough for me!”
If jarred at Take-it-easy,
Where only sluggards bask.
You shake him by the shoulder,
And point him to his task,
’Tis he will turn to nothing
The world and all its ways,
The while, ’twixt shrug and gesture,
In careless wise he says:
“Though others scratch and scramble,
l let the whole world be;
The flower grows, the wine flows,
The dawn smiles, the noon glows,
The bee sucks the red rose—
And that’s enough for me!”
If still you shake his shoulder,
And still the theme pursue
With talk about his duties,
And all that he should do,
’Tis then, grown grave, he’ll answer
In accents, calm and cool,
As though lie were the wise man
And you, in truth, the fool:
“Though others scratch and scramble,
Why should I troubled be?
The light flies from Life’s eyes.
The flower fades, the wind sighs,
The tree falls, the man dies —-
And that’s enough for me!”

SOCIETY
PENFOLD'S
WINES
The recent Anglican Synod held in Perth
proved again what a fine old crusted body is
the Church of England. A reverend icono-
clast moved that women be permitted to hold
office in the church —not to invade the pul-
pit, but merely to become chuchwardens and
AN OPEN SECRET.
“Toby, your face is all lines to-night .”
“Keep it dark — they're my marriage-lines.”
do minor odd jobs. The religious troglo-
dytes fell on the proposal and threw hymn-
books at it, though the mover pointed out
that women supplied by far the larger pro-
portion of the congregations, and were conse-
quently entitled to a say in the management
of church affairs. The opposition could not
deny that most of the money dropped into
the plate came from the superstitious sex;
and being stumped for logical argument made
vague noises about woman's place, being the
home. One ancient lay brother with no eye
for business said that the sight of a woman
walking up the aisle with a collection-plate
would be horrible and calculated to drive the
occasional men away from the temple. Such
ignorance is pathetic. Under present cir-
cumstances, when the dish is hawked by
depressed-looking males dressed as shop-
walkers or undertakers, there is little in-
ducement to pay up. But if the collection
were placed in the hands of blue-eyed salva-
tion sirens with trim figures and black-silk
stockings, religion would bound ahead like
an eight-cylinder motor. It would become
the hobby of every blase sinner in the com-
munity. Even aide-de-camps and book-
makers would be in the rush on Sundays.
* * * *
The Anglican Church of St. Luke, Liverpool
(N.S.W.). is entering upon its centenary year.
The foundation-stone was laid in 1819. and
the building was completed two years later
by the contractor, Nathaniel Lucas. Before
St Luke’s, services were held in Liverpool,
by Parson Marsden. Parish records date
back to 1808. The first minister of St. Luke’s
was parson Cartwright, who came out in
1809.

Everything possible should be done for the
fighting Australian who has lost a limb, and
bo should not be left in doubt for one moment
that his grateful country will provide him
with the best substitute procurable, making
no distinction between officer or private, or
between men with influence and men with-
out. It appears from a letter just received
by The Bulletin that a number of maimed
Australians now in hospital in England have
received the unhappy impression that dis-
abled men returning to Australia must put
up with rough substitutes unless they can
hunt up or acquire an influential friend or
two. This paper’s informant writes that the
astounding news reached London from an
Australian soldier who lost his right arm,
and, being offered a substitute limb with a
hook, refused it. He was then told frankly
that it was no use asking for a better arti-
ficial limb, unless he could get someone of
“influence” to speak up for him. The sol-
dier found a sympathetic Victorian and
eventually got a cork arm. But this was
more than balanced by a reduction of pen-
sion ! Apparently disabled men in hospitals
in Britain believe it. The Defence Depart-
ment ought to scotch the yarn.
“Centurion”: In reply to “Old Soldier”;
my description of the modern British cavalry
sword; if “curious,” as “0.5.” asserts, is
correct. I was put wise to the new weapon
at Zietoun, in 1917, at a school of instruction
for field-officers, and had to pass on my
acquired wisdom to my
squadron in due course.
This sword, by the way,
was introduced into the
British Army in 1912 or
1913. On foot the sword
is held out in a straight
line from the shoulder,
back of the hand to the
left, and therefore shell
uppermost. The point is
delivered with a straight-
arm lunge. On horse-
back the swordsman is
told to lie forward on his
horse’s neck with sword-
arm as described, the hilt
covering the head, which
should be held as low as
possible. The pace of the
horse and the lunge do
the rest. The parry is a
slight turn of the fore-
arm, difficult to describe,
but the wrist is not to
move independently. The
weapon is not the Court
sword or a battle-axe or
a crowbar, but exactly as
I have described it. The
Indian cavalry I saw in
Sinai and Palestine still
carry their curved cut-
ting swords. Whatever
was taught to cavalry in
the days of the Mutiny, it
is laid down now that the
point only must be used.
As to disengaging from
a prodded opponent, that
is left to the prodder’s
discretion. It is like marriage ; he must fend
for himself.
What should be the Kaiser’s punishment
is a topic that has been revived in Adelaide.
Governor Galway considers St. Helena too
pleasant a place for Wilhelm’s exile, but
knows a nice little spot where he would
not be too comfortable —AlcCarthy Island,
about 150 miles up the Gambia River in
Senegal, West Africa. Premier Peake opines
that, as wilful murder has been recorded
against the chief Hun, he should be hanged.
The lady war-workers seem to be the most
vindictive. “I'd put him in a cage and tor-
ture his life out.” “Take him to pieces inch
by inch,” were two expressions. One promi-
nent lady worker made this statement: “You
dare hot put in print what I would do to
him.” The young reporter let it go at that.

“Trooper No. 529”: In the days when
there was no correspondent handy to tell
what our boys were doing in Palestine a
Light Horse regiment had a brush with the
Turks east of the Jordan. Abdul was driven
off leaving three prisoners and a machine-
gun. A young Billjim was detailed to take
the bunch back, and he was jogging along
unconcernedly when suddenly he rode into
a big party of Jaekos. llis first impulse was
to bolt; but he pulled himself together and
sternly invited the convention to come along
with him, assuring them that the regiment
was following up and that they were practi-
cally surrounded. As a matter of fact
(although the Aussie didn’t know it at the
time) the regiment was returning another
way and the Turks had a clear passage to
their own lines. The crowd went into com-
mittee and discussed the situation, while the
bluffer sat on his prad and wondered what
was going to happen. His hard face settled
the point. There was a sudden rattle of
equipment as the crowd sullenly threw down
their arms and joined the procession to the
rear—seven officers and 51 other ranks in all.
In N. S. Wales where the teacher, after
years of struggle, has had his salary raised
to a minimum of £3 a week to put him rather
below the man who drives a tram-car, the
cause of education has been advanced by the
appointment of a Professor of Architecture
at £2OOO per annum. The Professor will
now turn-to on a large scale and deal with
that scarcity of four-roomed houses which
afflicts the community.
One of the most interesting documents
Tiie Bulletin can remember seeing issued
from a State Printing Department has just
been placed in the Public Libraries of Maori-
land. This is a 12-page foolscap edition of
the works of the local Statistician’s office in
relation to poultry and bees. On the night
of October 15, 1916, while the rest of the
world was either asleep or fighting. Maori-
land took a complete census of the hen-
house, and the box containing the lady with
a sting in her tail. The results obtained by
the enumerators were handed over to the
statisticians. From these have been compiled
a fascinating story. A mass of figures'
on page 1 reveal that in 10 years the chook
in ALL. has multiplied by 376,496, a start-
ling indication of what may be done
by intensive culture, when one remembers
the large number of patriotic banquets con-
sumed over yonder. On succeeding pages the
reader is supplied with copious arithmetic
about the poultry in detail. At a glance
he can find out how many citizens keep
poultry in each horottgh and in each county
—how many chicks they reared during the
last hatch. For instance, at Whakatane 399
citizens are addicted to propagation of the
fowl. Their flocks roost to the number of
7018, and there were 1770 new ones in the
fluff stage. At Waipukurau the number of
fowl proprietors is only 177. This variation is
unaccountable. But there are other intensely
interesting facts about the laying-machine
concealed in feathers. The statisticians have
worked out elaborate tables showing the
division according to sex as well as locality.
The rooster is tabulated apart from his
harem ; the number of ducks is given in each
locality, likewise the number of drakes; the
gentleman turkey is distinguished from the
lady and the gander sorted out from the
goose. The turkey population of the boroughs
was very limited on the night of the count,
being only 919, of which 338 were husbands.
Most likely they were waiting for the guillo-
tine. But there was no apparent reason to
fear a shortage of geese, either in town or
country. The grand total of the Dominion’s
population of gander was 9231, and of the
goose which lays an egg occasionally 20,791.
Tha»drakes out-numbered the ganders 3 to 1.
A very singular circumstance disclosed by
the statisticians is that at Eketahuna 10
drakes and 111 lady ducks had a family of

encouraging and suggests that M.L. should
be able to face the future undismayed. The
statistics about the bees are extensive and
illuminating, but the enumerators seem to
have been shirkers. They didn’t count the
innards of the hives.

PEACE AT THE GATES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Belgian cavalry are at the gates of Bruges.”— Cable.
The belfry watches o’er the lane
Of parting foemen once again,
A(i round by Notre Dame’s grey tower
March beaten robbers, hour by hour.
The houses wake from haunted sleep
And hear the news ; and no more weep
In sorrow for the long lost balm
Of folded centuries of calm.
Beneath the plane-trees Memlinc prays
For quiet down the cobbled ways,
And in the halls where pictures glow
He made five hundred years ago.
In Gothic rooms the candles gleam
Before the kind-eyed Saints, who seem
To smile at hurried feet outside—
The mighty feet by Right denied.
And belfry bells that rang alarms
Of old, red fields and burning farms
Sway softly to begin the tune
That speeds the “blonde beast”, going soon.
Canals that ran in blood before
Sing now of Peace at hand once more,
And ripple at the secret jest— y
The brave loud word, the last and best.
N.S.W.
HILARY LOFTING.

A./. : It the suggestion is made to an
employer of labor in Britain that the employ-
ment of women during the war may create
a difficulty when the men return, he jaughs—
if he. doesn’t boil-over and give a full history
ot lus skirted troubles. Women have hail
their chance and high wages; and, although
they have got through the work, that
they did it well or efficiently and
without being troublesome is only a
polite journalistic fiction. The public,
offices where women are largely employed
are places of vast confusion and inefficiency ;
departmental heads have to be contented
with the idea of getting the work done badly
or not at all. This is not to* say that some
women are not quite as efficient 'as the most
efficient of men ; but the bulk are not, and
have no wish to be even moderately
efficient. This was the boi’-ever (if
one harassed chief: “They have no
sense of fairness or responsibility. They
take advantage of you and the fact that the'v
are women. Whenever they feel inclined,
they take a holiday; and just when you most
rely upon them being present you find them
HIT ON THE BEG OIL.
THE MAJOR (who has just set her right):
“Bwains will tell, you know."
SISTER ISIE (humbly): “Yes, I suppose

absent. They allow paltry matter of preju-
dice, liking or jealousy to interfere every
hour with business. They will work more
eagerly and well for a man who is unmarried
—for a time; but shortly they will decide,
unanimously, that it is quite‘time he was
married, and won’t work for him at all.
Work will never be more than a sort of
side-issue with them; and they seem to
regard it as a personal triumph if they can
place you in a hole, and then help you out,
possibly, as a particular favor.” Recently
there has come a demand from some of the
women workers for equal pay for equal
work, which is a fair enough demand, and
one that men, who want to be sure of their
jobs, should support.
THE PENALTY.
“Mamma, did yon love to flirt when you were young?"
“ I’m afraid I did, dear."
“And were you ever punished for it, mamma?"
“Yes, dear. I married your father."
Aaron Banks, president of the Melbourne
Hospital committee, has started a sort of
gamble connected with the prevailing peace
talk. He has issued a public appeal, asKing
for signatures to a pledge binding those who
sign it to make the hospital a stated dona-
tion, once peace is declared, “as a thank-
offering.” It is rather an ingenious idea,
though such appeals wouldn’t be necessary
if the State did its plain duty, paying for
the upkeep of the various public hospitals
out of revenue instead of out of the hat. It
is a shambling way of running anything, and
its results are humiliating. Melbourne Hos-
pital alone lias to face the clearing-up of an
overdraft of £26.770—a preposterous state of
affairs.
Adelaide is anxious to know who was
really responsible for its leg being pulled so
hard on Thursday, October 17, when the
Town Hall bells were rung, and Parliament
adjourned, under the impression that Ger-
many had “surrendered.” The daily papers
got the same cables from Rotterdam as did
the rest of Australia, and placarded them at
their front doors ; but they deny all liability
for the enthusiasm which followed. Lionel
Hill, in the Assembly, has since asked the
Premier whether Major Smeaton, chief of
the censors, gave the information to the
Cabinet. Peake replied that all the informa-
tiontion came _ from the newspaper offices.
We were a little previous in rejoicing. I
am not apologising in any way.” The same
temperament which keeps Adelaide from ex-
periencing anything like a riot also prevents
it from going on the maffick; and it feels
much hurt that when it did blow off it was
upon a false alarm.

}\. M. Hughes in England (where he

“Chudah”: It is agreed on all hands that
the Jews are to have Jerusalem and Pales-
tine. In the United States it is decided—
and nobody will gainsay Woodrow Wilson
backed by nearly a million New York Jews
—that there is to be a Jewish army in Pales-
tine. Already about 8000 Jewish soldiers
have been sent from the United States to
England, thence to Palestine, and they are
called the Jewish Legion, and will remain as
nucleus of the Jewish Republic—that is,
some of them will. The ofßers will be back
at the old address as soon as possible. That
England and France have given their approval
to the scheme is shown by the Legion fighting
under British direction.
“Bad Australian”: When the National
Kitchens were started a howl went up from
every Private Enterprise hashery in London,
and it was pointed out that this'was another
Socialistic experiment calcu-
lated to undermine the founda-
tions of social life by letting
loose a horde of amateur food-
ruiners. All good patriotic citi-
zens were implored to turn a
deaf stomach to the tempter and
stand by the old order of chops
in the interests of public safety.
The restaurant-keepers had just
proved to their own satisfaction
that the experiment must end in
financial ruin and national dys-
pepsia. when the National
Restaurant, New Bridge-street,
E.C., which hands out a better
feed at a more satisfactory price
than most of the private con-
cerns, weighed in with the re-
sults of its first three weeks:
Profits for first week, £5O 14s.
sd. ; second, £7O 13s. 9d.; third,
£73 7s. 4d. A communication
from the Ministry of Pood says
that at the rate of £7O per week,
which appears to be the main-
tainable profit, the. capital outlay will be
repaid within 18 months. Verily the poor
profiteer has no chance against the Socialis-
tic Tiger!
“M.H.”: Bean correctly reported at the
commencement of the August push that one
Australian battalion, which was right in the
business, only had three casualties. The
company that was at the head of the push on
that part of the line didn’t have a single
casualty. Bean didn’t mention, however,
that, after the show, there were only 34 men
left in one company of a neighboring bat-
talion. The difference was that that battalion
was held up in lines of which Fritz had the
exact range, while the line was being
straightened for a further advance. Actu-
ally it is seldom that many casualties occur,
nowadays, in the hop over; it is in the hang-
mg-on to captured positions subsequently
that the damage is done. In one operation,
where 150 of us went over and with com-
plete success gained our objective, occupying
the Germans’ front-line positions, there were
only two casualties in the actual advance;
but before the reliefs came out a couple of
days later the casualties had increased to
IUO, of which 70 were light wounds—walking
cases. The small operations are in fact
terribly expensive as compared with the big
shows that make the cable page crackle.
From Flanders:—
To see men “feeling their legs,” after the
manner of the . infant learning to walk, is a common
sight in London. The matter of providing artificial
limbs is of special interest to Australia, for the
number of our men who have lost their legs is a
subject of common remark in England. The ex-
planation lies irt the fact that the Australians have
made themselves notorious for their activity in
patrol encounters when there are no big movements
on hand. Invariably, when an Australian division
takes over a sector, the order comes through: “We
must be absolute masters of No Man’s Land”; and,
invariably, we are. In the fighting by which this
mastery is attained a lot of machine-gun fire is met
with, which, as it is directed on a low trajectory,
inflicts a lot of leg wounds; and a machine-gun
bullet, at close range, does a great deal more harm
to the bones than a piece of shell. But the substi-
tute limbs now provided are marvellous. I have
just seen two men who have lost both legs, all but
the slightest remainder of stump, walking about with
the aid of sticks, a little stiffly, on artificial limbs
that actually articulate at the knee. When they
get more confidence they will be able to discard the
sticks.
“Hamer”: The Sammies have heartily
seconded the Australian objection to the
saluting foolishness ; and although only quite
recently new orders were issued calling
attention to the necessity for the salute, the
practice is dying out. The Yanks—and
London is full of them now—don’t
salute worth mentioning, and their example
has strengthened the Australian con-
tempt for the parade-ground observ-
ance. No doubt many of the officers
are glad, although only the other day I saw
a fussy red-capped staff-officer (the real
London campaigner) hold up and rate a
lanky digger. “Beg pardon, sir,” he said
politely, “but I've been so long in France
that I’ve forgotten all about it.” Then he
saluted properly, leaving the staff effigy to
have his lit by himself.

bags of human bones, preserved as Exhibit
A at the Edenhope (Vic.) police-stafion, in
case an old-time murderer should ever be
roped in, are about to have decent burial.
Ex-soldier Slater, M.L.A. for Dundas,
brought the matter before Vic. Assembly

THE ROYAL ONION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
~twl boUT J( apers> i n view of the great scarcity of
other vegetables and the profusion of silver-skins
are emphasising the fact that the late King Edward
ate two large Spanish onions everv night before
retiring. °
The onion, so long abjured,
A thing of scorn in many eyes
(Or noses), is at length assured
Respect. ’Tis said, and none denies,
The good King Edward ate a brace
Each night before retiring late.
This should old prejudice efface
And give it favor for a space,
If only history will state
How many Alexandra ate.
For ’tis a sociable root,
And makes for kinship in a sense.
If he eats, she must follow suit,
If it be but in self-defence.
Shall we, who are but plebeian mould,
Deny the gift a monarch brings ?
To eat the leek was held of old
An act contemptible. Behold
llow customs change ! The laureate sings
This fragrant favorite of kings.
The rose of England has her scent,
The onion hath flavor, too.
And who will ask for roses blent
With tripe or mutton in a stew?
The onion shall have her day,
And maidens fair and dames austere
Must bear it as a sweet bouquet,
And breathe its perfume too, that they
May claim each in a manner here
Exhales the regal atmosphere.
VICTORIA.

PANAMAS.
This special purchase by our South American buyer
comes as a big surprise, but it is none the less welcome
on that account. One of those rare opportunities that
demand quick action and spot cash. The details con-
densed in brief should interest Australians. Owing to
transport difficulties, due chiefly to commandeering
railroad rolling-stock for war purposes, a big Panama order for one of the middle-west States of tI.S.A. was
delayed. The South American manufacturer received instructions, first to hold, and then to cancel order, as it
was impossible to get them through in time for the .North American Summer Season. They were offered
to our buyer at a big reduction for cash. Sensing the whole position, and finding it possible to get shipping space
to Australia at an early date, he closed with the offer. That’s the whole story.

From England :
A re-tion of the English press has been making a
long wad about the money that is being made and
spent by munition-workers. Writers contrast the lot of
the hard-living, low-paid soldier with that of the
man who makes the shells, but the differences don’t
impel any strong longing to substantially increase
the fighting man’s pay. Certainly it is a" tr'fle dis-
gusting to hear boastful talk of “making fifteen quid
a . pav” by hefty munitioneers before less able-
bodied men who have to go' into the line for
a mere pittance. But the soldiers are not
greatly disturbed, for the munition-workers are
not ungenerous in their spendings. Being generally
related to the fighting men, the soldiers get a benefit
from the big earnings, while they receive none at
all from the incomes of the fat profiteers whom the
press overlooks. The natural conclusion is that the
oiftcry is merely that of selfish people who are
shocked at the idea of “working men” having some-
thing more than a living wage. It is a state of
affairs that shocks the middle- and upper-clas>
Briton. But all the clumsy attempts to stir a feel-
ing of wrath amongst the working-class soldiery have,
so far, failed miserably.

“0.L.”: The National Federation, at its
recent conference in Melbourne, appointed a
pair of committees which are designed “to
encourage pastoral and agricul-
tural development, as well as
the industrial potentialities of
the State.” Great cheering, of
course; but precisiely what is
the Federation going to do?
Of late months the pretentious
concern has hardly been heard
of. Scarcely a murmur has
emanated from the central office
where general-secretary Ilumo
Cook, ex-M.11.R. for Bourke.
sits and weaves his “national”
webs when he isn’t elsewhere
attending to his own private
business. The Federation was
hatched to save Australia from
something or other in January.
1917. In the long, fateful inter-
val, what, sign has this body of
political hybrids given of any
really constructive good intentions
either towards the tiller of the
soil or to those who handle the
raw material afterwards? The
Federation has never urged its
Parliamentary nominees to give
the local industrialists a show
by blocking cheap foreign manu-
factures or indicated any special
desire to help the rural pro-
ducer,_ such, for instance, as
agitating for the wheat-farmer’s
direct participation in the
national wheat-business? Yet
the concern has the effrontery,
at this late stage in Australia’s
war-crisis, to put itself in the
forefront of the advance-Austra-
lia movement and invite the
farmer and manufacturer to seek shelter
behind its banner of bosh and broken-pro-
mises.
