THE ESSENTIAL THINGS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Nothing- can be gained by leaving the essential
thing unsaid:”— America’s reply to Germany.
Y'oi ds have their place. Let free the muzzled lip
That softened truth to falsehood when it spoke.
Xm need for simpering now, nor censorship,
A spade’s a spade, a bloke is still a bloke.
Speak the essential thing and let it go ;
Tim man who fears it is our manhood’s foe.
Now let us say some things. The war is won,
Or will be ere another year has gone.
I he win-by-talk appeal is clearly done;
It is full time to get the last move on.
The ancient strife between the Outs and Ins
( an be no longer worth a row of pins.
Let us be plain. On military grounds,
They kept us in a heavy ignorance.
Rumor and falsehood went their daily rounds
To misinform us of affairs in France.
Now let us ask the one tremendous WHY
Australians fed Australians on a lie.
Let us be plain. Men we placed in power
Played to the foeman's hands by crushing speech.
They killed democracy in that red hour
When strong Australians held the deadly breach,
llt-re let the one essential be explained
hat that peculiar treason sought or gained.
Lei us be plainer still. The men we pay
To do the nation’s work usurped our place,
Set up autocracy and held a sway
That Freedom still regards as a disgrace.
Did they do this with any honest aim,
Or just to hide their idleness and shame?
The time to face the question has arrived.
No local dog-fight now could interfere
With Foch and victory, howe’er contrived.
The impudent assurance of last year
Fails at the happy word the cables bring—
Why was truth censored ?—that’s the essential thing.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.

Ela : A gi'eat institution is the Papuan
vhlage. constable, or V.C., as ho is known in the
lossession. The job is a very favored one with
the brown gentlemen, though it must bo the hi«h
social status that attracts them, for the pay is only
at the rate of 10s. a year. Brown brother falls over
himself after the position. The uniform is of
thick blue serge, crimson waist-belt, heavy leather
pouch and sheath-knife. The V.C. is issued a pair
of handcuffs but not the key. At one time the kev
was handed to him, and he found it was a great
idea, for with a little judicious “squeeze” he
amassed quite a lot of wealth. Nowadays the R.M.
keeps the key locked up in his safe, and the V.C.
has to escort to headquarters those who fall within his
clutches. The V.O.’s duties are fairly numerous.
He is usually the official chief of three or more
villages, and has the general welfare of the people
to look after, the so-called roads to attend to
( i (nigh he has the assistance of the natives for
this work), cocoanut planting to enforce, as well
as the regulations governing life and habits genev-
aJly. In Papua there is a code of regulations speci-
ally for the Native Matters Court, and these are far-
reaching in their application, from adultery to the
administration of a deceased’s estate. Wherever a
V.C. is stationed, the Government has a rest-house
built, which the constable has to keep in order.
He has to assist the travelling white man, provide
carriers for travellers, canoes and crews when re-
quired, and report every month or so to the R.M.
The V.C. has been a great success, though during the
last few years a few of them have broken the
monotony of things. One was hanged, and another
was shot for reverting to savagery and attempting
to eat his maiden aunt.
, Forty years ago the African box-
tnorn was imported into South Aus. by J E
Brown Conservator of Forests, for hedge planting!
It has been considerably used for that purpose, but
where hedges have been neglected and allowed to
produce seed the berries have been scattered and
tne plants have become a nuisance. The S.A. Par-
hament has therefore declared it a noxious weed.
V ell-kept hedges will be exempted. Prickly-pears
are also used in S.A. for hedges, windbreaks, etc.,
but seemingly the climate does not suit them as
well as as they have not become a pest.

Conger”: Argued the point with a
gohanna t’other day. Was fishing. Heard a rustle.
Looked round. Saw gohanna sneaking up tree with
my bait-bag m its mouth. Vowed revenge. Next day
kmcd three big frogs, disembowelled ’em, filled
orifice with mixture of plaster of Paris and salt.
Sewed ’em up again. Put
’em in bait-bag. Gohanna
sneaked bag again. Ran
up tree, swallowed frogs,
bag and all. Very shortly
he got a thirst up. Sneaked
down tree, ran to river
and drank enough water
to submerge Hun murder
boat. Next day gohanna
looking very sick. Swing-
ing by tail from branch of
tree as if trying to get rid
of something inside. No
result. Put his front claw
down neck, clinched on to
something and pulled hard.
No go. Came slowly
down tree. Went to fence.
Piece of wire hanging
from post. Bent end of
wire in shape of hook.
Poked hook down his
throat. Twisted his tail
around a stump, jambed
his four legs securery in
the ground. Held ‘back
in the britchen and
scratched gravel, jerked
and tugged backwards for
half an hour. At last
something white and hard
showed out of his big
mouth. Suddenly he sat
down hard, leaving a solid
lump of plaster of Paris,
Sin. long, three thick, on
end of wire. Shook him-
self, gave me a devilish
stare and slowly climbed
tree. Have decided it’s no
use trying to kill a gohanna by strategy.

Lignite”: Jock McCaffery, otherwise
Andrew James McCaffery, recently killed in France,
was champion henchman of the N. S. Wales
INorth Coast. He had the width of two
ordinary men across the shoulders and was
tall and strong' in proportion. 1 attended to report
a meeting of the employees of the Bucca Saw Mill
at which Jock was the prominent speaker. It was
decided to give each month 2 J per cent, from their
wages to the Belgian Fund, and as there
was a public holiday that week the gang
reckoned that they would work that day
and give their wages. At the momentous recruiting
meeting in Bucca Hall the big Australian mustered
seve;» or eight to enlist with him, and tried hard to
raise a squad. He was a white man right through.

At the fleurite-niin.es on Burr in jack the
metal is carted to the water's-edge for transhipment
per motor-boat. Recently a carter backed his load
too far, and horse and dray, with its load of fleurite
(worth possibly £6O), disappeared in 100 ft. or
thereabouts of water. The boss came along soon
after and found the carter enjoying a smoke. Asked
as to the whereabouts of his horse and dray he pointed
the stem of his pipe. “There’s the bubbles,” he said.
“Pei-ilia”: Will Lawson (B. 3/10/’18) is
all adrift in his application of “gone west” to Maori
mythology. While it is unquestionable that in the
Maori belief no recognised Hell such as Christians
were taught existed, Bishop Williams, in his
Dictionary of the New Zealand Language,” pub-
lished in ’4.4, gives the Maori equivalent of Hell as
“Reinga.” Naturally one has great diffidence in
eiiticising such a scholarly authority, who had a
reference library unprocurable nowadays, for it was
the undefiled classic diction of the race, flowing from
its native source. Yet before the translation of the
Bible, appeared in M.L. Hell was unknown to the
Maori,, and even to-day is only used in parrot-like
imitation of the Christian brother as a sanguinary
conge to friend or dog. Hell is untranslatable into
Maori. The Maori legend, of death and thereafter is
characteristically poetical. The body dies, the spirit
lives, and on disintegration the latter is wafted
through space to Te Rerenga Wairua (departing
place of the spirits) a once sacred spot at the ex-
treme north of the North Island. There, momen-
tarily poising on the sea-sprayed heights, the dis-
embodied. spirit plunges through the Te
Moananui a Kiwa (the South Pacific) into Te Reinga ,
the shadowland—or, again to quote Bishop William/
the “place of departed spirits.”

Azac ’: Now that various correspondents
have exhausted themselves and their knowledge in
attempting to describe a quick method of tanning fur
skins, I’ll explain how the job should be done. After
removing, the useless parts of a skin, soften it by
soaking, in order to enable all flesh and fat to be
scraped from the inside. A blunt knife will prove
useful for this purpose. Then soak the skin in warm
water for an hour. Mix water, borax, saltpetre and
Glauber salts so as to make a thin paste (the last
three substances should be in equal quantities). About
half an ounce of each ingredient will be sufficient for
an opossum-skin, and proportionately more will be
required for larger hides. When tile skin has soaked
in the warm water, lift it and spread it out flat so
that the paste may be applied with a brush to the
inside (more paste will be required for thick skins
than for thin ones). Double the skin together, flesh
side inwards, and place it in a cool place for 24
hours, at the end of which time it should be washed
cleaui and treated in the same way as before with a
mixture of loz. washing soda, }oz. borax and 2oz.
white hard soap. These must bo melted together
without being allowed to boil. The pelt should then
he folded together again and placed in a warm place
for another 24 hours. After this, dissolve 4oz. alum,
Boz. salt and 2oz. baking-soda in enough hot water
to saturate the skin (use soft water or rain water).
IVhen this is cool place the hide in it for 12 hours'
then wring and hang out to dry. The soaking ami
drying should be repeated two or three times until
the skin is soft and pliable, after which it might be
rubbed with fine sandpaper to secure a smooth finish.

THE BEAST OF PREY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rabbits were never worse than they arc in this
district. Coursing- at the plumpton is practical]v
abandoned. The rabbits have left nothing for hares.’’
—Me lb. paper.
'llic rabbit was at one time here
A creature free of blame,
So soft and gentle nought of fear
Possessed me when I came,
To sit and watch it loping by
Or peering from a tussock nigh.
I trapped it in the morning dew,
I stripped it of its skin,
And roast I had it; in a stew
I ate its kith and kin.
The rabbit now looms fearsomcly,
And in its turn ’tis eating me.
I see it larger than a bull;
ft seems to plot and plan;
Its spear-like teeth are bent to pull
The heart from out a man.
It plucks the pith from all I own
And gnaws my children to the bone.
I see the lion with no dread,
The tiger I despise,
For they are dead when they are dead
The rabbit never dies,
f shoot at one, with malice rife
The whole long hillside springs to life.
I'm going where the beaten men
In dismal rows are lain,
And nought will for my children then
But olden debts remain,
1-or where the rabbit feeds and bears
Starvation aj’e awaits the heirs.
VICTORIA.

“Gouger”; About the toughest old uut
alive lives by himself on the summit of scrub-clad
Mt. Fraser (N.Q.). He roots among the huge
boulders in the ravines for alluvial tin. All the
wild creatures are his friends, from the tiniest insect
to the big 16ft. carpet-snake. One morning recently,
while he was walking ahead of me, the old man
halted. “Go on; git out of it,” he said. A bit
mystified, I peered over his shoulder. Lying at his
bare feet, in thick heavy coils, was a big greyish-
looking snake. “Git out of it,” ordered George
again. The snake looked stonily at him. Then
Georgy lost his block. He lifted back his foot, and
kicked the snake a sounding thwack where its coils
were thickest. As the surprised reptile uncoiled,
George got in another hooray kick, this time land-
ing the big serpent among some lawyer-vine at the
side of the track. “You’ve got to let these, cows
see who’s boss at times,” the old bloke growled.
“That fellow seems to think he owns the whole
d mountain, lately.”
*-* * •
“Bill R.”: All that “M.” writes (B.
■ 3/10/’18) about the kurrajong- being ornamental and
useful is indisputable, but has one serious detriment.
Ornamental trees should be rapid growers, and the
kurra.iong lags even more slowly than Hughes’s ship-
building scheme. This defect, however, may be over-
come by transplanting the mature tree. Grub him
out any old way. Lop off roots that run far and
superfluous branches and transport the trunk to its
new address, taking care not to seriously mutilate the
bark. All that is wanted then is a hole in the
planet and guy-ropes to hold the vegetable up until
it takes root. Kurrajongs so transplanted in a year
or two become beautifully foliaged and appear to
grow more luxuriantly than in the situation where
they started as seedlings.
“H.V.E.”: Have found the large domed
incubator of the lyre-bird on the ground among ferns
(usually near thy margins of mountain creeks), high
up on cliff-ledges or isolated boulders (these are
favorite sites), on the top of stumps (once on the
top of a sawn stump destitute of cover and within
20 yards of a main road), in open forest, perched
on fallen trees, at the butt of a tree-fern, in the top
of ditto, on a canopy of bush-lawyer and other vines,
in the fork of a tree about 18 feet from the ground,
and in a large tree-hollow. Greg. Mathews, by the
way, once remarked that since the advent of foxes
the lyre-bird had changed its ground-nesting habit
and began building on sttimps and other elevated
places; but as a matter of fact the bird camped on
stumps, rocks, etc., long before the introduction of
the fox. The lyre-bird, though a shy fowl generally,
loses its timidity when a chick occupies the nest —
that is, the lady lyre-bird does so. While I was
photographing a nest the proprietress scratched about
contentedly within four feet ef the camera. But
whenever the fluffy and sharp-clawed chick inside
the nest began the exercise of its high G, the mother
bird came fussing around my legs. Meanwhile the
old man, perched on a log, surveyed operations from
a safe distance.
* * * *
“Pick”: Though a somewhat unwieldy-
looking fish when towed ashore the porpoise is the
marine athlete. He swims rings around the fastest
vessels afloat, and when he abandons his customary
lurching movement through the water, either to
set out in pursuit of prey or to escape threatened
danger, he becomes a whirlpool of velocity. It is
when rough seas batter the shores that his club
Holds its sports meeting. Ranks of them ride
abreast on the incoming billows, racing, until whirl-
ing sands among the breakers warn them of the
shallows. Then they somersault backwards and
disappear, to rush in again on the crest of
the next giant roller. They are mighty hurdlers,
too. In single file dozens of them will often follow
each other over a rock that -stands five or six feet
out of the water, and with such precision that
often three or four will be in the air at the one
time. Anyway that’s what King Billy told me when
he was a small boy.
“Jay Pay”: At last a type of bovine has
been discovered that is practically tick-resistant.
This is the zebu or strain, derived from
India. I believe this strain is also diffused over
China, the east coast of Africa, etc. At the last
Townsville show a pen of these cattle was exhibited.
They had the zebu’s distinctive hump. W. McDowall,
of Christmas Creek station, bred them, and thev
were a Hereford-Devon cross, crossed with a
Brahmin bull. There is no fault to be found with
the quality of the meat. One of this pen, a cow,
when dressed weighed 8001 b., and I am sure the
bullocks would weigh much heavier. It is claimed
they would thrive along a bridle-track. They are
very wild and fast, and in hilly or rough country
would take some yarding.

“Syd Ney”: It is wonderful how birds and
animals get used to the unnatural and to them
unexplainable sound of gunfire. In many parts of
the Front if is quite common to see hares, part-
ridges and pheasants close to the line, seeming
to thrive in spite of the risks they run of sudden
extinction, llats, of course, are a permanent pest,
and grow to an extraordinary size on the hideous
food they find in those quarters. These animals are
often gassed and frequently stand stupidly to be
kicked to death bj soldiers. Horses become so
accustomed to gunfire that thej r know as much
about it as their drivers, and can tell whether
a shell is going to land near them. I once
saw a hare meet his finish in queer circumstances
near a once-beautiful but now sadly-battered town in
Northern France. He found his way into a field
populated by a regiment of Tommies. Somebody
raised the news, and in about two minutes the
whole battalion was in full tear after Bunny. He
led them round the paddock, causing a good many
casualties, and finished up gloriously with the whole
regiment piled atop of him. How far his mutilated
carcase went round among the successful, hunters I
don’t know. I was lucky to get away with a
starboard paw.
“Pick”: Bingfii first shapes out his boom-
erang flat. In that form, though a handy implement
fo- straight throwing, it will not “come back.” To
the most flattened end, by alternately soaking the
timber in water, then, while wet, heating it over a
fire, and frequently pressing upon it with his foot,
the armorer gives a slight twist, ending with a
lateral curve. Over this operation he will spend
hours, until, by trial shots, he has proven the weapon
to his satisfaction. The boomerang intended to
return is never thrown straight at the object, but
is sent on its way with a right or left curve to its
flight. Air resistance against the flattened screw-
blade causes the timber to whirl rapidly and con-
stantly “Screw away from a direct path. Thrown
with sufficient vigor, it thus circles itself back. It
may even pass two or three times around the thrower.
“T.S.” : In my district, Greenvale(Vic.),an
insect —I believe it is a relation of the gum-leaf
moth —is attacking the trees, and it is stripping
the leaves off 1 el-gums a century old, 10 say
nothing of young saplings. The sap of the vegetables
attacked seems to be drying up. I have known of
the existence of the enemy for about 30 years, but
it has increased enormously hereabouts within the
last two years. I don’t know if it attacks fruit-trees,
but if it does I reckon it will cause orehardists to
use some ragtime language.
“Eddyson”: This is how Olliver told the
story: “T’ show yeh wot a dirty seoundril that
McPhillamy is. We was workin’ a few acres iv
alluvial 30ft. down on the bit iv flat ’tween the hill
’n’ the creek there et Piedish. ’Twas Tommy Dow
discovered it, ’n’ he took McPhillamy in. They was
a coupler weeks ahead iv me ’n’ Peterson, but we
got the nex’ claim, ’n’ fair belted our shirts off t’
bottom ’n’ tear the wash out, me alwiz bein’ iv a
suspicionable nature so fur ez ginger-’eaded Scotch-
men is concerned. I didn’t like this McPhillamy;
not that he ever done me no ’arm, but he was
ginger, ’n’ Scotch, ’n’ mean about given credit et the
shanty he run up where his pub now is; so when
they’re haulin’ wash I slips along now ’n’ agin iv
nights, ’n’ ’elns meself to the fill iv a pillow-slip
’r so from their paddock. I stows it under me bunk,
’n’ the missus puddles it doorin’ the day. They was
oira. nice bit iv a gutter, ’n’ one way ’n’ another me
’n’ the missus cleans up eleven ounces ’n’ a few odd
’weights out iv McPhillamy’s dirt, ’n’ I’m foelin’
real frien’ly with meself, him bein Scotch ’n’ ginger.
All this time our claim ain’t pannin’ out too bad, ’n’
we're workin’ towards Dow ’n’ Mack’s pegs all We
know how. We broke through into them one
Chewsdee, ’n’ what yer think? That cow McPhillamy
’ad worked fully three feet of our stuff along the ’ole
length iv the boldin’! Of course I jumps into him
’n’ he outs me with the butt iv a shovel, ’n’ then
there's the law. We’re fer gaolin’ Mack ’n’ Dow ez
thievin’ Scotch scoundrels, apart from gettin’ our
own back; but when we’d gone to no end iv
expense, hirin’ iv lawyers ’n’ what not, up conies
McPhillamy t’ me, ’n’ he sez: ‘Olliver,’ sez he, ‘yer
a man of no hearrt ’n’ no gratitude. Wad yeh’no be
contentid V braw thankfu’ t’ the men who mined
yer own dirt for ye? Dinna ye be too impetuous
wi 1 the law, mon. ’Tis a canny bad plaything, ye
ken, ’n’ there’s five gude witnesses ’n’ true I have
that ye stole yer own wash awa’ as fast as we could
raise it’! What yell think iv that? That low
’ound’s bin watchin’ all the time, lettin’ a man go
to the trouble iv shakin’ his own stuff night after
night fer a matter iv weeks. Didja ever ’ear iv a
dirtier trick ? Iv course me mate Peterson roun’s on
me. He ain’t content with breakin’ me nose across
but must have his cut iv the gold I bin thinkin’ all’
this time was ’onestly mine, ’n’ calculates his ’art
out at twelve ounces, that infernal McPhillamy
havin’ got away with about twice ez much ez I
recovered. I’m out with law expenses besides, about
forty five quid, ’n’ we done nothin’ after all but
what was right ’n’ lawful. That’s a good many
years ago, but to this day I mistrusts a ginger-’eaded
Scotchman wherever I sees him.”
“D.F.”: Vagrant travellers have reported
at dubious times that they found tobacco in use
amongst native tribes far inland in New Guinea-
tribes that had never seen a white man and had no
trade lelations to speak of with the coast natives. It
tobacco is not native to New Guinea it seems to
have got there nearly as early as it was introduced
to Europe. The Dutchmen* Seliouten and Le Maire
visited the ssuth-east coast of N.G. in 1610, and it
is alleged that they found the natives had then
plenty of smoke-pipe.
“S.”: The cook in the mill hands’ camp
at Bumblefoot was a long, lean, immaculate old
gentleman who had been an actor once, when fancv
cooking was a hobby, and who now manipulated
corned-beef and cabbage, and rolled roly-poly or
rounded off a plain plum-duff in a manner that
endeared him to the hearts of men who had long
since abandoned the hope that these things could
be made to rest at ease and abide with mutual
satisfaction in the interior of humans. “Toff” Hal-
liwell also recited a good deal of Shakspeare to the
camp at odd times, “Othello” being a great favorite
of his. When Easter week came five of the boys
went to Melbourne, and one night found themselves
in a theatre awaiting a performance of the nature
of which they had small understanding. The plav
had been in progress about half an hour when
Tommy (most impressionable of the herd) arose in
his place in the gallery, dealt each of his comrades
an apple, and said: “Now, one—two—three, and get
to him!” He threw his own apple, and missed the
popular actor by a hair. “Never mind, we’ll wait
for him,” he said in a whisper like cement falling
into a tank. “He’s the cow what ruined poor old
‘Toff’ Halliwell’s life. It’s that lago what Halli-
well’s always goin’ on about!”

The old question about the birth of a
maisupial has been revived in Adelaide Register,
and several bushmen cling to the teat theory. D.
E. Laurie, a Government expert, summing up, give'
judgment:—•
. marsupials there is insufficient nourishment
in the uterus, and the young are born in a very
immature condition. .. .How can a bushtnan or, for
That matter, the average man in the street appreci-
ate. the facts of science? Our friends who are so
positive probably do not appreciate the help of a
good modern microscope, backed up by skill in
technique... .Ver v likely, if one of our practical
friends did a little histological as well as biochemi-
cal study of this curious junction of the partially-
deveioped foetus to_ the mammary gland, he would find
'till another point in the marvellous chain of evolution,
lie should first of all study the intimate arrangements
for nourishing (in utero) the young of placentals;
then he would see the primitive analogy. The
young are, of course, transferred to the pouch (or
marsupium) by the mother. The idea of the forma-
tion of the embryo on the teat is too ridiculous for
words.
Anybody" in Australia may argue further about this
matter in The Bulletin’s old friend The Watchman.

The gloomy news comes from Adelaide
that carbon dioxide is being used with great success
to kill weevils in the accumulating wheat which
can’t be disposed of for want of ships. It is pleas-
ing to read that the weevil is being slain, but it
sounds as if we might have to eat gassed bread for
quite a long time.
FIDDLIN’ ’ROUND.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
'Tis too wet to work to-day—
All the world’s a mess of slush;
In the cowyard poddies play
In the sloppy, sticky mush.
An’ the dairymaids look glum
Fiddlin’ ’round !
Dad’s a heap o’ nerves just now—
Can't get any ploughin’ done.
Says the weather is a cow ;
An’ of harvest there’ll be none.
Says his luck’s in Kingdom Come
Fiddlin’ ’round.
Phil is trackin’ after Sis —
Chirps to call her ev’ry night—
Out to steal a squeeze or kiss
Seems to be his sole delight.
We have watched ’em (very dumb)
Fiddlin’ ’round.
Fiddlin’ ’round while Joy takes wing,
Fiddlin’ till our cake is dough.
What’s the use of anything?
What’s the answer ere you go—■
Fiddlin’ ’round?
VICTORIA.

“L.” : Northcliffe is giving every encourage-
ment to the freelance journalists of London.
The latest outbreak of his restless enterprise
is the setting apart every morning of a couple
of columns in the Daily .Mail for short
articles and sketches contributed solely by
outsiders. The minimum payment is three
guineas, and the length of article must not
exceed 400 or 500 words. By way of help to
amateurs and beginners he has published a
list of “useful suggestions” :
Avoid being local. Choose a subject with a wide
and general appeal. Always touch the personal
note. Never forget the feminine interest. Women
form the bulk of newspaper readers, and even men
enjoy reading about feminine topics. Under present
conditions military and naval subjects treated with
the inside knowledge and accuracy born of personal
experience have the best chance of “catching on.”
Always give plenty of thought to the first sentence
of article, story or sketch. Success or failure fre-
quently depends on the degree of interest that the
first sentence excites. It should be arresting and
contain one gripping fact. Conclude with a sob or
a smile. Marshal your information in attractive
order. Be simple. Use short sentences and avoid
long pars. Have copy neatly typed.
Wonder where he got the idea that women
form the bulk of newspaper readers?
“II.”: The latest number of United
Empire, the magazine of the Royal Clinical
Institute, contains the full text of the paper
on “Overseas Poetry” recently read by Her-
bert Warren, late Professor of Poetry at
Oxford. He says that: —
Australian poetry really began in 1842 with the
publication of the first volume of verse by Henry
Parkes, that great poetic Imperialist, the protagonist
of Australian Federation.
Charles Harpur is declared to be "the
first native-born Australian poet.” Then
Warren explains that “the gold rush in
the ’fifties brought a number of poets, includ-
ing R. H. Horne, Woolner, -Michael, Henry
Kingsley and Adam Lindsay Gordon.” It
was not gold that sent Gordon to Australia.
But let Warren get on : —•
Gordon, the gentleman-rider and pugilist, became
the best-known poet in Australia, some say the most
characteristic and natural poet of his adopted home.
Gordon played many parts in Australia, but
this is the first time I have seen him appear
in print as a pugilist.
“Jacko” : Recently-deceased Davy Buchan-
an’s Nation was not the first news-
paper to be published west of the Blue
Mountains. It didn’t appear till somewhere
in the ’sixties, whereas the Bathurst Advo-
cate had been born long before that. Ben-
jamin Isaacs started this paper in February,
1848, but was reckless enough to libel some-
one, which involved the surrender of £4O and
the prompt printing of the final issue of the
sheet. The Bathurst Free Press took up
the running with the material in 1849, with
William Farrand in charge of the pay-book,
and did <mod business for many years.
Bathurst later had three panel’s all in the
boom. Now it can just manage to support
two.
They are beginning to feel the jolt of war
on Uncle Sam’s Inky Way. Latest issue of
the American Newspaper Directory re-
cords the blow-out of GIG publications. The
price of paper and the enormously increased
postal rates have knocked out the profit.
Fred Broomfield, that picturesque way-
farer on the Inky track, has been on his back
in Prince Alfred Hospital for months past
through a fracture of the right leg. The
crack is mending slowly, and the proprietor
thereof hopes to get up in a week or two
and go in pursuit of a few good books, which
don’t find their way to the hospitals as weary
patients wish.
A Satchel of Books
The Oxford Book of Australasian Verse, chosen by
Walter Murdoch (H. Milford). —This book has been
compiled in Australia and printed in England to fill
a glace in a well-known series of anthologies. There
are already six or seven collections of'*Australasian
verse on the market, and the new one fails to justify
itself by superiority to its predecessors. The absence
of any of the work of Brunton Stephens, Essex Evans,
Boake, Farrell, Daley, Lawson, Paterson, Ogilvie,
O’Hara and Bayldon is a fatal defect, for which, no
doubt, the owners of copyright are responsible ; /.ora
Cross and Leon Gellert may have appeared too late
for the compiler. However, there was a gap in the
series, and it had to be filled, so a number of minor
writers were anthologised who surely would have
been left to bloom unseen if there had not been such
a. large vacancy in the Oxford editor’s collection, llis
order of merit is roughly indicated by the fact that Gor-
don and O’Dowd are each represented by eight pieces ;
Kendall, Harpur, Gay and Shaw Neilson by six ; Miss
Baughan, Miss Mackay, Louis Esson, F. Williamson
and A. Maquarie by five each. O’Dowd has been too
generously treated; Rodcric Quinn's four poems
might have been added to with advantage. Arthur
Maquarie (a pen-name, by the way) left Australia
at an early age, and his writings have not been in-
fluenced to any appreciable extent by the country of
his birth; lie therefore'should not* have been in-
cluded. While we take credit for the literature pro-
duced in Australia by Gordon, Clarke, Daley and
others who were born elsewhere, we cannot also class
as Australian the work of the native-born who emi-
grate early and have not been affected much by Aus-
tralian conditions or atmosphere. Although there are
many good Australians not amongst those nresent in
the Oxford book, it contains much that will be fresh
and interesting to readers of Australasian verse. The
editor, now Professor of English in Perth University,
used to write the weekly “Elzevir” column in the
Argus, and kept a friendly eye on Australian books,
lie could have written an attractive survey of Aus-
tralian poetry if copyright restrictions had not made
it difficult to avoid drawing attention to his losses ;
but as his book is intended primarily for readers
abroad he should have given them some information
about his selected poets. One or two of them are
unknown even in Australasia.
Sir I.‘umbras at the Ford, by D. 11. Broster
(John Murray, London). A rattling yarn of the
English military expedition to France in 1795. The
book is well written. Our copy is from Angus and
Robertson.
Cinderella’s Suitors, by Isabel Maude Peacocke
(Ward, Lode and Co., London). A novel by a well-
known Maoriland author who has already several
books to her credit. The yarn is about a type-
writing girl who comes into a fortune. Her aunt has
tied up the cash in such a way that she loses it all
if she gets married. This chases away all undesirable
lovers. But the right man, a poor but decent writer
of stories, takes the chances and gets the maiden.
Well written and thoroughly readable.
Business, Robbery, etc.
Two commercial visitors now in Melbourne
are F. E. Jocelyn, of Hongkong, and S. H.
Collett, of India, with experience of Ceylon,
Malaya and the Dutch Archipelago. Jocelyn
emphasises the Japanese capture of Chinese
trade. The Japs, they state have the
cotton trade and give John what he
wants, not what Nippon wants to sell.
Import of Australian goods to Japan has
largely increased, especially flour, tinned
meats, butter, jams and cereals. Yet Aus-
tralian exporters are hot careful enough in
regard to Japan, and careless to China, which
is supposed not. to be so critical. Collett
says: “It is to-morrow’s business that
counts.” Business for the asking, if firms
go out and get it. Also he confirms the
universal suspicion that trade missions run
by civil servants are not much good.
* * * #
“T. 0.1.” : I have an ancient Sydney curio,
in a fine state of preservation. It is a “Table
of Factors for Interest at 8 and 10 per cent.,
by Joseph Hyde Potts, of the Bank of New
South Wales, Sydney, 1821.” He was
accountant to the bank, which was then in
Macquarie-plaee, the house of Mrs. Reibey.
Potts Point and Potts Hill were named after
him.
* * * *
Some of the land purchases in Victoria for
subdivision among returned soldiers seem to
require a stack of explanation. The items
include four acres at £55 per acre ; six acres
at £4l 13s. 4d. per acre; IGO acres at £l7
10s., and such-like. Even the biggest of
these couldn’t stand very severe cutting-up.
* * # #
“0.L.”: Manufactured woollens being
scarce in Australia and staggeringly expen-
sive, the Defence Department gets its home-
supplies where it can. This is part of the
label on a rug issued to a returned soldier
camped on a job at Eildon Weir (Vic.) :
“Wool, cotton and shoddy. Made in Switzer-
land.” Imagine it! This soldier, coming home
to the great • wool-growing country he has
helped to save, takes an out-of-the-city job
under primitive living-conditions, and wraps
himself o’ nights in the faked product of a
foreign factory manned (probably) by Ger-
man-speaking operatives. We can and do
make fine rugs here, of course; but, so long
as Australian enterprise is cramped by the
fear of post-war dumpings from abroad, our
woollen factories will fail to multiply. The
Labor party didn’t sink the fiscal issue for
nothing.
“G. Arden Hill”: Re the meat scrim-
mage. If the Government would erect a
weighbridge and fenced race at Home-
bush, and weigh every beast for sale, the
trouble would be less heated. The buyer
would then know how much beef he was paying
for and whether or not there was a mar-
gin of profit at the proclaimed (and ignored)
retail prices. The idea is not impracticable.
When it was suggested, about 20 years ago,
to weigh every skip of coal at. the pit head,
there was a hubbub about decrease of out-
put, loss of trade, etc. Yet it is done to-day
without the slightest inconvenience to the
industry. With the weight of a bullock be-
fore him and a ready-reckoner in his hand,
a buyer could nod with a tranquil mind.

Commendable example of commercial
candor from an Adelaide newspaper
(T5/10/’18) :
Stocks must g 0.... The house for bargains.... A
few examples.... Special value in good American
undershirts and pants, 2s. lid., worth 3s.
Thank Murphy, there’s one honest trader in
S. Australia '.
British West Africa supplies large
quantities of cotton to British markets,
and there is likely to be a big yearly
increase when the ease with which cara-
vcnica, or tree-cotton, can be cultivated is
fully recognised. This cotton is perennial,
and requires re-setting only every eight or
nine years, a great advantage in labor-saving
over "the annual cotton-plant. The lint is a
fine long staple and commands a ready sale
on European markets. With the high prices
now ruling it is worth about Is. a lb., but
should U.S. cotton fall as low as sd. this
lot would be worth 9d. Germany has largely
cultivated the caravonica tree-cotton in
the tropical and semi-tropical colonies she
once held, and many of the fine dress
materials, on which the Hun specialised,
contained large proportions of the article,
as did blankets and other so-called woollen
materials. Jas. Halcrow, F.R.G.S.. writing
in London Times, considers that Queensland
(and no doubt other places in Australia)
is suitable for caravonica, and urges the
Commonwealth to help England by plant-
ing it. There is a tremendous shortage of
cotton now, and it looks as if the Common-
wealth might be without fabrics altogether
it' they are not made locally. Anyhow, it’s
certain that we can’t get the industry started
without the raw material.
* * * *
Samuel Barnes, Vic. Minister for Mines,
has advanced £lOO to assist in taking out
clays at Ballarat suitable for making “white
pottery.” The enterprise is not a private
one, the prime mover being the Ballarat
School of Mines, which lately carried out
highly-successful experiments in the making
of household ware from district clays. As
imported teacups and dinner-plates have
reached top-rung prices, and the Australian
householder has to depend chiefly on rubbish
from the “East,” Samuel’s opening up of the
money-bag seems hardly adequate to the
occasion. Victoria, with its provenly-good
chances of being able to supply Australia
with essential crockery, should be given every
encouragement by the Government to the
producers to go ahead, even to the extent of
appealing to the Federal authorities to read
up Hughes’s passionate remarks about Pro-
tection.
A recent Senate return showed that
during the year ended June 30, 1917, Aus-
tralia imported from Japan dutiable goods
worth £2,893,971, as compared with £634,566
in 1913. Chief items were: Textiles,
£1,480,779 (1913. £415,280).; metals and
machinery, £197,884 (1913, £7163) ; earthen-
ware, £263,200 (1913, £20,553) ; jewellery.
£123,039 (1913, £19,140) ; and woodwork,
£181,754 (1.913, £34,580). The daily papers
frequently assure us that the shipping
scarcity and other war-results have given a
stupendous fillip to Australian industry.
Well, the. above figures show where the s.f.
really is. #
“C.P.”: Did you ever wonder why ten-
ders for Government contracts are so much
higher than those for private contracts of the
same nature? Reason is that the Govern-
ment standards are different to the ordinary
ones. If the Government lets a contract,
say, for a house, all doors, windows, etc.,
are of different heights and widths to the
regular ones turned out by joinery firms.
Hence all these fittings have to be specially
made. The Public Works Department is a
contrary animal. # #
“Edo”; The recent trouble on Federal
Government's east-to-west railway was not
caused through engine-drivers’ unjust de-
mands. During construction of the line the
men were distinctly promised a rise in pay
when the line became a going concern. Then
when passenger trains started, small-headed
officials tried to get out of that promise on
the quibble that the trains were not ‘ ex-
presses,” and this in face of the fact that
they are the fastest and most difficult to run
in the Commonwealth. Then, again, most of
the drivers with their families live out on the
Nullabor plains, remote from all comfort, and
they receive less than drivers in closely-
settled regions. The cumbersome Arbitra-
tion Act could not deal with their case.
Even if the steam-valve men had not come
out, the traffic staff would have done so
and hung up things, for almost similar
reasons. The only way to make things go
peaceably is for someone qualified to pick up
an axe and go after the pin-headed under-
officials who ran Commissioner Bell and his
Minister into trouble, and. incidentally, the
whole community.
* * * *
“II.II.” writes'; —
I submit that there is a great deal too much
circumlocution about the raising of wages. The
piecemeal way of doing it is ridiculously expensive
and keeps a whole herd of barristers and solicitors
and judges grazing in fat pastures of litigation. I
believe the legal herd is the .only one that is increas-
ing, and it can’t be frozen for export. Rather it
freezes its clients. A Court has decided that £3
per week is a minimum living wage for an adult
male worker. If the cost of living goes on rising
there may be another half-dozen pettifogging
increases. I suggest that the community looks ahead
for once and fixes the minimum at £6. It would
be necessary to raise the fixed prices of local com-
modities, of course, for a £6 man couldn’t supply
bread at the same cost as a £3 man —he couldn’t
grow or mill the wheat so cheaply, or carry it by rail,
or build the mill or the railway or the road leading
to the railway or the bakehouse so cheaply, or hake
or deliver or construct the delivery-cart, or feed the
delivery-horse so cheaply. Still the question of prices
would be a mere matter of adjustment. The aggre-
gate value of goods produced would naturally go up,
for the value of goods is, barring a mere fraction, the
cost of the labor employed in making and handling
them. The increase would look good in the statistics.
Certainly we couldn't make any corresponding increase
in the price of goods sent abroad. They would have
to be sold 4>t the buyer’s figure as usual, but the
price of goods used locally could be put up something
extra to cover the loss involved in selling cheaply
to the foreigner-—that is to say, exports could be sub-
sidised. It stands to reason, that our £6 a week
industries couldn’t manufacture under present con-
ditions in competition with the imported goods of
£2 a week outsiders, and there are always some £2
or even £1 a week outsiders. Probably if we had
the best industrial organisation and the best machin-
ery in the world the trouble might be got over, for
organisation and machinery can move mountains;
anyhow the case would have to be considered. To
facilitate consideration 1 would give every adult
citizen two votes instead of one, and I would give
him twice as many members to vote for. And if
there was any further scarcity after that, the only
device would be to push the living-wage rate a
little higher. The essential is to keep it always
ahead of the cost of living, instead of lagging behind
part of the time and coming up to the scratch in
spasms.
* * * *
“Eustace” : The other night the G.-G. and
his Lady Helen arrived at remote Swan Hill
(Vic.). At the inevitable reception his Ex.,
after saying his piece, withdrew. At the
local post-office a country youth sat bewail-
ing his luck in being detained to send press
telegrams to the citv papers. About 10.30
p.m. an affable stranger strolled in and asked
some questions as to the amount of press
wires to be transmitted. The youth, having
a stern regard for his departmental oath of
secrecy and being in a bad temper, answered
shortly and gave no information. This
failed, however, to ruffle the amiable visitor,
and after some efforts at small talk he de-
parted still cheerful. It was not until later
that the lad discovered he had disciplined
the G.-G. It quite cheered up his evening.
THE PAPER FAMINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A paper famine’s in the land,
The sufferers are many.
Their woes I hardly understand;
I’m not beset bv any.
If paper’s scarce enough maybe
The time extremely close is
When journal owners all must see
Creation’s news is served to me
In homoeopathic doses.
My “daily” will be so reduced
In minutes five I’ll con it,
And never poet will we boost
Who dares exceed a sonnet.
The “leaders” all will shrink until
They occupy inch places,
And scribes need strain no more their skill
To force an inch of thought to fill
The paper’s empty spaces.
But most it pleased me, I declare,
To note what this curtailment
Of paper uses everywhere
To each domestic male meant;
Behold the wretch our 'peace who kills,
And fills our lives with dolor,
Presenting his eternal bills,
Must now let up, or flout his jills,
The national comptroller!
VICTORIA.

The way of the producer of Australian
picture-films appears to be tolerably thorny.
On July 25, the Chief Secretary office, Syd-
ney, notified the proprietors of the photo-
play, “The Woman Suffers,” to release the
film. The statement, in writing, was to the
effect that “no objection will be offered” to
the exhibition of the picture. After being
shown for a couple of months, however, the
picture was withdrawn by order of the same
office. No reason was given; it just went out
flop. About the picture itself there could
easily be two opinions. It is fine photo-
graphy, picturing a yarn as suggestive
as the typical kind which comes from
U.S., and is shown frequently. But
the merits of the picture itself do not enter
into the question at all as regards the action
of the censorship. Either the film was bad
and the censors failed for months in their
duty to the public in allowing it to be shown,
or the film Is good and the censors have made
a mess of their job. Whichever way it is
looked at the censors’ action must stand
condemned. In South Australia, Vic-
toria and Queensland the picture has been
exhibited. The main point for Australian
cos. which are prepared to put cash into ven-
tures of this kind is that the written autho-
rity of the cino. censor lias now become a
mere “scrap of paper,” to be torn up at any
time. The officials concerned will give no
reasons for their actions.
“Melb”: “Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” at the
Princess’s, drew two overflowing houses the
other Saturday afternoon and night. The

“Shanghai,” when the mail left, had made
a big hit at Drury Lane, and the bookings
of seats on a prodigious scale for many
months ahead indicate that Australia is not
likely to see Dorothy Brunton again for a
long time to come. The Stage contem-
plates the likelihood of the show being run
right through the customary panto season. But
that would be a violent and revolutionary
break with the long-standing traditions of old
Drury. The national theatre without its
centuries-old Christmas panto is almost un-
thinkable, but “Shanghai” undoubtedly pos-
sesses some of the most attractive features
and elements of the Christmas fare relished
by generations of Cockneys.
“H.”: When the Russian Ballet comes
to London there is always a rush
of fashion and aristocracy to the boxes.
Some of the Asquiths, patrons of the
Arts, turned up to the Coliseum on
the first night, with Viola Tree, Thomas
Beecham and other celebrities. Next
to the opera the ballet has most attrac-
tion for the world of fashion ; but in the land
of the Bolsheviks dancing is universally
popular. Even the orthodox Marxists have
subsidised the ballet throughout
Russia. The Russians take
dancing seriously. Madame
Lydia, who has just come from
Spain, said Madrid was too stiff
and Barcelona too commonplace
to appreciate dancing. Seville,
she said, was a city of art. It
had a wise appreciation and fine
original dancing of its own.
London, not Lydia, was really
on trial. But London is at the
old place on the atlas.
“S.S.”: How came the cen-
sors to pass “Cleopatra,” the
film in which Theda Bara was
starring in Sydney and Mel-
bourne a couple of weeks back?
And since the censors did not
deem it essential to blot out
the bare places of America’s
more celebrated “vampire” in
these screenings, what is the
limit of undress permissible in
the opinion of those very excel-
lent gentlemen who are en-
trusted with the duty of running
the moral rule over the fig-
leaves in our picture-plays?
“Cleopatra,” as presented by
Theda, is merely a more or less
feasible excuse for the removal
of garbage. The garb Theda
begins with is so visionary that
sensitive spectators are re-
assured at the jump with a con-
viction that, at any rate, she
can go no further in the display
of h unpin cuticle unadorned.
But Miss Bara gives a barer
Cleopatra with every succeeding
vision, until a sort of nervous
disorder takes possession of the
audience. I would be the last
to interfere with a poor actress’s
efforts to earn a bare living, but
I do think the dress comp-
troller in America has made a grievous mis-
take in endeavoring to effect the whole war
savings of a nation of 100 millions out of
the wardrobe of one picture-artist, and a
medium-sized artist at that.
“D.”: We have had of late years
quite a number of plays manufactured from
more or less po'pular short stories, and not
one that I can recollect that was even a com-
mendable play, considered from any reasonable
artistic standard ; also they have a peculiar
resemblance. In each we get two or more well-
marked characters (“Business Before Pleas-
ure,” like “Potash and Perlmutter,” is a fair
example) about whom the interest circulates
and upon whom the dramatist places entire
dependence. The effort is always to weave
the attractive character figures in popular
sketches into a dramatic whole, and the
Sherlock Holmes play is the only one 1 re-
call for the moment that embeds the well-
advertised hero in a story that has even
interest of its own apart altogether from
dramatic qualities. The “Business Before
Pleasure” plot is not a bit better than those
utilised to rope Australian short stories for
theatrical purposes. That is to say, it is
pitifully weak. Why attempt plot in such
cases? The interest is wholly in the
comedy work of those characters whose suc-
cess in the literary field has marked them
down for stage work. It cannot be abso-
lutely essential from the producer’s point of
view’ to lift those characters out of a good
setting and dump them into a bad one.
“S.T.S.”: The extension of easy divorce
in the U.S.A. has given quite a new tone to the
drama, especially the- film-drama. A film-
man can now quite honorably propose mar-
riage to another man’s wife. Under the
old arrangement, when the husband over-
heard his friend saying, “You are too good
for him. Fly with me!” he reached for a
revolver. Nowadays, he starts guiltily and
soliloquises, “Gee! She’ll do it, too!” and
reaches for a diamond necklace for his
dissatisfied baby doll. The old cynicism
about a man not running after a tram-car,
after he has once caught it, has lost all
point. The position to-day is that he is
liable to be chucked out unless he keeps on
paying his fair.
At a recent lunch in London, indulged in
by the British Screen Club, it was claimed
that the cinema is a British invention, the
first patent for a “movie” having been
obtained by one Greene in 1889. The first
film was produced on paper immersed in
paraffin wax, which made it semi-transparent.
It depicted scenes around Hyde Park Corner,
London.
“Keith Kennedy”: There are various
ways of coaxing the public in. Recently in
our country tour, four men were standing
in front of the hall discussing whether they
would go into the show or play billiards.
The house was not filling up too quickly, so
the ticket-seller, who was Scotch, got
anxious. He heard the man who was most
in favor of billiards addressed as Fred.
Beckoning him over he said, “A young lassie
was lookin’ for ye a while ago ; she thought
ye were in the hall, and went inside.” “Was
she dressed in white?” asked Fred inno-

HE: “Yes; perhaps she would be better in

Gently. “Yes,” said the bird-limer. Fred
went back to his party. Presently I heard
him say, “I’m goin’ in. Liz is inside.” That
Settled it. The whole four lined up.
“Snell” : Encouraged by the big manager’s
public announcement of the fact that he was
simply famishing for Australian plays, I had
sent him a little four-act comedy of my own,
and he had written expressing himself de-
lighted with the thing. This character was
“superb,” that situation was “intensely
dramatic,” the dialogue was “uncommonly
clever,” but certain alterations in scenes
would have to be made to adapt the play for
his purposes. Would I call? Would I what?
I called. “Oh, yes, about your play, The eh
—eh—eh —The eh” To his secretary :
“Damn it, wake up! What’s the name of
the play?” “‘The Man Hunters,’” said the
private sec. dubiously. “Oh, yes, of course,
‘The Man Hunters.’ A fine thing, young
man; a good plot, good characterisation, the
talk is easy and natural. That character of
the eh —eh —of the—eh” He was get-
ting quite angry with the secretary. “Oh,
the Dutch beach-comber,” said the sec. “Yes,
to be sure, the Dutch beach-comber I thought
would suit T to the ground. I would
like to produce this play, The--eh—eh—
the” “ ‘The Man Hunters” prompted
the sec. “ ‘The Man Hunters.’ It should be
produced, and it will be produced, I’m sure,
but for many reasons we cannot handle it
just now. You will take it with you? Eh—
eh. yes, good day.” I was handed a script of
“The Man Hunters.” “But this is not my
play,” I said. “Not your play?” “No. My
play has a different title.” “Oh, very well,”
ejaculated the big manager, losing patience.
“There’s a basket of them in the corner.
Dammittall, help yourself!”
Henry Hamilton, joint author of a long
series of Drury Lane spectacular dramas,
most of which have been staged in Sydney
and Melbourne, passed out recently. “The
Derby Winner,” “Cheer, Boys, Cheer,” “Sins
of Society,” “Marriage of Mary Fair,” “The
Whip,” “Sealed Orders” and “The Best of
Luck” were some of his works. His last
piece, written in collaboration with G. R.
Sims and dealing with the Navy in war-
time, is booked for early presentation at
Drury Lane. Hamilton was originally an
actor and played in the companies of Henry
Irving and Wilson Barrett.
Les. Hoffman, conductor at St. Augustine’s
Orphanage Band at Geelong (Vic.), has been
offered a billet as conductor of a well-known
Sydney band. Hoffman —a champion on his
instrument —plays the cornet in the orches-
tra of the Geelong Theatre, and is an old
boy of the Orphanage. He picked up the
baton dropped by Percy Jones, another
musical old boy, whom he succeeded.
Decima and Eddie McLean, who as chil-
dren twirled themselves in many dances
before the Australian public, are now a big
draw in the'“Yes, Uncle,” programme at the
London Shaftesbury. They were booked for
Berlin in the summer of 1914, and just
managed to get clear before the curtain went
up on the war drama. Another old friend
headlined in London is Ivy Shilling. Her
stage partner is a Russian, Paul Jakovlieff,
and they are on the Shaftesbury programme.
As the “Spirit of the Green Jade God,” Ivy
scintilles to Howard Carr’s music.
“Melb”: “Stage Wait” writes (B.
17/10/T8) that I am at sea in giving casts
of the “Two Orphans.” I wrote that Bellew
was the original Pierre in New York. This
was on the authority of Belasco’s Auto-
biography, in Hearst’s Magazine. “Stage
Wait” quotes a later New York cast, with
Bellew in the small part of the Chevalier.
If so he must have been comparatively old
and grey, and with the gloss and bloom gone.
Iu his younger time Bellew was a New York
matinee idol, in which capacity he succeeded
Itignold, who followed Montague. The very
popular drama of the “Two Orphans” was
frequently acted with changes of cast, and
with the same performers in different parts.
It was more familiar in Sydney than in
Melbourne.
“N”: I think “S.T.S.” (B. 10/10/TSI
need not worry as to “gairl” being a perma-
nency. The pronunciation of her is as
mutable as the lady herself. About 18 years
ago W. F. Ilawtrey brought “A Message
from Mars” to Australia, and I was engaged
to make a few remarks. I had to allude to
what, in my ignorance, I called “girl.” When
I said it that way at our first rehearsal,
llawtrey’s Etonian sensibilities got a hor-
rible jolt:. He stopped the rehearsal to make
puffed remarks about my “colonial twang,”
and to explain that the real —haw! —refined
way to allude to a girl was “gell.” And he
took care that his large assortment of over
40 actors and actorines always said “gell”
in that show.
THE VEGETABLE FAMINE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“We here,” the good old actor said
To his companions of the bar,
“Are doing nought to earn our bread.
Let’s form a co. and journey far
To play infallible ‘East Lynne,’
And gather fast the shekels in."
They answered him with bitterness,
Aud cited instances of late,
Concerning pros, who’d felt the stress
Of rural ignorance and hate,
Who had been driven from the stage
With sprouts and such like foliage.
“ ’Tis well,” the good old actor cried.
“We’ll take with us a stock of sacks,
And play the drama far and wide,
Nor sorrow at the turnip thwacks.
We’ll weep not when we feel the power
Of carrot fierce or cauliflower.
“Nay, will we not with shrew device
Incur the spite of country men,
The rich tomato to entice,
The parsnip and the lettuce? Then
We’ll send our nightly tailings down.
These things are priced as pearls in town!”

To arrive at the true net profit various small
amounts have to be deducted from the figures
in the last column. Thus last year £919(5 was
spent in turbine replacement and £4900 was
written oft' loan-flotation exes, and loan d.s-
counts and for damage settlements. The
term was full of trouble, especially the latter
portion, when the big general strike upset
everything. Fuel, for instance, cost the Depart-
ment 10.7 p.c. of the gross revenue from
lighting, etc., in 191(5; last year it absorbed
19 p.c. Electrical plant is almost unob-
tainable, and what can be got is at unheard-
of prices. Also £14,2 <2 went in allowances to
employees on active service, but as this
municipal enterprise has not to make divi-
dends for shareholders, the consumer was
not hit up to make good the heavy additional
expenditure. That is the difference between
municipal and private enterprise ; P.E. would
have pushed up rates the moment its usual
dividend was in doubt. However, the balance
of profit last year after making the sinking
fund contribution of £18,391 was insufficient
to cover the 3 p.c. on the capital expendi-
ture to be added to the deprec.atlon reserve,
as stipulated by the Council resolution of
1916.
Reserves now stand at £G54,509, made up

Liabilities are made up of deposits £13,954,
mortgage £25,247, overdraft £2161, and i 218
due to sundry creditors and for unpaid divi-
dends. Comparing last year's figures with
those for 1914 it will be seen how cautiously
the management is proceeding. Deposits
have been reduced by £3363 in the four years.
But though the amount out in advances is
less than in 1914, interest earnings (apart
from investments) have increased from £7686
for that year to £9928. What new business
has been nut on the books lately is, of course,
very profitable.

Two pitiable sights on the Murchison field
(W.A.) are the Great Fingall and Black
Range rinnacles. Plant is being steadily dis-
mantled on the former, and the only sign of
life on this once great mine is a gang loading
machinery on to railway-trucks and two or
three tributors looking for leavings. The
fine plant on the Black Range Pinnacles is
still intact, but the whole of it is about to be
offered to the highest bidder.
•**•■*»
Option hunters are beginning to take an
interest in the new find at the head of the
Kurrawang woodline (W.A.). Small quan-
tities of alluvial and reefing gold have been
obtained since the rush set in some weeks
ago. and a large number of areas with and
without prospects are on offer. The locality
is between 50 and 60 miles north-east oi
Southern Cross, whence Charlie Jones was
making in 1910 when an impassabV ssft-
lake caused Mm to turn back and discover
Bullfinch.

Australia
Efficient Lighting
Is greatly to be desired in every
home where comfort and conveni-
ence is closely studied. No more
efficient artificial light has been
invented than that produced by
QUIRK’S SAFETY
AIR GAS SYSTEM.
In addition to the light being pure
and brilliant, it is far and away
cheaper than any other form of
artificial light. The closest analysis
gives the extraordinary result of
Quirk’s System, producing a more
efficient light than acetylene at
One=sixth of the Cost.
It is a steady light; no flickering so
distressful to the eyes; no erratic
pressure that causes the light to
jump. By a new and ingenious de-
vice which has been evolved by our
engineers, steady pressure is abso-
lutely assured. In a lighting system
this is unique, and places Quirk’s
Air Gas System on a higher plane
than any other.
No Complicated Mechanism.
This device simplifies an already
simole machine. There can be no
fear of the machine getting out of
order. That is one of the great
features of Quirk’s System. Once
installed it will run smoothly for
years, requiring practically no atten-
tion.
Fool Proof.
The great fear in the minds of many
who think of artificial light is the
danger of explosion, of combustion,
leakage and one hundred and one
other troubles of a home-made light,
but with Quirk’s Safety Air Gas
System all this is removed. It is
“fool proof.” There can be no ex-
plosion, no wastage of gas, no leak-
ing, no smell. None of those
disagreeable and dangerous elements
are even remote possibilities, let
alone probabilities.
The Summer Months
are dreaded by the housewife who
has to carry on the cooking, the
ironing, etc., under the old condi-
tions where artificial heat added to
the sun made home life a burden.
Quirk’s Air Gas System does its
work without heating the atmo-
sphere. It does not add discomfort
to the kitchen. On the contrary, it
affords the housewife an opportunity
of doing her work under the most
agreeable conditions.
For Country Houses
no better system can be installed.
The maximum result is achieved
with a minimum of discomfort and
expense. Nothing to get out of
order, nothing to explode, no smell —
all clear, bright and healthy. It
can be adapted to the largest as well
as to the smallest house. If acety-
lene gas was previously installed the
existing fixtures can be utilised.
A Competent Staff
installs every plant and sees it is
working satisfactorily before hand-
ing it over. Nothing is left to
chance. If you have any doubts
write to the Quirk’s Lighting and
Engineering Ltd. for an Illustrated
Pamphlet, and get a thorough
acquaintanceship with what the Sys-
tem can do in the way of economy
and efficiency.

The man of Hebrew faith seldom pushes a
barrow; he either owns the barrow or super-
intends it. It wasn’t possible for all the
Hebrews who enlisted up to the time when
the figures were made up (there were 323,000
enrolments then) to be officers, but they came
closer to it than any other folk. Out of their
total enrolment 62 per 1060 were officers.
People of no religion (atheists and such)
came next with 32 officers per 1000: Presby-
terians (a practical, strenuous folk mostly
named Mac) showed 28 per 1000: Anglicans
26 ; Methodists 17 ; Roman Catholics 12. So
when it was a case of “Onward, Christian
Soldiers,” as mentioned in the hymn, the
Hebrew captain was often in charge simply
because there were four times as many of
him as the Hebrew privates required.
Politics are miserably small potatoes in
this country. Some bones of people murdered
50 years ago have been lying in a Victorian
police stable since grandfather's time, be-
cause the full-blown Legislature of two
Houses, with Cabinet, Governor, etc., had
never heard of them and 9. long succession
of John Hops wouldn’t move on their own.
Then the local member got on the track, and
instead of telling John flop to get the needful
order from the nearest authority or to act
without it, he held up the Assembly—l3s
statesmen at £3OO each —over the dusty frag-
ments. That is the way to be an active
member. Some members aren’t active, but
nearly all of them are local.
Ivor MacGillivray, late member of the
S.A. Assembly—he was one of the oldest and
best-liked men there —was entertained re-
cently by the National party. In handing
him a cheque the chairman was indignant
that MacGillivray had been turned down at
the last elections because he put country before
self. He went out with John Verran and
other compulsory-service democrats. MacG
entered the S.A. Assembly as member for
Port Adelaide in 1803, and in 25 years was
only absent two days from the sittings of the
Chamber.
From Buda Pest:—
Hungary (or rather the Magyar caste in Hungary),
one of the boss criminals, tries to flee in a coat of
whitewash from the wrath to come by declaring
itself independent of Austria and abjuring its
Hun king at Vienna. Instead of being one of the
three thieves who are running away, it proposes to
drop back into the crowd and help to chase the other
two thievps. The proposition is too thin, for the
Magyar holds a front place among the most hated
individuals in Europe. In his good times he and
the German-Austrian have been two Legrees
oppressing the small nationalities of the
empire. In occasional bad times the German-
Austrian has been Legree, and the Magyar
has been Sambo, the' nigger-driver, oppressed by
the person above but a bitter tyrant to those below.
Hungary had a powerful Parliament when the rest
of the empire had none, and when it rose in the
great cause of libery in 1848 under the boss impostor
Kossuth and his crowd, it wasn’t the lack of Parlia-
mentary Government that was the chief trouble.
What it most wanted was greater scope to wallop
its slave. The little peoples made up more than
half the population of Hungary, but their differences
of race and language made then an easy prey to
the solid Magyar. When the Magyar was feeling
fresh ho prohibited the teaching, writing, preaching
or printing of their languages, prohibited all save
Magyar-speaking people from holding office, and
administered the higher justice (of a sort) in Mag-
yar. Anybody who couldn’t speak Magyar went
without justice. As for Parliamentary representation
the Magyar counted the vote. In these matters he
hasn’t much improved with years. A big fly in the
Magyar ointment in ’4B was that a certain amount
of justice could be got (if costs were available) by
appeal to the Hun courts at Vienna. The Hun
served out a very fair brand of equity, because he
had no sympathy with the Magyar’s swelled head or
with any swelled head, save his own swelled head.
On a general average it is probable that the Little
Peoples liked the German-Austrian a trifle better
than the Magyar. When the glorious war of freedom
began it was made clear to them by Kossuth and co.
that they weren't included; in fact by losing the
right of appeal to Vienna, poor as it was, they were
to be a shade worse off than before. The Croats, the
best fighting element among the fragmentary folk,
hesitated a while, but being told they were dogs and
should approach the Magyar on all fours if they had
anything to say, they barked violently and threw
in their lot with the Hun tyrant for preference, and
jumped on Hungarian “liberty” with four very
capable feet. The Magyar’s plausible dream of
getting out in time and taking the serf races with
him suggests the same obtuse intelligence with which
the Kaiser is afflicted. When New Hungary is mapped
out it will probably be rather less than half the
oil concern. It is an ingenious inspiration to pose
as one of the oppressed nationalities—just about as
ingenious as it would be for the Kaiser ,to cry to the
Allies for and on the pretence that he is habitually
trodden on by Denmark.
From an Australian ship in the North
Sea:—-
I’d like to call Australia’s attention to the
fact that whilst steps are being made to re-
dress the grievances of the A.1.F., the Australian
Navy isn’t even being asked if it has any grievances.
I may say at once that it has. The Australian Naval
Administration is either stoically apathetic or very
dull. The sailors of the Australian Navy are quite
as educated, intelligent and enfranchised as are their
military comrades-in-arms; but they have no cham-
pion to arise and expose their wrongs. Nothing
appears in the press, simply because the correspond-
ents visiting the ships are hurried round and have
no chance of conversing with the men, and are then
told “All’s well” in the wardroom. The ratings,
when on leave, do not care to air their grievances
in places where they would probably not be listened
to, and certainly not remedied. As an example of
official stupidity take the following episode, which
occurred on board this ship last winter: It is a cold,
raw morning, with about a foot of snow lying about.
An Arctic Sea wind is blowing great guns, penetrat-
ing and chilling almost to the marrow. The ship is
at anchor; and the men are ordered out to drill on
deck in thin white duck suits. The captain and
officers looking oh are warmly clad. Remember that
many of the saildrs have spent long years in warm
climates prior to the war, and you can imagine their
feelings out there in the show, half-frozen, Whilst
plenty of thick winter clothing is available in their
bags. The men have asked, time after time, to have
similar grievances redressed, but all their efforts
have proved futile. We don’t mind the unavoidable
hardships of the service, but we resent the stupidities
hallowed by tradition.

SPECIALIST EXPLAINS CAUSE OF
STOMACH TROUBLE.
VALUABLE ADVICE TO SUFFERERS.
'There are many different forms of stom-
ach trouble,” said a well-known specialist re-
cently, “but practically all are traceable to
excessive acidity and food fermentation. That
is why the results obtained from the use of
drugs are usually so disappointing. Admit-
ting fermentation and consequent acidity of
the food contents to be the underlying cause
of most forms of indigestion, it naturally
follows that the use of a reliable antacid, such
as Usurated magnesia, which is so frequently
prescribed by physicians, will produce better
results than drugs or combination of dru«-s.
Accordingly I almost invariably advise those
who complain of digestive trouble to get some
j unrated magnesia (note the name carefully
as other forms are not so suitable for this
purpose) from their chemist, and take half
a teaspoonful of the powder, or two 5-grain
compressed tablets, with a little water after
meals. This, by immediately neutralising
the acid and stopping the fermentation re'
moves the cause of the trouble and ensures
normal and healthy digestion.”—Advt
Enjoy the satisfaction of knowing that you
are buying a Hat which represents maxi-
mum value and maintains its exceptional
soaring qualities and good appearance to
the end by always asking for Woodrows.
