Sydney, October 28, 191 S.
My Dear Arini, —
The Melba-Verbrugghen concert brought
ii prodigious crowd to the big Town Hall,
and devotees went hot-foot for seats long be-
fore the Dame was due. Melba looked radi-
ant in a pink gown with a long sash that
touched the floor as the wearer hurriedly
wormed her way through the crowded orches-
tra, to take another volley of applause. She
kissed her hand to Verbrugghen (who de-
served a tribute for supplying a brilliant
orchestral background to her voice) and
waved her arms to stop the clamor of the
crowd. A dense mass of humans was
wedged into every available niche, but, as
the evening wore on towards six o’clock
Philistines with a big mileage to cover for
home stole away like ghosts. Mercifully
' erbrugghen’s back was turned towards this
desecration, and the crash of orchestral
drums, fifes and fiddles drowned the sinners’
departure. _ A new Belgian pianist, Francis
do Bourgitignon, recently on active service,
was an acquisition to the programme. The
Lord Mayor’s Fund is likely to gain £l5OO
from the festival.
. The Tank, with Captain Browne in charge,
is continuing to knock down brick walls
and rattle the bones of distinguished per-
sons who ride within it. A temporarily-
dismantled State Excellency emerged with a
cheerful smile and more or less on all fours
after his jaunt in the elephantine contrap-
tion. Jack’s Day is getting half the takings
O' P-S promenades. Soldiers’ dependants are
credited with the other half. Saturday,
JNov. 2, is mentioned as the final appear-
mechanical animal on Victoria
I ark Racecourse.
The big aquatic carnival for Jack’s Day
ought to add £2OOO to the Fund. The after-
noon was ideal from a yachtsman’s point
of view. At one time, late' in the afternoon,
when several races were on the home stretch
at once, a fleet of over 50 boats came like
great white swans towards the Yacht Squad-
ron s moorings at Kirribilli. With the after-
noon sun blazoning their sails the spectacle
was entrancing. The exciting turn promised
for the afternoon, the blowing up of a
mine, went oil’ without any warning nearly
ail hour before its scheduled time. The
Davidson lady, floating around in a launch
with some returned wounded soldiers as her
guests, saw the eruption; but a greatly-
annoyed public, arriving later, was dis-
appointed. Mrs. Alfred Milson was a solid
worker on a big committee, and Gard Trou-
ton was general rouscabout.
Wireless-operator Bobbie Towns lent a
hand by demonstrating with the whispering
instrument at the Kirribilli fete for Jack’s
Day. He had 2A years’ active service, most
of the time with M.L. infantrv at the Front
and was gassed and knocked off the fighting
list. The young Sydney man is a grand-
son of Captain Bobbie Towns, the pic-
turesque pioneer merchant, shipowner, bank-
director, gold-miner and pastoralist who
married a sister of William Charles Went-
worth in 3833.
Private Jim Osborn, only son of Lieut.-
Oolonel Osborn, of the Permanent Artillery,
has been killed in France. Young Osborn
was through the., early.: days of the war as
/< V;- pt! }j n 111 *A.I.F., was wounded: at
Gallipoli and hit again later in France
Invalided out of the A.I.F. in England this
tine young Australian was determined to get
another shot at Fritz, and enlisted in the
Argyle and Sutherland Highlanders. He fell
m action on October 10.
Last week s clamor for Jack’s Day buttons
knocked the supply off its legs, in a manner
of ’-peaking. The Inky Way stall was short
of about ±2O worth of metal badges, which
wore on order, till the lady in charge un-
furfed her gamp for action. Incidentally,
Mr. D Alpuget s generous gift of fruit and
eocoanuts from the Islands (.cases of pine-
apples, paw-paws, bananas, etc ) gave the
presswomen’s effort a big help.
HALS. Warspite, with Mrs. Glossop as
Flag-Captain and a crew manned by the
I riendly Union of Sailors’ wives, will
anchor at the corner of Pitt and Moore
streets on Jaci< s Day. The cargo consists
or anything you fancy, from a bos’n’s whistle
to a home-made anchor. About the only item
unprocurable is bos’n’s language. There’s a
shortage in that since the submarining of the
Leinster.
Fergus Hume’s sister Bessie, who claims
hilly Dunedin (M.L.) as her native town,
came in for some recent notice in London
by presenting to the Belgian representative
(in London) a casket of M.L. woods for King
Albert and his Queen. The gift enclosed
some verses dedicated to Belgium.
Major Alan Wright, of this dusty town,
was married in England lately to Dorothy
Hislop.
Artist Francis Hodgkins’s exhibition of
oils and water-colors, hung in Anthony Hor-
dern's’ Art Gallery, is a riot of rainbow tints.
There are some charming pictures in this
Maorilander’s collection, with here and there
a daring canvas that borders on the grotesque.
The arresting effect in “Barn in Picardy”
and “The Lantern Dancers” required clever
draughtsmanship. The barn looks as if a
German army had passed through it. There
remains beside this phase of Miss Hodgkins’
splash-work such peaceful scenes as “The
White House on the Canal.” This is in the
modern English manner. Of the advanced
French school, “The Gypsies” is a bold and
powerful picture.
The Town Hall concert (Oct. 25) for Jack’s
Day was a comparative failure, although the
programme was strong enough to make up
two first-rate shows. The committee bristled
with names of note, but it didn’t bristle
with even one energetic publicity agent. So the
result was half-empty: galleries and many
empty chairs below. The Tingira boys’ drill
display was alone worth the entrance money.
Mrs. Alex. Gordon’s song, “In an Old-
fashioned Town,” was a C‘--q - ating item.
Lieut. McCulloch’s lecture. My Ship,” with
very poor pictures of .the. Butle of Jutland
(mostly smoke haze) was almost inaudible
at _ any distance. Captain Glossop ex-
plained, with pictures and pointer, his
adventures on the Sydney. Carrie Lanceley
v/as also included in the long programme.
The Davidsons were in the official pen ; but
not all the time. His Ex. mounted the plat-
form to offer a few remarks, and was closely
shadowed by A.D.C. Saltmarshe.
The Y.W.C.A. hall held an audience that
was mildly shocked at some of the statements
in Miss Garnham’s report of Indian women’s
life 1 in the coolie locations of Fiji. Much of
this unpleasantness was aired some months
ago, but the lady, who belongs to the London
Missionary Society (Calcutta), hasbeen en-
trusted with the job of adding to the details.
It’s up to Fiji, as a Crown colony, to deal
with .somebody, if the facts are as nauseous
as alleged.
The Commonwealth Treasurer floated the
seventh War Loan under the happiest con-
ditions. Australians reading the cables over
their breakfast bacon,
had their digestions
improved by rations
of good news from
every Front. Per-
haps the news was
too rosy to help the
Loan. Optimists
settled the peace
terms over a second
cup of tea, and de-
cided that a vigor-
ous pursuit of War
Bonds was not so
necessary as it had
been. Then Acting
Prime Minister Watt
sounded a warning
note that the mother
►State was lagging
behind her quota,
and trouble was coming up with both spurs
on. Women, and notably small fry who had
to scrape together the deposit money, re-
sponded eagerly, and ten-pound War Bonds
went off like crackers. Some industrious
pei son with a head for figures may inform
us later how big a part the average business
gji'l has played in lending money to her
*u Un L ry i this effort has been
the Sydney Commonwealth Bank. With
Governor Denison Miller temporarily out of
Australia, the Deputy-Governor, James Kell
has been the live wire. Born in Mittagong!
the son of a successful N. S. Wales farmer
young Kell deliberately chose a mercantile
career. The. bush called in vain. He began
ty add up ledgers in the N. S. Wales head
office of the Bank of Australasia. After
varied experience and continuous service in
one or other of its branches, the bank clerk
or ISS6 became the sub-inspector of 1906.
hew men know commercial Australia better!
In the course of business as sub-inspector
James Kell visited every city and every
important town in this continent. As the
Deputy-Governor of the Commonwealth Bank
he is one of the forces that count in financial
circles. His energy and ability have greatly
contributed to the success of the present
Loan. Photo by May Moore.
The new Chair of Pharmacology in Sydney
’Varsity got an official warming the other
evening. The occasion was Dr. H. G. Chap-
man’s first professional address in the Great
Hall. The Chair is an off-shoot of our b g
Medical School in the dusty ’Varsity pad-
docks. Through its medium the new don
will encourage N. S. Wales pharmacists to
tread the path that leads to a science cap
and gown. Australian plants—we may be
trampling useful drugs underfoot in every
bush wilderness—are going to be carefully
cross-examined.
From Brisbane: —
Patriotic parents should see the display of toys
in Smellie’s big window, opposite the G.P.0., all
made by returned soldiers in the Red Cross work-
rooms of our military hospitals. The makers are
eager for Christmas orders.
Jimmy Grant, who will take his pen to America
with Minister for Railways Fihelly, is still in the
thirties. He began his career as a railway porter,
and went to U.S. and Europe four years ago with
Railway Commissioner Evans as private secretary.
Two Brisbane girls who have coaxed passports
are off to England this week to be married to their
soldiers. One is Esme Lahey, daughter of the
David Laheys, who is to marry Lieut. A. W. Clarke.
The other voyager is Myee Harrison, daughter of
the late S. Harrison, well known in Ipswich . and
Charters Towers. Lieut. W. 11. Bryan, M.C., is
waiting for her.
In the basement of the Colonial Mutual Chambers,
Queen-street, is the Prisoners of War Gift House.
Gifts of plate, china, jewellery, old lace, etc., may
be left for sale. Cheques representing over £4OO
have been sent in, and a sympathetic soul paid three
months rent in advance. A small sub-committee of
the Red Cross is running the Gift House.
Capt. Leslie Blake, M.C., a Gympie boy, has died
of wounds. He was in the Geological Survey Depart-
ment, and was a member of Mawson’s Antarctic
expedition.
The A. S. Leslies, of South Toowong, lost their
third soldier son when Corporal Arthur Leslie was
killed in France, on September 30.
Drowned in the Leinster, Lieut. Frank Laracy, a
popular Toowoomba boy. He was a chemist by pro-
fession, and won the Military Cross as well as a
commission. His people were waiting for a cable to
say when he would be home.
Capt. Hayward Moffatt, M.C., one of the Camoola
Park family, has been killed.
Travellers from North Sydney were
accustomed for many years to see on one or
other of the “business” ferryboats every
morning a singularly handsome, erect and
well-groomed citizen, notable as an old
inhabitant of Bay Road in the hilly suburb.
The good looks, erect carriage and benign
presence changed very little with the pass-
ing years, but lately it was less frequently
seen on the accustomed seat on the sunny side
of the ferryboat; and last week Mordaunt
William Clarke, who arrived in Sydney when
he was about six years old, died in his old
home, aged 85. He was a leading man in
insurance circles and a son of North Syd-
ney s early Anglican parson. Mordaunt
Clarke knew Bay Road when a tang’ed under-
growth of primitive bush grew wild where
of recent years he caught the train to Mil-
son s Point.. When Sydney was beginning to
grow gas-lamps and call itself a city the late
Mr Clarke was a student at Trinity College,
Dublin. Some years later he married a
daughter of the late Captain Gother Mann,
of the north side of the harbor.

heavy music of “Judas Maccabteus” for its
concert in the Town Hall. A big audience took
inost kindly to the well-known semi-chorus,
See the Conquering Hero Comes.” and pos-
sibly substituted Allenby for Judas return-
ing from victory near Jerusalem. Phil New-
bury, the nrincipal tenor of the performance,
was m good voice. Reginald Gooud con-
ducted a choir which overflowed with re-
in for cements from juvenile Conservatoriura
students.

The summer frock is not all cobweb and
a cloud of ninon. There are new and stout

terns as large as saucers, waiting to be made
up into this season’s skirts. Someone must
be anxious to look like a verandah blind, or
these goods would be bustled off to the fur-
niture departments.

Under the beguiling label, “Old Gold and
Silver Fund,” a patriotic curio-shop . (take
the lift at 62 Hunter-street) has contributed
£5342 to war funds in the two and a half
years of its existence. Hon. sec. ,T. E.
Richardson will grateful!v accept your best
tea-caddy or any trinket that you feel would
be more useful for the good cause than lying
in a forgotten box. Hair ornaments- —the
mid-Victorian vogue—will appeal to collec-
tors. The O. G. and S. Fund owns some of
these weird fancies, including ear-rings, pear-
shaped and about four inches long, made
from the brown locks of somebody’s great
aunt or small grandmother.

Melbourne, October 28, J. 918.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Governor Stanley and his lady grave a men-
only dinner-party on Wednesday night. A
reception followed, and the women folk were
allowed to attend that. General Pau and his
missioners were guests of honor. His Ex.
wore his most gorgeous uniform, a dream out-
fit with silver epaulettes. He spoke French
to his visitors, with half-amused, nervous
side glances at his wife, who is a fluent
French scholar. Dad was also put in the
shade by his small daughter, who chatters
French like a Parisian.
The Stanley dame looked even better than
usual in a trained gown which avouiul round
her figure in brocaded velvet of rose and sil-
ver. A diamond tiara flashed like an adver-
tising sky-sign. The drawing-rooms, wide
open to the terrace, were filled Avith roses
and lily-of-the-valley.
Governor Newdegate, in sending out invi-
tations to his dinner to honor the French
Mission, asked for “full dress” ; so Hobart
put on all the robes and gold braid it had.
Speaker Evans sent a special appeal to his
Ex. that wigs might be excused. It appears
Evans has only the Avig of his predecessor,
who was a man of the John Forrest build,
Avhile Evans himself is about as big as Billy
Hughes. The wig would have been an ex-
tinguisher for Evans, so he carried the horse-
hair in his hand during the function as a
guarantee of good faith. What he should
have done Avas to Avear it, filling in the blank
space with shavings. They’re a shiftless lot,
these politicians.
The G.-G. lately confided to a V.A.D. girl
Avho asked him what horse had won the
Caulfield Cup, that he “hadn’t been to the

Mrs. Theo. Anderson, from Westralia,
daughter of our Harvie Patterson, is making
a round of visits here. From the Leding-
hams, Eobert Harpers and Winter Patter-
sons she is going on to stay with sister
Mrs. Feakes, wife of Commander Feakes, in
Sydney. Mrs. Tom Ledingliam gave her a
party to meet old friends. Mrs. Fairbairn,
Mrs. Tolhurst, Mrs. Archie Currie, the
Appletons, Jeffersons, Bowes Kellys, and
Eardley Wilinots all responded to the call.
There is talk of running our sometime
friend H. B. Irving for a seat in the House
of Commons, on the ground that theatrical
interests are urgently in need of a practical
and experienced representative at Westmin-
ster, and it is generally agreed that H.B. is
just the man. The statement he recently
drew up in connection Avith a Bill
before Parliament regulating the employ-
ment _ of children on the stage has been
described as a “masterly document,” com-
bining the qualifications of his legal training
with his intimate first-hand professional
knowledge of the stage. No doubt he Avould
make a picturesque M.P.; but the prelimi-
nary difficulty of finding a constituency and
fitting him into the party system is not
easily overcome. An independent, however
gifted, has not much of a show in England.
Some time ago tlie Charles Fairbairns
removed from their Banongil station several
vanloads of household goods, destined for the
family humpy at South Yarra. One load,
containing a lot of “priceless” table-frills,
rare old antimacassars and similar expensive
trappings, caught fire on the Bannoekburn-
road and was burned to death. The
anguished owners held the carrying-firm re-
sponsible for the loss, and presently took
the case before Judge Cussen, who assessed
the _ damage at £4OO, giving judgment
against the carriers accordingly.
The Town Hall was crowded for the
women’s welcome to General Pair. Im-
portant Leagueites occupied seats on the
platform and Lady Stanley was in the chair.
The General in starting ventured a few
words of English : “Women, ladies and
gentlemen,” he began, and then wondered
what the joke was. Dr. Siegfried interpreted
the rest of his remarks. The Leaguites had
prepared an illuminated address for the
guest. They fussed over the package on a
centre table before the visitors arrived, but
later became so excited that they forgot to
present it. Farewells were over and the Gen-
eral and his party were on the steps of their
motors when a flustered woman rushed after
them and thrust the trifle of painted white
satin into the old soldier’s hand.
Holy Advent Church, at Malvern, which
was helped into being by a fat contribution
to the building fund from the Septimus Mil-
lers, has had a succession of more or less
interesting parsons. Charles Perry, who
chose poetess Dorothy MeCrae as his chief
helper, was one of them. Another was Theo-
dore Redhead, sometimes known as “the
sporting parson.” In those days Redhead
was an active, husky sky-pilot, who went in
for yachting and took a personal interest in
the spiritual welfare of the local young man.
Now also appears Edward White, the pre-
sent vicar, as a somewhat different parson.
In spite of _ the high cost of paper and the
low rate of clerical pay, White has issued
a book of verse about his distant days at
Melbourne Grammar School. The Diocesan
Book Society is undertaking sales.
The Win-the-War Equiestrienne Club,
which has been wading through rather deep
waters of late, has decided to disband. The
girls, however, simply adore dressing up in
scarlet sashes and flamboyant badges, and
can’t bear the idea of being right out of the
game. Wherefore some of the association’s
very naicest members propose to join up in
an exclusive squad to be known as the Red
Cross Riders —that is, if the central R.C.
administration will let them. That import-
ant body hasn’t been asked its opinion yet,
though the new idea is so perfect a dream
that it seems impossible that its realisation
should be prevented.
The Syd Sands pair entertained Dr. Sieg-
fried at Heidelberg while the rest of the
French Mission were in Tasmania. They
motored him to Healesville, and then petrolled
him down to Flinders to the Watts.
The Hennessys are once more at the Grand
after an exciting motor trip from Sydney to
Melbourne. They recommend it as a thrill
to all adventurous motoi’ists.
Senator “Curly” Keating travelled through
Tassy with the French Mission and talked
to it in its own language all the way. It’s
a proud time for the language pluggers who
survived the depressing teaching methods of
years ago. •
General Pau exclaimed when, last week, he
saw the Comforts Fund flower-stall at the
Town Hall, loaded with large bunches of
lily-of-the-valley! It reminded him of the
Parisian flower-markets and Spring in
France. The fund, by the way, has been
doing very nicely from its flower receipts,
though the stall has always been a source of
substantial income.
The Walter Bayles family is soon to
have a permanent camp at Toorak, after
their long sojourn among the sheeu and poul-
try at Hexham, on the Western plains.
Walter, who is a brother of Norman, M.L.A.,
is building an up-to-date shack on a choice
block of the late William Bayles’s hillside
property over the big Toorak rise. He
watches the pile go up from his flat at
Myoora, the old Harper home near by.
This is Admiral
Creswell, who is re-
tiring from the Aus-
tralian Navee, in
order that an officer
with recent war ex-
perience may carry
on. The bluff old
officer has been a
sailor for 52 years.
He was born at Gib-
raltar in ’52, joined
the training-ship Bri-
tannia in ’66, and
had an exciting life
afloat before he was
appointed Director of
the Commonwealth
Naval Forces. He
was knighted i n
1911, when he was
made rear-admiral. In those days Richard
Ncvill was Chamberlain to the G.-G., and
it was part of his quaint job to sec to the
ceremonious detail of beknighting people.
Nevill had quite a bunch of knights to deal
with in Creswell’s year, and he gravely put
them through a rehearsal. It was like a
dancing lesson. “So many steps, sir, and
bow.” Creswell shook his head doggedly
and refused to prance and duck. It didn’t
appeal to the man who had started life fight-
ing Chinese pirates. This portrait of the
mutineer is by Ruth Hollick.
Lieut. Jack Gould-Taylor, of the Aus-
tralian Flying Corps, who was mentioned
the other week as having won the D.F.C.,
has been killed in. action in France.
Sister Gertie Munro, an Australian nurse,
died recently at Salonika from pneumonia,
following malaria. Secretary Munro, of
the big residential Nurses’ Club in Mel-
bourne for Military Nurses, mourns for her
niece.
Australian nurse G. Davis now ranks as
major, and has been made Matron-General of
Australian Hospitals in India. Last week
she was decorated for special services.
Major-General Legge, just gone to Sydney
on official business, had a very sad message
sent after him. Ilis second son was lately
killed in France.
Dr. and Mrs. Somers, of Mornington, have
just had news of the death in action of their
second son Gervase. He was only 19.
Sister Rachel Pratt came back on the
last troopship, after much hard work in
France. She was one of the first nurses to
be wounded, and has amongst her treasures
a letter from Birdwood thanking her for her
efforts.

D. 5.0., who stuck to his fighting job in

Adelaide notes:—
Two Red Cross efforts were billed for Saturday: a
f6te which Lady Galway opened at the Richard
Smiths’ home at Glenelg, and a fancy-dress carnival at
Henley Beach. It wasn’t the weather you’d order for
outdoor frivols, but the usual crowd rolled up to
both shows. The Glenelg fete gave Mabel Law Smith
a chance of saying her good-byes. She left on Tues-
day for England, where she is to be married to
Captain Iludd.
Local musicians gave a complimentary concert in
the Queen’s Hall on Saturday to veteran P. A.
Howells, who has been 50 years in Adelaide.
Things are being put into shape for welcoming the
Anzacs on furlough. Free tram and train passes
are to be asked for, the families of country men
are to be given an opportunity of coming to town
to meet them, and as far as possible Billjim’s family
will share in any festivity that is arranged. All of
which will be far more to his taste than banquets,
speeches and huge demonstrations at which the
returned man is paraded to satisfy the curiosity of
the public.
Col. Powell, who has been on active service with
the A. M.C. for four years, is on his way home. Lat-
terly,he has been at Harefield, where he has been a
big brother to sick and wounded Billjims.
Another Australian back from England is Mrs.
Lyster Colley, the J. L. Stirlings’ elder daughter.
The Red Cross Society had barely emptied its col-
lecting-tins when the Navy League launched its appeal
with a Nelson Day celebration in the Town Hall. A
white silk ensign, which is to be presented by the
local branch of the League to the cruiser Adelaide,
was on view during the evening.
There died at Port Adelaide G. \V. Dempster, the
well-known sporting solicitor. What time he could
spare from law he gave to racing, shooting and bowl-
ing.
Spanish influenza has claimed Mrs. G. H. Wyatt
(better known here as Mrs. E. W. Oldham) as one
of its victims in South Africa. She was well known
in musical circles in Adelaide 20 years ago.
Marjorie Eadie (daughter of the Bendigo
doctor) is expected to remark “I will” to
Evan Pearce, of Strathfield. on November
14 After the honeymoon in Sydney the pair
will set about flatkeeping in Toorak.
Button Day realised nearly
45000. Mayoress _ Mrs. Frank Stapley and
tlx© councillors' wives made a haul outside
the Town Hall in elegant kiosks, while the
committee button-holed the charitably in-
clined at the centroway. Nurses from the
various hospitals, the Returned Soldiers’
Association and a baby elephant from
\\ irths Circus also kept the hunt going.
A testimonial should be sent by the Re-
patriation Department to the Victorian
Farmers’ Union, which of all the State’s
political concerns stands out as a practical
body of repatriators. At Koo-wee-rup re-
cently a gathering of Y.F.U. members
assembled, plough in hand, to prepare the
ground for a soldier to whom a farming-
block had been allotted; and during this
working-bee it was decided that in future all
returned soldiers in the Koo-wee-rup district
should have their land prepared in the same
way. The good example lias been followed
by numbers of other branches, and the prac-
tice is spreading all over the State, with the
V.F.U. in the forefront of the movement.
Here, at last, is a political outfit which is
making a systematic effort to do something
besides making semaphore gestures from
public platforms.
Cacialli, the baritone with the flashing
black eyes, has blossomed out as a tutor.
He gave a concert the other Wednesday
night and sang thunderously. Of four pupils
that lie presented, one was an Italian youth
struggling apparently with the idea he was
Caruso. He struck all the Caruso attitudes,
but lacked the Caruso chins and chests—
also the Caruso voice. It was a disappoint-
ing imitation of the original.
Alfter a fortnight’s postponement on
account of influenza, Stella Power had her
concert on Saturday night. Melba was away
in Sydney, so could not lend her more than
moral support, but Stella filled the Auditorium
and satisfied everybody. She sailed on to do her
job in a dress of burnt amber and green
with green stockings and shoes. Contralto
Ivy Phillips sang in a blue frock, elaborated
with lace. Florence Brown at her ’cello
wore black and green.
For the opening of her exhibition of pas-
tels and paintings Marion .Tones scored the
largest crowd seen at an art show for a long
time. Lady Helen bought a picture and
spoke a piece, voicing the forlorn hope that
the mere mob would some day take as much
interest in art as it does in a Cup favorite.
Among the numerous portraits are a genial
representation of etcher John Shirlow and
a pleasing study of poet Rupert Atkinson’s
handsome spouse. The artist won the Gal-
lery’s Travelling Scholarship at the begin-
ning of the year, and will go to Paris after
the war.
Actress Katlilene Macdonell is staying
with the Baldwin Spencers and putting in
her time saying sbulful good-byes as a prelim-
inary. to returning to Canada. She speaks
of doing a season in London and then com-
ing back here with a co. of her own.
Attractive reading matter is urgently
needed for the military hospitals. Please
remember this when you have finished with
your novels and magazines, and drop them
into the Red Cross bins or depots.
Urged by barrister Kelly, the State has
decided to give the scent-making industry a
helping hand, of sorts, by importing the
seeds necessary for growing certain oil-pro-
ducing plants. Oman, Minister for Rose-
mary and Lavender, has put his O.K. on the
plan, and the State laboratories are to be
available, when required, for making experi-
ments. Kelly is the president of the Associ-
ation for the Cultivation of Flowers for
Scents—a long, clumsy label —and it is
largely through his persistent gardening and
encouragement of gardening friends that the
Government is beginning to help. The con-
cern has already done a good deal in the way
of raising lavender and such prolific perfume-
providers, for sale to local distillers; but so
far the industry can’t produce its own seeds
iu adequate quantities. The retailing seeds-
man, of course, is either bare of supplies or
wants to make big profits on his stocks, which
are in many cases moribund pre-war stuff.
Hence the demand for State interference.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

From Tiie Bulletin’s Melb. prophet:—
In picking' a bunch for the Melbourne Cup a week
before the event the prophet is worried in the first
place by doubt whether the Caulfield Cup winner will
even arrive at the post. Had everything gone right
with him King Offa would have deserved a front place
in the Melbourne Cup market. But at time of
writing his fitness for the fray is very questionable,
seeing that ever since Caulfield he has been under
treatment for a sore leg. All things considered.
King Offa, though a natural stayer, cannot to-day be
thought of as a real good thing for the two-nrile race
next Tuesday. He may win, but is more likely
to fail in the last quarter of a mile. It doesn’t
often happen that the Moonee Valley Cup result offers
any suggestions for the big event at Flomington, but
the running last Saturday seems to give an outside
chance to both Rael Locin and Calais. If that
running was Court Jester’s best it looks as though
he may be counted out. Taking the two full brothers,
Estland and Finmark, together with Kennaquhair,
Arch Mareila and the best from Bradfield’s stable as
the most dangerous half-dozen, the writer expects
Finmark to win both Derby and Cup. Rael Locin
should have at least a rough outsider’s chance of
getting a shop.
* * * #
If Finmark wins the V.R.C. Derby, and
wins it decisively, he is practically certain to start
at a short price for the Cup. As own brother to
Estland, who won the Canterbury Cup (M.L.j last
year, and the A.J.C. Randwick Plate the other day,
Finmark holds an unwritten certificate for stamina.
A really good 3-vear-old is always a reasonable in-
vestment for the Melbourne Cup, and Finmark, not-
withstanding his defeat by Gloaming at Randwick
and by Woorawa at Rosehill, may be as good as the
average Derby winner. Anyway, Davis, his trainer,
who as a jockey rode some good Derby horses, and
has trained some really top-class animals, reckons
him a gcod dead better.
Arch Mareila has been the A.J.C. handi-
capper’s tip for Melbourne Cup from the time the
weights were issued, and the gelding’s respectable
effort in the Metrop. certainly justified the judgment
of the man who rated him 111 b. higher than his
Melbourne Cup mark. On paper a horse that runs
second for the Metropolitan with Bst. has a mighty
fine chance to win the 2-mile race at Flemington with
7st. 31b. and Frank Dempsey in the saddle. West-
court last year ran second for the Met. with Bst. 41b.,
beating Lingie at a difference of 61b., and he was
just able to do it again in the Melbourne Cup. This
year Arch Mareila was receiving a stone from Lingie
at Randwick. Over the longer course at Flemington
he will meet Lingie on 111 b. better terms, whilst
Kennaquhair gets 211 b. the worst of it as compared
with their previous handicap. There’s no arguing
against Arch Mareila having a chance. Seeing that
owner Sam Hordern has money to burn, he was wise
to engage Dempsey, at a fancy price, to steer his
promising candidate.
The backing of Night Watch for Mel-
bourne Cup was more suggestive of a stable doubt
about King Offa’s foreleg than of a sincere belief in
the merits of Wakeful’s seasoned son. Thusfar the
best performance to the credit of Night Watch was
the gelding’s defeat of Ashview at Caulfield the other
Saturday. Ashview was giving away a stone, and
was only beaten by a head. Bradfield’s followers, who
presently laid odds on Magpie for the Caulfield
Stakes, hadn’t rushed to back Night Watch as
though they thought it a good thing; so even with
the minimum weight Leslie McDonald’s gelding
doesn’t look very tempting.
After all said and done, assuming that
King Offa keeps comfortably on his legs until next
Tuesday, his chance of winning Melbourne Cup from
the best of the visiting horses (at the weights) must
remain a mere matter of guesswork. One may
assume that the good-looking Englishman won the
Caulfield Cup quite easily, in a racing way of speak-
ing, under the disadvantage of a sore foreleg, which
had slightly interfered with the finish of his prepara-
tion. Good. But as far as the respective qualities of
the “form” in the Caulfield Cup and the Metropoli-
tan there is no means of judging. Wolaroi was the
only "representative of recent Sydney form that ran
in the Caulfield Cup, and he merely confirmed his
brilliance of the previous week without making it
clear that he could have run. second, say, for the
Metropolitan with 9st. 31b. on his back. Could
Wolaroi have split Kennaquhair and Arch Marella in
the Metropolitan if he hadn’t been engaged in win-
ning the Shorts Handicap on the same day? Another
interesting query is: Did Wolaroi beat Eusebius by
two lengths in the Caulfield Cup as the judge made
out? A lot of people made the difference out to be
not more than a neck.
If Eusebius were not addicted to running
wide at the end of his races one would have no hesi-
tation in backing him to beat Outlook, if not Fin-
mark, for the V.R.C. Derby. Outlook certainly
shaped like a non-stayer at Randwick, and although
he beat Eusebius over 11 furlongs in the Eclipse
Stakes at Caulfield, the victory was nothing as com-
pared with the latter’s meritorious third for the Cup,
three days later. Winning the Eclipse Stakes is no
guarantee that the nag can last out a Caulfield Cup.
A good third in the Cup suggests the probability of
seeing a Derby' through.
The Tye Bros, have seemingly tired of
importing horses from England and getting com-
paratively few races out of them. Ethiopian and
their other representatives in training are announced
for sale after Cup week.
“Vic”: The lirst Melbourne races were on
the flat now occupied by the Spencer-street railway
yard. Batman’s Hill, since cut down, was the
grandstand. Kawkner had a booth there, for hard
and soft drinks and pies. The proceedings were
adjourned to Flemington about ’42, but not
till about ’GO was the advantage of the hill
on the north side detected. The stand was on
the south side by the Saltwater River. Then came
Bagot. It. is curious (or perhaps not) that neither
he nor Byron Moore, both surveyors, should have
cared anything about racing. The committee
wouldn’t have a racing man as secretary, and this,
both times, flung many out of the ballot. It. was
Bagot who shifted the grand-stand and finish over
to the hill.
“Halter” : Taking the poor muster of three-
year-olds at Randwick this Spring into consideration
perhaps there is nothing to be surprised at in the
character of the Caulfield Cup field. But it was a
field w*jich looked like a warning or something to
someone—a prize of £5OOO being run for by a
crowd of which at least half are scarcely up to
suburban handicap level. Not many times has there
been a weaker field at Caulfield than that saddled
up last week. Out of the 16 starters four were
“imp.” It looks as if the day is not far off when
the Melbourne Cup field may consist of 27 strangers
and 0 local horse.
* * * *
Suburban proprietary racing, interrupted
by happenings of more importance at Randwick,
was resumed in Sydney on Saturday. The menu
was served at Canterbury Park, where 150 nags
transpired, and during the afternoon battled out 10
races. One event was of four episodes, it being
necessary to find accommodation at the barrier for
65 maidens. Division winners took £62 10s. from
the club, seconds and thirds £l5 and £5. The
Flying was taken in two sections —winners £35.
The other events were worth £75 to the first arrival.
The spectacle of 150 horses pursuing a total prize
money of £BOO for 10 races (plus £l5O acceptance
fees) was not altogether a record for Sydney, but was
so near to being one as to again raise curiosity about
how much longer such proceedings are going to be
tolerated. The gag about such meetings as the
one held on Saturday being a necessary provision
for “the encouragement of the thoroughbred horse,”
or for “gratification of the sporting instinct prevalent
in the community” long ago became weevily. Horse-
racing on these lines is a mockery of sport. Tolera-
tion of it is equivalent to sanctioning a series of
leather-flapping gambles, through which men and
horses are diverted from useful work. There is
money in it, of course—for racecourse proprietors.
For owners and trainers there is the smell of an
oilrag. If the horses had to be nourished out of
the stakes offered by the proprietary clubs slings
would have to be provided to hold the bones up.
Meanwhile, the position of the Government calls
for harsh words. At this Canterbury Park meeting
£23,330 was handled by the totalisators. The
figures, a great increase on previous arithmetic
thereabouts, mean several things, but, at the moment,
the most important point about them is that the
Government rake-off amounted to £1630. This is
more than double the amount which the numerous
horses went in pursuit of, or pretended to. This
is bushranging with a vengeance. On a once-famous
occasion Premier Holman announced that the idea of
the State deriving revenues from “gambling” was
obnoxious to his soul. Wherefore he was opposed
to the totalisator. Holman is now, as Treasurer,
participating in a raid upon the totalisator which
for rapacity would do credit to a Ministry of Shy-
locks. The anomaly of a race programme of 10
events decorated with £BOO prize money and a Gov-
ernment revenue of £1630 from the proceedings
simply clamors for correction. If Holman and
others are unable to see that yn this matter they
are doing further injury to the only arguments by
which use of the totalisator can be justified, the
sooner they hire someone to impart some sense of
decency into their tote programme the better. And
if they cannot see that revenue derived like this
is smirched, then their dulness is incredible. In
this page’s judgment there is not even the sem-
blance of a ease to be made out for the proprietary
racing club, and from every point of view and in any
circumstances the 7 per cent. Government levy is an
outrage. Appropriated regardless of the stakes offered
on the racing menu —well, it was not for that anyone
ever asked for the totalisator!
Apropos of the Sydney proprietary alleged
racing outfits there is a scheme in the incubator by
which one will pass to the undertaker, its racing
dates being absorbed by the A.J.C. The matter has
some not unamusing incidental aspects. But these can
be passed. The main thing is that the passing-out
process having commenced, it should be encouraged
and the prevailing conditions cleared away. The
change had better be made a thorough one _as
quickly as possible, while the time is ripe for doing
it painlessly. Otherwise some of the alleged clubs
have a 0 to 4 chance of getting badly hurt.
Anyone backing a hurdler when there
isn’t anything to jump usually has reasonable cause
to suspect that the animal will omit to fall. But
not always. Swanker was made a 5 to 2 favorite
for the jumpers’ flat race at Canterbury Park on
Saturday. They started—and Swanker, discovering
there were no fences to tumble over, got so agitated
that he tumbled over his feet.
The once near-about Maoriland cham-
pion Bimeter had got so close on Saturday to recover-
ing his rapidity that he got third in a division of
the Flying at Canterbury Park (Sydney). Cerise
Colors and Hem were in front, but there were no
Biplanes behind. Bimeter started at sto 2. He
must have cost the discerning punter quite a lot
since he commenced being a certainty in Sydney.
Hitherto Bimeter’s main trouble was to get away at
the start. Latterly his difficulty seems to -be to stop
going astern at the finish.
“Trot”; Unless the talk of jockeys and
trainers and the evidence of one’s eyesight is all
wrong, the sooner the cinematograph as an assistant
stipe is recognised the better. Wherefore I beg to
suggest without demand for fee that a movie opera-
tor be located every 20 yards round the track from
the first turn to the last, at which point there should
be at least six on different angles. Then at the con-
clusion of a race, if anyone had any questions to ask,
the open book could be produced and the proceedings
examined in detail. Certainly questions are not asked
now frequently enough, though the stories told by
the boys afterwards are sometimes an antidote to
printable language.
* * * *
“Trot” : The description given by “Cleido”
(B. 10/10/’18) of the methods employed by jockeys
when wasting for a ride is fur too mild. Violent
purgatives, heavy continuous sweats and starvation
are all on the menu, and the way these remedies for
excessive weakness are at times applied i 3 so crude
as to be almost unbelievable. Scores of times I have
seen boys put aboard horses so weak as to make it
a physical impossibility for them to ride in any rational
sense. There are some lads so robust in physique
that even these dynamite methods fail. They make
weight so rapidly as to be more or less useless
except over hurdles, and that is a specialty which
few are suited to. There are others of lower natural
vitality who never recover from the treatment—
and all racing men know what become of them. As
a class jockeys are not a particularly well-informed
lot, to say the least. Considering their training, how
could they be expected to be, anyhow ? The problems
of nutrition and physiological change have no mean-
ing to them—nor to most of their bosses. Yet with
an intelligent diet the youth or man cast in the
right mould for riding would be able to easily do
ali and more than he now so often painfully struggles
to achieve and damages his constitution to per-
petrate. To the rider normally weighing from Bst.
to 9st. a 101 b. or 121 b. variation could be made a
simple and natural process, leaving him unimpaired
in strength and comprehension. But he must know
what to eat instead of what he likes —and must
stick to a menu all the time. If he knows his job
it is worth loading up the right instead of the wrong
stuff —and ever so much cheaper.
*• * * #
Norman Conrad, an Adelaide boy, wounded
at Gallipoli and France, now pursues excitement on
a motor-bike. The other day he won the mile handi-
cap at Sellick’s Beach (S.A.). in 42 4-ssee., which
is probably a world’s record for a touring machine
such as he rode. At the same sports Edgar Ferguson
lowered the one-mile passenger record (motor-
bicycle and sidecar) for Australia, time 55 2-ssec.
The previous best was perpetrated by 11. A. Parsons
(Victoria). Parsons and his passenger loitered 3sec.
longer on the journey.
* * * *
The ravages of the climate of Bananaland
seem to be having more disastrous effects on the
rising generation than ever. At the Military Col-
lege (Puntroon) annual sports the other day the
silver boomerang went to cadet-sergeant Dunbar, who
happens to be a product of the Cairns hinterland.
Dunbar could only manage to win the broad jump
(20ft. 6in.), the 440yds. (56sec.), the 120yds.
hurdles (17 2-ssee.), the high jump (sft. 9in.), and
arrive second in the 220yds. These proceedings are
a solemn warning against the awful weather.
“Dust-up,” on the same mournful subject:
Let me introduce another Bananalander, bred and
born—Sydney Curnow Ashby, just 23 years, and 12st.
His 1918 performances: Member of Valley Water Polo
Team, premiers for year, and most prolific goal-
thrower of season; member of Hamilton Cycling Road
Team, premiers of year; winner of the five-mile and
mile State cycling championships and several handi-
caps ; member of Valley Football Team. He scored
the two sensational tries of the final match which
won the League premiership. Previous performances
include winning track and road championships over
all distances—l 2 times of Bananaland. He represented
the State on track and road in Sydney, securing the
fastest unplaced time in the Speedwell 50-mile race
’l5, after spilling. Also he is holder of the unpaeed
grass-track amateur record of Australia (lmin.
5 l-ssec.), and of Bananaland’s half-mile hard-track
unpaced record (lmin. 2 2-ssec.). Has won run-
ning, swimming and jumping events in good company,
can use the gloves and rifle with the next, and can
play good cricket, bowling and batting left hand
like dad John Ashby, an old international cricketer
and Brisbane toeballer. This climate is simply
ruining the stamina of everyone over 97.
* * * *
One V. S. Murphy, had a busy day at the
first Westralian ’Varsity championship meeting the
other day. Out of the nine events on the pro-
gramme he won the 440yds., 880yds., 120yds. hurdles
and broad jump, and was second in the 100yds.,
throwing the cricket ball and the high jump. Of
56 points scored by Arts in the inter-faculty com-
petition Murphy accumulated 30. There seemed to
be nothing happening but Murphy all the afternoon.
* * * *
Sydney Grammar’s 561 for eight wickets
against Scot s College in G. P. Schools’ cricket last
week-end was a rapid performance. N. S. Langdon,
who caused most unpleasantness by making 121 with
the bat and helping himself to four wickets for 25,
has been a busy lad in school athletics. At the
G.P.S. championships this year he dead-heated for
the 100yds., and was second in the 220yds. and
440yds. ; and at Sydney Grammar championship
meeting he won at 100yds. and 220yds., and was
second in the 440yds. Sydney Grammar's win by
465, however, is only a good second to the debacle a
few years ago, when, strangely enough, the same
school walloped the Scots by 774. Even S.G.S.’s
tally of 848 on that occasion pales before Melbourne
C. of E. Grammar’s record of 961 against Geelong.
* * * *
F. G. Eager’s motor-car jaunt last: week
from Sydney to Brisbane in 19 hours 28}min. can
be written down as the greatest whiz of its kind in
Australia. Boyd Edkin’s record —which, by the way,
was put up from Brisbane to Sydney—had 6 hours
24fmin. chipped off it by the 40-h.p. Overland that
went through without a hitch. Compared with the
715 miles in 27 hours lOmin. perpetrated by the Bris-
bane express, the hurriedness of the ear is a moving
circumstance.
“Yarrum”: Dropped into Sydney Hippo-
drome the other night, and saw’ a go between a
couple of pigmies. They did love each other! Except
that it didn’t last the distance the go reminded me
of a fight I saw at Araluen (N.S.W.), when
Yankee Joe and a man from the Ovens went 94
rounds with the bare knuckles, and, assisted by their
pickers-up, consumed seven bottles of rum during the
six hours the disturbance lasted. Next morning, early,
the combatants were at work together, lip to their
waists in a race, just as if there had been no quarrel
and less ruin.
Chess is not wliat might be called an
excessively rapid game. Sometimes you score, but
usually you sit quite still thinking with great in-
tensity about what would happen in about 24 hours
if you did something different in 75min. to what you
cogitated over doing three hours previously. Nor
can chess be very aptly described as a sporting
game, because taking a chance with the Rook or
something means blue ruin. Moreover, no one was
ever detected going to play chess in clamorous
raiment or a motor-car behind a cigar. Chess is a
vice, not a sport. Its victims approach the pro-
ceedings with corrugated eyebrows and an intense
yearning to .commit homicide if anyone attempts to
start a conversation. The time between drinks is
long enough to give W. Hughes, Esq., a chance to
make 217 speeches. With which few remarks it is
hereby mentioned very much out of place, that the
conflict for the Westralian chess championship ended
in a dead-heat. Jim Sayers and J. Hilton fought out
a gruelling finish and emerged with an equal number
of wins. As Hiltcn couldn’t spare a month or so to
run off, they let it go at that. In 12 years Hilton
had triumphed on occasions and Sayers had all
the scalps on four.
“G.A.”: What is the distance limit for
the homer ? This flight (reported by the secretary
of the Flinders Homing Club, Port Augusta, S.A.) is
claimed to be an Australian record. A pigeon, a
hen eight months old, flew from Zanthus on the East-
West railway to its home at Port Augusta, airline
distance 827 miles, in 264 hours (taking flight-time
as from 6 a.m. to 7 p.m. each day). Australian-bred
pigeons have a reputation for stamina, and this seems
to prove it.
“John Quill”: The first prosecution in a
long, long time for illicit fishing in the Macquarie
River (N.S.W.) occurred at Rockley recently, when
a fine was imposed on the offender. The Fisheries
Department falls far short of its duty in adequately
protecting the fastly-diminishing cod. In reply to
the Wellington amateur fisherman’s outcry against
the use of nets and cross-lines in “prohibited areas”
recently, the Department said that it was unable to
appoint an inspector, and hoped that interested citi-
zens would help the police in such matters. This is
no good, because most people don’t like playing the
rdle of informer. Some time ago I stated in The
Bulletin that, unless the Department became more
active, a decade or two hence the renowned cod
would be almost extinct in the north-western streams.
My prediction appears to be coming true, for certain
people at Bathurst have offered the Department to
finance an attempt to stock the Bathurst waters of
the Macquarie with Murray cod. Twenty years ago
the sending of cod to the Macquarie would have been
regarded- as being quite as ridiculous as sending coal
to Newcastle. The “prohibited areas” to-day gener-
ally represent about four miles of a river’s course
above and below a town. Outside these areas the
“professional” fishermen use all methods of capture
—from cross-lines to dynamite—-without interference.
Personally, I don’t see any reason why the entire
course of the rivers shouldn’t be prohibited areas.
The north-western Streams suffer most from the
unscrupulous ways of the professional; the Murray
and Murrumbidgee are larger' streams and more
capable of supporting the industry.
SPRING SONG OF AN OPTIMIST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
My creditors are clamoring for payment;
Each day are papers sent me blue in shade;
Babette is discontented with her raiment,
My own is also lamentably frayed ;
But despite unthinking scoffers
I’ve a plan to fill my coffers.
For I know where there is money to be made.
Though wroth the worried rent-collector waxes,
I mean to pay the robber all I owe,
And promptly square my water-rates and taxes,
And clothe Babette anew from head to too;
For I’ve given some attention
To a scheme which, I may mention,
Cannot fail to bring me in a lump of “dough.”
No longer will I be a prey to sorrow ;
Next week on fizz and oysters will I sup
If but a paltry tenner I can borrow —
Won’t someone kindly put the money up?
I’ve no plan for interfering
With a bank, or profiteering—
But I've pot a nap in pickle for the Cup!
VICTORIA.

The anti-war and anti-Kaiser feeling that
has sprung up in Germany isn’t in any sense
a revolt against the horrors done by German
soldiery on the unarmed peoples that were at
their mercy. So long as the war went well
not a voice was raised in Hunland against
the worst of horrors, and the Hun “worker”
-—the private soldier —was just as brutal as
he knew how whether he had an officer —often
a "worker” like himself in peace time—by
his side to give orders or whether he had a
chance to be a detached brute. The rising
Hun is torn by two emotions —blind rage
against the rulers whose failure promises to
put an end to his enjoyment, and blind terror
lest he should fall into the hands of invaders
as inhuman as himself. He has no illusions
as to what sort of a time he will have in that
case.
The fiercest deputation that, had yet waited
on Minister Poynton came to complain that
he had described some of the men on the
Commonwealth shipbuilding job as “raw
hands.” What he said was that the work
done was really excellent especially as some
of the men were raw hands, which they cer-
tainly are-—raw to the building of steel
ships. The deputation declared that it tvas
insufficient to say that the men were angry;
“there Avas a storm.” An apology Avas de-
manded. Even the vanity of Caesars and
Kaisers and Czars isn’t ahead of the child-
ish and insane pomp of men who rage over
a compliment like that and feel that another
hideous jyrong has been done to “the worker.”
Yet the same crowd demands that the Hun
shouldn’t even be made to apologise or made
to pay a fine for rape, murder, torture, star-
vation and enslavement done on Belgian and
Serb workers. To ask such a thing would
humiliate the German people, including the
man who holds Poynton’s job in the Hun
country.

Legal Notice.
TN BANKRUPTCY. IN THE SUPREME COURT
L OF NEW ZEALAND. NORTHERN DISTRICT.
No. 70/18.
In the Matter of “The Bankruptcy Act 1908” and
in the Matter of FRANCIS ARTHUR RICH, formerly
of Waimana in the said District but now of Parts
unknown, Surveyor, a Debtor.
EX PARTE BARNARD CLARKSON SHEPHERD,
a Creditor.
To FRANCIS ARTHUR RICH, formerly of Waimana
in New Zealand, Surveyor.
WHEREAS Barnard Clarkson Shepherd formerly
of Whakatane and now of Northern Wairoa in New
Zealand Contractor has filed his pet-ition in bank-
ruptcy wherein he alleges that you are indebted to
him in the sum of £35 2s. 9d. and that with intent
to defeat and delay your creditors you departed from
New Zealand about the month of October 1915 and
at present remain out of New Zealand with the like
intent being unable to pay your debts in New Zealand
out of your own moneys and whereas by an order
of this Honorable Court made on the 18th day of
October 1918 it was ordered that service of the said
petition and of the summons issued herein be served
upon you by publishing an advertisement notice
once in Tiie Bulletin newspaper published at Syd-
ney. Therefore take notice that unless you the said
debtor attend at the Supreme Court at Auckland in
New Zealand on Wednesday the 18th day of Decem-
ber 1918 at 10.30 a.m. or so soon thereafter as Coun-
sel can be heard the matter will be heard and deter-
mined in your absence.
A copy of the said Petition and Summons will be
supplied to you the said debtor upon your applying
for the same at the office of this Court at Auckland.
Dated at Auckland this 19th day of October 1918.
O. E. BOWLING, Deputy Registrar.
Norman Potts of Opotiki is Solicitor for the Peti-
tioner whose address for service is at the office of
Messieurs Bamford and Brown, New Zealand Insur-
ance Buildings Queen Street Auckland New Zealand.
Note. —Should any person know the address of the
Debtor he is requested to send this notice to him.

Nervous Indigestion
Many Australian people suffer from ner-
vous indigestion. Worry, a deep grief, over-
work, lack of exercise or a general run-down
condition of the system will cause it. Among
school-children overstudy and confinement in
badly-ventilated rooms will bring it on.. It
is a disease of the nerves rather than of the
digestive organs, and is corrected by giving
needed nourishment to the nervous system by
building up the blood.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are an ideal tonic
for nervous people because they are non-
alcoholic, and nervous patients should avoid
alcoholic stimulants.
The treatment consists largely in rest,
recreation and the use of the tonic, the dis-
continuance of any practice that may have
caused the trouble, and abstinence from tea,
coffee and stimulants. As the nerves receive
their nourishment through the blood the
latter must be built up and made rich and
pure. Especially if there is loss of weight
and pallor indicating a weakness of the blood,
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are best adapted.
Write for the free booklet, “Diseases of the
Nervous System,” to the Dr. Williams’ Medi-
cine Co., Box 594 G.P.0., Sydney. Your
own chemist or storekeeper sells the pills, or
they will be sent, post paid, at 2s. 9d. per box,
six boxes 15s. 3d. — xXdvt.

The conviction grows stronger in Europe
that the Hun fleet must tome out. The idea
oi: retaining it as an a*set to barter with at
the Peace Conference is fading, for ie
signs are that there will be no bartering,
and that the Hun won’t get even a gallerj
ticket for the Peace Conference. The visible
possibilities are six: (1) The fleet ma.N
as before and wait to be partly
to pieces and partly captured; (-) “ m ‘•
be destroyed so far as it is Possibie to de
stroy such a vast and solid affan, but pio
bably the most strenuous efforts would onij
amount to a depreciation of oU per cent.,
(3) it may be sunk, which is rather con
cealment than destruction, if there is enough
deep water available to allow of it
sunk past recovery; (4) it may be surren-
dered by the Government, which doesn t look
profitable; (5) it may be surrendered or
sold for a soug to the Allies by its own offi-
cers; (6) it may come out to- take the hun-
dredth chance of beating the Allied fleet and
wrecking the Allied plans at the 11th houi
and 59th minute. Possibly the Huns waited
so long because defeat seemed almost cer-
tain, and they didn’t want a gr«it sea calam-
ity to happen just when the army aud the
people had enough bad land news to struggle
with. And possibly they may have waited
too long till land calamity has taken the
fight out of the fleet. If there is an igno-
minious collapse in harbor the lost fleet will
be the jest of history—the second greatest
navy that the world ever saw breaking up
without a single big fight to its credit, lhc
battles at sea have been- the merest skir-
mishes considering' the size of the fleet that
might have skirmished. Even Paraguay and
Peru, in their brief career as naval powers,
made a more dignified finish. They did the
Richard Grenville 1 act.
J. M. Paul, Bathurst native, who died
at Orange last week, was a brother of .Lieut.-
Colonel Paul, of Soudan fame (father of
Mick Paul, the artist) and of “Bill” Paul,
who represented Bathurst in the N. Is. Wales
Assembly, 1889-91. At the next election he
was pushed out by F. B. Stittor. He was
86 when the light failed.
“Vic”: I think General Pau and the
French Mission must have declared that the
Public Library is the greatest feature of
Melbourne. They had a quiet look around
it, without the disturbance of a crowd.
(Metaphorically they went do fyn on then-
knees and thanked Providence for the inter-
val.) I doubt that any library m.U.b. beats
Melbourne for French literature. Napoleon
the Third gave the Victorian Library a com-
plete reprint, in many volumes, of The
Moniteub newspaper, as published through-
out the yearjs ITB9-95.
“Melb”: The great Catholic conclave of
all the Australian prelates in Melbourne had
for its main business a discussion- on the
Eucharistic Congress of the World, to be
held in Sydney as soon as possible after the
war. It was to have been held in 1915- A
cardinal from Riome will preside, and Car-
dinal O’Connell from the United. States is
also likely to attend. It is certain that an
Australian Cardinal will be created. My. tip
is between Cattaneo and Mannix. Certainly
not both.
“Oldfield” : In the sane discussion that has
lately been given to venereal diseases I notice
no mention of a very simple manner of con-
tracting them. In the surfing season num-
bers of persons hire costumes on the various
beaches, little thinking of the serious risk
they run. If a surf costume be infected by
v.d. germs, one 1 has practically no change of
escape. At busy times the hirers-out have no
time to disinfect these dresses even if in-
clined that way. which is very doubtful.
AVliat about the Health Department taking a
hand?
“Solomon Matfaprop” looks in: —
As a matter of o ourse General Pau and the other
French visitors were taken to see Daceyville, Sydney’s
“garden suburb.” Everybody of importance or
otherwise is taken there. Recently the S. M. Herald
complained because a conference of bush mayors,
etc., visiting Sydney at the public cost, was allowed
to call at Daceyville without a Minister —a full-
blown Gladstone or Walpole or Disraeli—to take it
round, and the Abralnam Lincoln in charge of the
Three-roomed Qbttage Department wrote indignantly
disclaiming ai(y intentional disrespect towards the
bush aldermen. Some day, when the madness that
is impending comes upon me, I will burn Daceyville
simply because I am ifired of hearing about it. The
blessed place was started with 336 J acres of public
land, which gave it a cheap beginning. It was built
with loan money, awl the p'.n. having 1,860,000
signatures on the- back the money was got more
cheaply than a privateparson or municipality could
hope for. Being State property it escapes some
imposts which fall upon private property. The half-
square-mile place was calculated to hold 1437 cot-
tages, varying from three rooms to four rooms and
a kitchen. If they weie crowded on the same scale
as the average three- or foiir- or five-room suburb
the place would still hold fyr less people than the
said suburb, because the folk don’t live on top of
each other. If Balmain, Annandale, Darlington,
Glebe, Erskineville, Newtown, Paddington and Red-
fern were reconstructed on the same lines they would
spread as far as Granville or Liverpool, and the
inhabitants would be ruined in time and railway-
fares getting to and from theiir jobs. Daceyville is
a solemn solitary confidence trick. It shows how a
handful of people with small means can be Roused
without overcrowding within five miles of a city (and
even that is a long way) provided the fac-
tories, workshops, wharves, railway station, hospital,
police-station, gaol (possibly), lunatic asylum (possi-
bly), municipal and private offices, wholesale ware-
houses, travellers’ accommodation, etc., that ordinary
places have to make room for are kept out of the
garden or show suburb and left to overcrowd the
plain suburb next door. If the wonderful place was
made 10 times the size, so as to make the lesson
more impressive, the scheme would burst ; it would
be an attempt to make a plane about nine times the
size of Redfern with all the unpleasant but necessary
realities of toil and real life shoved over the edge
into Paddington, which has troubles enough of its
own. Daceyville pays a -modest dividend with cheap
land to start upon; with all the State to back its
municipal p.n.; with few repsiirs to face, for every-
thing is new; with no old hot se to replace ; and with
its balance-sheet drawn up by friendly people who
don’t need to prove its alleged solvency by not in-
creasing the rates. As an essay on the hovsing pro-
blem, it is a fraud, and lam tired of it. I wonder
if it is combustible.

caricature of himself. He has added a few words, giving his
experience of the use of DR. SHELDON’S DIGESTIVE
TABULES, which we feel sure will carry great weight with
all our readers, coming- from this eminent artist.
“My profession demands such alert attention and
close observation that I have freouently, in following a

over meals I have naturally suffered from severe twinges

me some of your Digestive Tabules, and from that time
I have always carried a few in my pocket; and, as anti-
dotes to the effects of worry, I have never known any-
thing like them.” —Yours faithfully,

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE came from tumbled country past the
humps of Buffalo,
Where the snow sits on the mountains
and the Summer aches below. _ > ;
He’d a silly name like Archie. Squattin
sullen on the ship
He knew next to holy nothin’ through a gor-
forsaken trip.
No talk he had qf women, no refreshing
thoughts of beer;
If he’d battled, loved or suffered, vital facts
did not appear;
But the parsons and the poets couldn t teach
him to discourse,
When it come to pushiu’ guyver at a pore,
deluded ’orse.
If a nag got sour and kicked agin the rules
of things at sea,
He’d tickle him, and flatter him, and kid
him up a tree.
“Here’s a pony got highsteries —pipe the
word for Privit Rowe,”
The sergeant yapped, and all the brood came
cluckin’ to the show.
He’d chat him confidential, and he’d pet and
paw the moke;
He’d tickle him, and flatter him, and lit him
with a joke;
And presently that neddy sobers up, and sez:
“Of course,
If you put it that way, cully, I will be a
better ’orse !”
There was one partic’lar skewbal’ known
aboard as Marshal Neigh
Whose monkey tricks Avith Privit Rowe was
better than a play.
He’d done stunts in someone’s circus, and
he loved a merry bout,
Whirlin’ in to bust her boiler, or to kick the
bottom out.
Rowe he sez: “ ’Struth, theres an idjit!
Oh, yes, let her whiz, you beauty.
Where’s yer ’orse sense, little feller? Where’s
yer bloomin’ sense of duty?
Well, you orter serve your country !” Then
there’d come a painful hush,
And the nag would droop his mapper, and, so
’elp me cat, he’d blush !
We was heaped ashore by Suez, rifle, horse,
and man and tent,
Where the land is sand, and water, and a
blazin’ firmament.
We had intervals of longin’, we had sweaty
spells of work
In the ashpit of Gehenner, dumbly waitin’
for the Turk.
We goes driftin’ on the desert, nothin’ doin’,
nothin’ said,
Till we get to think we’re nowhere, and half
fancy we are dead ;
And the only human interest in the red
horizon’s rim
Is Marshal Nfeigh’s queer fancy for the lad
that straddles him.
When plain livin’s bored us stiff, the Major
calls on Artie Rowe
To devise an entertainment. What his
skewbal’ doesn’t know
Isn’t in the regulations. Him and Rowe is
brothers met,
And that ’orse’s sense of humor is the oddest
fancy yet.
But the Turk arrives one mornin’ on the
outer edge of space;
From back of things his guns is floppin’
kegs about the place,
And Privit Artie Rowe, along with others of
the force,
Goes pig-rootin’ into battle, holdin’ converse
Avith his ’orse.
Little Abdul’s quite a fighter, and he mixes it
with skill;
But the Anzacs have him snouted, and, oh,
ma, he’s feelin’ ill!
They wake the all-fired desert, and the land
for ever dead
Is alive and fairly crawlin’, and the skies
are droppin’ lead.
But they’ve got the Ot’man goin’, an’ the
gaudy hunts begin—
It’s for us to cliiv his Trousers, and to
round the stragglers in.
Cuttin’ closest to the raw. and swearin’
blithely all the way,
Is Artie from Moolinga on his charger,
Marshal Neigh.
We’re pursuin’ sundry camels, turkey-trot-
tin’ anyhow,
With the carriage of an emu and the actions
of a cow,
When a sand dune busts and belches arf
a million of the foe.
They uncork a blanky batt’ry, and it’s
Allah, let ’er go!
We're not stayin’ dinner, thank you. Lie
along yer ’orse and yell.
While the bullets pip yer britches, and you
sniff the flues of Hell.
Here it is that Artie takes it good and solid
in the crust,
And he dives from out the saddle, and is
swallered in the dust.
I got through and saw them pointin’ where
the skeAvbal’ faced the band.
He Avas goin’ where we came from, sniffin’
bodies in the sand,
Till he found Rowe snugglin’ under, took
him where his pants was slack.
And by all the Asiatic gods he brought his
soldier back !
With a bullet in his buttock and a drill ole
in his ear,
He brought Artie in among us. Square and
all, hoAV did we cheer!
There’s no medals struck for ’orses, but we
ruled there orter be, . .
And the pride of all the Light ’Orse it is
Marshal Neigh, V.G.
VICTORIA.
EDWARD DYSON.
Judas Iscariot.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MR. JOSEPH HOLT, the celebrated
Irishman —and justly celebrated —
reined in his horse on the slope of
Castle Hill, and turned in the saddle to look
back at Parramatta. , „ . f
It was a beautiful day, m the Spring of
1807, and the lovely valley m which the
second town of Australasia nestled on the
banks of its winding river was very fair to
look unon. The gleam ot water show-
ing between green trees, the white cottages
with their dark roofs of shingle and ot
thatch, the bluish foliage of the tall eucalyp-
tus and the golden glory of the new wattle,
whose sweetness filled all the atmosphere—
it was a view that always delighted the soul
of Mr. Holt. And this afternoon it soothed
it a little, when it was in need of something
soothing. , ' . . ..
“Well, then,” he muttered to himself,
“’tis too elegant a prospect for them that’s
in it, so it is. I’ll wager me boots ’tis lost
upon Billy Bligh, now.”
Mr. Holt at this time was a sturdily-built
man of middle-age, strong and active-looking,
with a sunburnt face encircled by a fringe
of reddish chin-beard. Great dignity, a
touch of truculence, something of a whole-
some self-appreciation, and unmistakable
benevolence were its characteristics. He held
himself with a martial uprightness and
air of command that fitted his own concep-
tion of what he was above all else—a sol-
dier, a strategist and a tactician. Nothing
was more agreeable to him than to be ad-
dressed as “General.” In no capacity did he
consider himself more eminent than as a
leader of men. It was true that the United
Irish were only a memory of failure —though
a present nightmare to those who had the
governance of such of them as were banished
to New South Wales. It was true that his
career as a general had been short and un-
successful, though not unsweetened by the
joys of transient victory. It was true that
he was an exile in a distant and disorderly
land where most men were either tyrants,
slaves or go-betweens. And it was true that
he was a very upright man who gloried in
being an Irishman, a Gentleman and a Pro-
testant —with capital letters.
Mr. Holt was on his way to visit his late
employer and kindly friend, Mr. William
Cox, of Clarendon, near Richmond. (One
would give much to see his face and hear
his comments at the sight of the constant
flight of aeroplanes that take place over the
roof of the old farmhouse to-day.) He had
been into Parramatta to call upon his Ex-
cellency Governor Bligh, deeming it politic
in one who had been so unjustly suspect
during the regime of his predecessor, Cap-
tain King, to take frequent opportunity of
demonstrating his loyalty to the existing Gov-
ernment. Unfortunately, he had struck the
choleric post-captain upon one of his bad
days, when nothing could sweeten the feroci-
ous ill-temper of that irascible vice-regent.
“And what do I care, Mr. Holt, for your
devotion to law and order? Damme, sir—•
what do I know of ye?
for being a rebel, wasn’t ye? Ye was i
sent to Norfolk Island three year ago for
being suspect of still being a rebel,
wasn’t ye? How do I know ye’re not a
With a hauteur that carried no weight
with the Governor, Mr. Holt had protested
his good behaviour and his good intentions.
“Your Excellency,” he said, with great
impressiveness, “I may have been unfortu-
nate, but I have not become deceitful. My
honor, sir, is untarnished. When I assure
your Excellency of my devotion, your Excel-
lency may rest assured that I am devoted.
On the word of a soldier, sir”
“A soldier, by God!” roared Bligh. “Don’t
talk to me of soldiers. The scum of creation,
sir! What is our motto. ‘A sailor before
a marine, a marine before a dog. a dog
before a soldier.’ And what sort of soldier
was ye, pray? Why, a d —-d, bog-trotting,
hedge-soldier in Ireland. A croppy,
sir. ’Twas only by luck ye escaped hang-
ing”—
“Sir!” began the outraged Mr. Holt.
“Silence, sir! Look ye—get back to your
pig-farm, and stay there. I don’t wish to
see ye. And have a care, sir, have a care.
I am not to be hoodwinked, sir. Damme,
no! Mind your ways, sir; mind your
ways!”
With bitterness in his heart Mr. Holt
had repaired to James Larra’s inn, and
there discussed a bottle of wine and his
grievances with his friend Major Abbott,
who had done his best to pour oil upon the
troubled waters of the Irishman’s mind.
But his anger still rankled as he rode away
from Parramatta in the early afternoon.
He shook his fist at the little town in
the valley. “Bad luck to ye, Billy Bligh.
Go to Hell y’r own bad-minded way. ’Tis
an enemy y’d make of an angel from Heaven
sent for to_ help ye! An’ ’tis friends ye
need,” he murmured, “ ’tis friends, indeed,
ye’re needin’ this day. An’ ’tis meself was
willin’ for to help ye.”
From amongst the trees by the roadside
a man stepped quietly into the highway.
Mr. Holt turned his head and looked at
him.
“Jonas Absalom!” he cried in astonish-
ment. “I thought ’twas at th’ Coal River
ye were?”
“No, General —not this three weeks.
They’ve let me back —on good behaviour.”
“Then I hope, me man, that ye’ll conduct
y’self well.”
The other laughed. He was a tall, sal-
low fellow with one bright eye. Over the
other he wore a black patch. His features
were sharp, with the suggestion of a ferret’s
in them, and his lean body was clad in a
faded black tail-coat and worn and shiny
pantaloons that were too tight and too
short. He looked like a seedy undertaker’s
assistant.
“I mean to, General. ’Tis just at the
present moment I’m planning how to make
myself good with them. Oh, I’ve an excel-
lent plan”—he rubbed his bony hands
together and grinned—“an excellent plan,
General!”
Mr. Holt looked at him with disfavor.
“And are ye back in the same job, then?”
he asked.
