“Yes —I’m still schoolmaster at Parra-
matta. Some influence Avas brought to
bear, and they alloAved me to re-open my
school. I have resumed tuition,” he said
pompously, “in the Arts and Sciences and
the use of the Globes. DraAving lessons
extra, and elegant deportment—that is extra
too.”
"An’ Avhat’s your plan, Jonas Absalom —
Avliat schame do ye have to get favor Avith
them high up?”
The man came close up to the off-side of
Mr. Holt’s horse and laid his hand upon its
wither. The horse snorted, and shied away,
as though he did not like the looks of the
unpleasant person avlio took such liberties.
The unpleasant person Avas also roundly
cursed by Mr. Holt.
“D—n y’r fool’s soul. Jonas Absalom—
have ye no sinse? To come up on the off
side like that! Stand y’r distance, me man.
I’m not likin’ ye any closer than Rory does.
Well, what devil’s work are ye plottin’ an’
plannin’ now?”
“You will joke, General! ’Tis no devil’s
Avork at all, as you call it. ’Tis the work
of an honest citizen, of one anxious for the
Avelfare and safety of the community, and
ready to put himself to some inconvenience
to ensure such safety and welfare.”
“As ye did in Dublin, Jonas Absalom?”
“Not quite, General—but something like
it. You allude, no doubt, to my eAddence
in the Turnbull case?”
“1 do. I mane when ye saved y’r own
dirty skin be turnin’ King’s evidence, 4n’
bangin’ y’r brother-in-law. That’s what I
mane. Is it the like of that?”
“I was young then, General, and life was
sweet. ’Twas not necessary that both of
us should suffer.”
“Faith, no. xVn’ ye took good care ’tAvould
not be y’self that suffered.”
Mr. Absalom shrugged his shoulders. He
peered up at Mr. Holt’s sarcastic counte-
nance with a little smile.
“Ah, w T ell —what was, Avas, General. ’Tis
ten year ago—leave it rest. I’m minded
to tell ye now what’s in me mind. ’Twill
benefit you as Avell as myself. You are
not in good odor with the authorities.”
“How do ye knoAv that—an’ what’s it to
ye. annyway?”
“I heard every word the Governor said to
you this morning. I was waiting to speak to
him. I, could not but hear. He bellows
like a bull when his temper’s ruffled. I
Avalked out here to meet you on your way
to Richmond Hill. I’ve a proposal to make.”
Mr. Holt looked at him doubtfully. He
knew Jonas Absalom —Irish .JeAV, informer,
renegrade and confidence-man of his day.
But he kneAV him to be a clever rogue, and
perhaps he might have some suggestion that
would help him to a better understanding
with those who distrusted him. He had his
Avife to think of. So he prepared to listen
to him-
“ Speak up!” he said. “What is it?”
“’Tis this. You’re not loved by Govern-
ment, General. No more am I. They dis-
trust all the Irish —because they fear them.
They distrust you because, if you Avished it,
you might be the chosen leader of all the
lads from the old sod that’s in the colony.
They know that. I know, and the rest of
us knoAv, how you’d have nought to do Avith
the uprising in eighteen-four. But Govern-
ment never believed that. They’d not have
believed it if Jehovah himself had vouched
for you. But they could prove nothing. So
they sent you to Norfolk Island for a year
and a half. And a proper time you must
have had there!”
“Well, Avell,” broke in Mr. Holt impati-
ently. “Come to th’ point, can’t'ye !”
“ ’Tis that I’m coming to. You wish to live
in peace, I take it? Quiet, unmolested?”
“I do. ’Tis all I want, then/’
“Well, there’s but one way for you—and
’tis the same for me. We must prove to them
that we’re on their side. We must show ’em
Ave’re to be trusted. Only then Avill they
leave us alone.”
“And hoAv’s that to be done, Jonas
Absalom ?”
The JeAV lowered his voice, and came a
step nearer to Mr. Holt. His unprepossess-
ing features worked with suppressed excite-
ment, and he glanced swiftly dowm the empty
load and into the depths of the forest, as
though he feared to be overheard or over-
looked. With a little huskiness in his voice
he went on.
“Listen to me, General Joseph Holt. ’Tis
this way—’tis so Ave must do. We must
organise a conspiracy.”
“What! I’ll have nought to do”
“Patience—have patience, my dear Gen-
eral. Listen to me.”
Mr. Holt frowned at him. So intent Avas
Jonas Absalom upon his explanation that he
did not note the frown, nor the fierce clench-
ing of liis riding-Avhip in his right hand, as
Mr. Holt gently urged his steed a little way
towards him, so that the JeAV was within his
reach.
“Yes. We must delude these fools of crop-
pies with a plan of rebellion, and at the
proper time must come foi'Avard and give the
plot away to Government. That’s what we
must do. I’ll undertake” -
But he did not finish. With a roar of fury,
that wms at first an inarticulate bellow and
then developed into a torrent of oaths, Mr.
Holt slashed him across the face AAdth his
whip. /
“By God, ye scum—by God! Ye dirty
spaAvn of Hell! Ye d—d informer ! Traitor !
Dog! May God strike ye dead where ye
stand! Out o’ me way, ye foul carrion—out
o’ me way!”
He beat Mr. xVbsalom —cowering and try-
ing to shield his head with his bent arms—
mercilessly.
“Take that, Jonas Absalom !” he screamed.
“Oh, ye d—d villyan, ye! Ye’d betray Christ
himself. Judas Iscariot!” he hissed, a« he
spurred his horse over the sobbing Jew, and
galloped up the road.
Peter McGill. Thomas O’Shaughnessy and
young Patsy Riordan stood in dire peril of
their dives. Heavily ironed, they aAvaited,
outside the courthouse at the Green Hills—
as Windsor, N. 8. Wales, Avas then called—
the arrival of the magistrate Avho would
commit them for trial for High Treason-
Peter was an old man of 70, infirm with
rheumatism. Tom O’Shaughnessy was blind.
Young Patsy Riordan was half-witted. Never-
theless, they Avere about to be indicted with
the atrocious crime of haA’ing conspired to-
gether and with divers other ill-conditioned
persons—to wage war against their sovereign
lord King George the Third, his throne and
person, and Mr. James Absalom, the school-
master at Parramatta, was going to expose
the villainous plot which they had fabricated
against the peace and good government of the
realm. As surely as the sun would set to-
night, Sam Marsden would commit them. xVs
certainly as it would rise to-morrow, the Judge-
Advocate’s Court would find them guilty. And
as undoubtedly as the fact that after sunset
came the black night, would one of them
swing in each of the three settlements —-at
the Hawkesbury, at Parramatta and beside
Port Jackson. For the very worst charge
against them, which was not in the indict-
ment, would assuredly accomplish that. They
were Irish.
Burly Sam Marsden rode up to the court-
house. Dismounting, and handing his bridle-
reins to an obsequious bystander, he went
inside. X scowl passed across his red face
as he glanced at the prisoners. They were
hustled into the little building by the con-
stables, and the motley groups of onlookers
crowded in after them. The last couple were
startled by the sound of galloping hooves, and
turned to see Mr. Joseph Holt, in a state of
some excitement, and Major Abbott arrive
in front of the courthouse, Avith all the
evidence of hard riding in the aspect of
themselves and their steeds.
The little room was crowded almost to its
capacity when the newcomers forced their
Avay in and approached the table at its fur-
ther end, behind which sat the Rev. Samuel
Marsden, Senior Chaplain and Magistrate
of the Territory, drumming his fingers im-
patiently, as he aAvaited the commencement
of proceedings.
“Hullo, Major!” he exclaimed heartily, as
he rose and extended his hand, “xVnd Mr.
I-Iolt! Are ye interested in the doings of
these scoundrels?”
“Scoundrels!” snapped Mr. Holt. “Say
rather, your Reverence, poor innocent vic-
tims of a d—d scoundrel!”
“Tut, tut,” grunted the chaplain, “if they
be innocent ’twill, no doubt, be easy for
them to prove it. I only knoAv that they
stand charged with treason and sedition. I
must call the case.”
“Mr. Marsden,” said the Major, taking his
seat beside him —as Avas his right as a mem-
ber of the bench of magistrates, while Mr.
Holt frowned before the table —“Mr. Mars-
den, I beg you to give consideration to what
Mr. Holt will have to say. There is no doubt
in my mind that this fellow Absalom has
plotted the destruction of these poor men for
his oAvn ends. He seeks to curry favor with
Government. ’Tis as Avieked a business as
I’ve come across.”
“’Tis so, indeed, y’r Honor!” said Mr.
I-lolt.
“Well, Avell,” grumbled the parson doubt-
fully. “We must hear what he has to say.
I’ll give ye an opportunity to speak, Mr.
Holt. But I must say that I have little
favor for your turbulent countrymen. They
make endless trouble in the community.”
“God’s mercy, sir—look at them ! Turbu-
lent! A cripple, a blind man and an ijit!”
Mr. Holt waved his arm at the unhappy
group in chains. McGill, bent nearly double,
seemed to be apathetic. O’Shaughnessy, his
empty and unsightly sockets turned towards
the rafters, was as much so. And the vacant
Patsy Riordan dribbled in his adolescent
beard and grinned foolishly.
“They don’t seem very harmful, certainly,”
assented the chaplain. “However, we must
proceed. Ye shall have your say, Mr. Holt.”
The clerk read the sonorous indictment,
with its majestic phrasing and its deep con-
cern for the safety of King George; and
Jonas xVbsalom was called.
Mr. xVbsalom told his very circumstantial
tale. He had, he said, witnesses and
documents in confirmation of it. It
was the usual story of a projected
uprising and the proclamation of a republic.
Very happily Mr. Absalom produced the
draft of a treaty with France, which pro-
posed the handing over of New Holland to
the Emperor Napoleon. Inferentially, he
claimed to have saved the colony for Britain.
And then arose Mr. Holt—wrathful, fiery,
eloquent and convincing. He told the story
of his encounter with Jonas xVbsalom, near
Parramatta, two months before. Striking a
dramatic attitude. Avith a hand alternately
pointed toAvards Heaven and Mr. Absalom,
he concluded with this magnificent perora-
tion —which he did not feel bound to credit
to its original author, Mr. John Philpot Cur-
ran, the great advocate of the Dublin Law
Courts, avlio had declaimed it of an informer
named O’Bryan. But Mr. Marsden did not
know that.
“HaA r e you any doubt,” he demanded passi-
onately, “that it is the object of Absalom to
take down the prisoner's for the reAvard that
follows? Have you not seen with what more
than instinctive keenness this bloodhound
has pursued his vict-ims? How he has kept
them in view from place to place until he
hunts them through the avenues of the court,
to where the unhappy men stand now, hope-
less of all succor but that which your verdict
shall afford? I have heard of assassination
by sword, by pistol and by dagger; but here
is a wretch who would dip the Evangelist in
blood —if he thinks he has not SAVorn his vic-
tims to death he is ready to swear without
mercy and without end. But oh ! do not, I
conjure you, suffer him to take an oath ; the
arm of the murderer should not pollute the
purity of the gospel. If he will swear, let it
be on the knife, the proper symbol of his pro-
fession. Mr. Marsden, reverend sir,” he con-
cluded, pointing at the trembling informer,
“that wretch’s name is not Jonas Absalom.
’Tis Judas Iscariot!” he thundered forth.
The prisoners were discharged.
***•'•*■
As he led his tired horse up Prospect Hill
that evening, Mr. Marsden, returning to
Parramatta, overtook the discomfited Mr.
Absalom and discomfited him still further.
“Come here, you !” he bellowed.
The unhappy creature approached, cring-
ing. Deftly the burly Yorkshireman—ex-
blacksmith’s striker, and muscular apostle—
seized him by the collar. With vigor and
deliberation he beat him soundly with bis
hunting-crop, and then flung him down by
the roadside.
“Ye dog!” he bellowed. “’Tis indeed
a Judas ye be! Come not into my sight
again, or I’ll fine ye worse!”
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
